THE RED PAGE.
Hexameters—More or Less.
It is sometimes complained that The Bu
letin does not print long poems. T
answer (in the old form) is easy : The Bu
letin rarely has space for long poems ; i
never receives long poems ; there are no 16
poems. Poe’s dictum was contradictec
(merely) by a while-ago contributor ; but i
reasonableness is patent.
The phrase, “a long poem,” is simply a f]
contradiction in terms. A poem deserves
title only inasmuch as it excites, by elevating t
soul. Ihe value of the poem is in the ratio of tl
elevating excitement. But all excitements ar
through a psychal necessity [we would prefer
say physical necessity], transient. That degree o
excitement which would entitle a poem to bes
called at all cannot be sustained throughout
composition of any great length. After the lap
ot half-an-hour, ab the very utmost, it flags—fai
a revulsion ensues—and then the poem is,
effect, and in fact, no longer such.
u Poe instances “ Paradise Lost”—which
“to be regarded as poetical only when
losing sight of that vital requisite in a’
works of Art, Unity, we view it merely as
series of minor poems.” And if the essenc
°f Poetry is its power to elevate the sou
this 'must follow-—until the human brair
becomes capable of working at the liighes
tension for, say, 48 hours at a stretch.
* * * *
You can add Shakespeare to the a posterior
argument. Many persons prefer his sonnet
to his plays: hardly anyone admires the
plays in their totality. What we value are
the short, interspersed, poetical passages : the
dramatic action is often incoherent, fre
quently ludicrous, hardly ever exciting
And nine persons in ten, asked for their
favourite poem, will quote you some brie:
lyric of half-a-dozen verses—all pregnanl
with intense emotion. You may weld lyric
passages into an epic or a drama, and add
the force of narrative or of character, but the
‘poetry remains always in the lyric passages.
Its effect may be intensified by their situation
—never created. The modern mind will
rarely endure narrative, or descriptive, or
didactic verse; and it follows from this
exordium that—supposing an elevation of
thought to be the thing sought—the modern
mind is artistically right. Poe presaged
what is now a general attitude. Most
of us are mental flesh-eaters, as well as
physical—wanting much stimulus in small
compass. When an ounce of lyric meat is
obtainable, why munch a yard of epic celery 1
* * * •
Nevertheless, bulk counts ; and The Bul-
letin is always on the look-out for an Aus-
tralian poet who shall combine epic scope
with lyric force. And, in the meantime,
some passages may be quoted from two sets
of alleged hexameters which have recently
found lodgment in this office. Perhaps it is
unnecessary to declare that the hexameter
will never be any good in English; but it
is not any good—as poetry. It has to be so
artful that it loses all its energy. Nearly
everybody who adds a little Greek and Latin
to a little knack of verse has tried the classi-
cal metres; and the results, from Philip
Sidney’s downwards, have always a foreign,
literary-exercisish flavor. Our best trans-
lators of Homer and Virgil have known or
felt this. Perhaps the very best English
translation of the HCneid, spirit and letter,
is that by Conington—in the metre of
Marmion ! And of Clough, and Kings-
ley, and Longfellow one can only say that
the effect is occasionally pleasant, and the
hexameters are generally dubious, and it
appears that with the same expenditure of
poetical force the writers might have achieved
a better result in blank verse or the heroic
couplet.
• * * *
Poe, who was really a most acute literary
critic, put his pen on the weak spots. Eng-
lish hexameters are not good, he said, because
English, as compared with Greek and Latin,
wants spondees and vowels. And he trod
hard on Longfellow’s dactylic feet, and un-
kindly showed what “respectable prose”
the Longfellownian hexameter made, thus:—
Also the church within was adorned ; for this
was the season | when the young, their parents’
hope and the loved ones of Heaven, | should, at
the feet of the altar, renew the vows of their
baptism. | Therefore, each nook and corner was
swept and cleaned ; and the dust was | blown
from the walls and ceiling, and from the oil-
painted benches.
Are these lines in the Longfellow of to-day
Bat four-fifths of “ Evangeline ” and “Miles
Standish ” convert just as prosaically. And
Calverley, a master of classical translation,
invites comparison of
Silenced but unconvinced, when the story was
ended, the blacksmith,
or,
Clasped each other’s hand, and interchanged
pledges of friendship
with the first line of the Iliad. “Silenc’d but”
is a dactyl, encdb a short syllable. “Interchang’d
pledges ” is a reproduction of menin aeide. Only
conceive the havoc that we should make in one of
Homer’s lines if we inserted here and there such
encumbrances as ncdb, ngdpl, between two of the
short vowels.
It seems that the English hexameter, as a
poetical vehicle, “ must go.” William Wat-
son, in his recent “Hymn to the Sea,”
alternates it with pentameter, and gets neat
academic effects—but no poetry. And the
critics allege that Ovid “makes mock of”
our pentameters also.
* * * *
This is an unpromising introduction to the
“Railroad Medley” sent to The Bulletin
from far-away Venice; but “Beta’s” hexa-
meters, if they may not get rid of the primal
curse of their being, are quite good enough
to blunt any secondary objurgations.
A RAILROAD MEDLEY.
Oub from the roar and the smoke with speed that
grows faster and faster,
Leaving behind you the shouts and guards and
porters and faces ; . . .
Oub by the signals and points ; and the strident
cry of the levers
Rings in the signalman’s box as the carriages
swing round the curving,
Hurl you back with a jolt, in fear of immediate
destruction.
Then comes a description of the ride through
suburbs—“ an ocean of brick ” —till the roofs
grow sparser, and the train reaches the open
country. Then—•
Onward through valley and wold, in a maddening
waltz with the landscape
Circling its arms round you in wilder and wilder
embraces.
Deep is the green of the fields and deeper the
green of the hedgerows
Towering to elmy heights, that are quick with the
voice of the song-bird.
Placid the stretch of the meads, that from green
are kindled to golden,
Down by the marge of the stream where the king-
cups glow by the water,
Reaching in sinuous bands as they follow the
bends of the river.
Up to their knees are the kine in the cool, deep
grass of the meadow,
Languidly whisking their tails and chewing the
cud sigh complacent ;
Or one moistens his nose in a pool that is circled
with sedges. . . .
And now, alas ! three-fourths of the ‘ ‘ med-
ley”—some good, all readable—is sacrificed
to inexorable Space—the modern fourth Fate,
most fatal. The train has left the valley, and
climbs the heights to the really poetical close—
Onward you speed through a world that is moon-
lit, mystical, magic;
Follow the edge of a lake where the mists lie,
never a ripple ;
Faintly and cold through the haze hang the
ghostly shadows of pine woods,
Vague on the further shore where the rocks sink
sheer to the water;
Forests are falling down from the crags that are
broken and buttressed,
Soaring in opaline blue with a fine white veil from
their summit
Blown like a streamer afar to its fringe that is
silver’d with moonlight;
Wizardry domes and spires with a world of pale
light behind them,
Magical cups of the hills where the clouds lie
folded in slumber.
Here at the side that you skirt rise the tall brown
sedge and the bulrush
Rigid, erect from the mere; on the motionless
surface reflections.
On to the end of the Lake where the waters con-
found with the marsh land,
Growing each moment more firm; and the aide
shake and the aspens
Till these thicken and bind and condense at la
to a forest,
Walnuts and oak trees blent, and the inoonligi
lies on the purlieus ;
Whitely they fly like wraiths : To the heart of th
shadowy woodland
Onward you speed through the gloom and th
light from the leaves flecked around you. . .
Ah ! what a rout is that in the mist by the marg
of the lakelet;
Just where the willows bend and whisper anc
bow to the water,
Bacchus gleams there white, and the ivy berrie
that bind him
Round by the loins hang loose and beat like bell
to his dancing;
Thyrsus in hand and the cloak of the tiger’s-skin
from his shoulders
Flung far back, and there glows the broad expans
of his bosom;
Thyrsus he flings .aloft, while the Maenads mad
den about him,
Cast bare arms to the breeze with their tresse
a-stream on the night wind,
But not a sound is heard from the ghostly dance
of the Bacchse
Threading athwart the gloom and the light ii
shadowy forest.
So sink to sleep again ; and at length in the gray
of the dawning
You will awake and look out and see the whole
land lapped in
Motionless mists that, like dreams, the sun wil
scatter at rising.
Bacchus and his rout we have seen before
but they always make a pleasant picture.
Not so pleasant, maybe, to Longfellow’s ad-
mirers will be some passages from a locally-
produced parody on “Evangeline,” by
“ Bogg c -,” who writes that
Longfellow’s hexameters rouse me to frenzy.
Longfellow wasn’t a poet, anyway; but a very
labored rhymester who appears to have, “con-
veyed ” gems from half-a-score of literatures to
adorn his own uninspired verse, where they lie (in
Sheridan’s simile) “like lumps of marl on a barren
moor, encumbering what it is not in their power
to fertilise.” But his one-leg;ged spondees, and
the abysmic commonplace of his sentiments, toge-
ther move me to perpetrate this—by the mile.
ANNA MARIA: A TALE OF HYSTERIA.
This is the forest primeval. The murmuring
pines and theAemlocks
Stand in precisely the fashion in which other
hemlocks and pines stand—
Stand with their roots in the ground and their
other ends adverse;
Stand like serried skittles, awaiting the ball of the
•pitcher ;
Stand like members of Parliament, seeking the
eye of the Speaker.
Loud from its rocky caverns, the deep-voiced
neighbouring ocean
Shrieks, and in accents disconsolate answers the
howl of the forest.
In the Hysterian land, on the shores of the Basin
of Minas,
Distant, secluded, still, the little village of Grand-
Pre
Lay in the fruitful valley, looked as if fallen
asleep there.
Never the bustle of steam, or the clearing-out
sales of the drapers
Troubled its peaceful repose, or the heads of its
slow-going people.
(Please note just at this point of my story serenely
the sun sank.)
“Boggs” unkindly lays stress on Long-
fellow’s stage-carpenter’s moon and sun—
rising and setting whenever a simile seems
necessary. Then he describes the Hysterian
hero and his daughter :—
Somewhat apart from the village, and nearer the
Basin of Minas
Lazarus Aaronson dwelt, the richest pawnbroker
in Grand-Pre—
He and his child, sweet Anna Maria, the pride of
the village.
He was no great shakes to look at, was avarioious
old Lazarus —- *-
Warped were his back and his morals : his nose
was warped also ;
But gay Anna Maria more than made up for the
failings
Which a too critical optic might have perceived in
her father.
The courtship of Anna Maria and John
Henry, son of Billy the Butcher, gets due
attention, and there is a fine picture of the
consternation caused by the edict of exile
“ Notice ! Notice 1 Notice ! ” (This was the hor-
rible way it read.)
“Any Hysterian found in Hysteria after 6th
proximo,
Will be, sans further warning, summoned accord-
ing to law.”
When old Lazarus heard it, he gave bub cne crv.
“ O my shekels ! ”
Then fell headlong to earth and died in warpish
convulsions.
Down sank the sun, and the gloaming came over
the village of Grand-Pre,
Up rose the sun next morning, quite in accordance
with habit ;
Still, it does no harm to state the fact and it starts
the day’s work fair.
The embarkation is ruthlessly carried out •
John Henry and Anna Maria get on differ-
ent steamers ; and spend the rest of their
lives in agonised search for each other.
Once they almost met, in boats on the Father of
Waters :
Anna Maria slept, and her craft was moored to a
mud-bank ;
But the useful sun, quite tired of rising and
setting,
Dawdled in bed five minutes —in which five
minutes John Henry,
Passed in his little skiff, unknowing his fowl was
handy.
True, some tremor of sound, skimming over the
wavelets,
Made him rest on his oars, the while he mur-
mured, reflecting :
“ That sounds like the snore of my own, my Anna
Maria? ”
But the whisper passed on, and so did John
Henry. At last, grown very gray and feeble,
he reaches an Indian camp, where the chief
and his white-skinned wife sit apart in their
wigwam—
Old was she and ill-favored, yet he prized her
above the others,
Simply because her hue was a change from their
blank sameness.
John Henry spies her—
Then with one terrible cry of “ My long-lost Anna
Maria 1 ”
Fell like a log at her feet, and was picked up dead
as a door-nail.
Down the moon sank; but the sun didn’t rise,
for it was not quite time to.
And these are all hexameters—more or less.

BEFORE YOU
T. E A F»!
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that' your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A ROSY PHILOSOPHY.
Here’s a rule for all occasions—
Have it out!
Shun postponements and evasions—
Have it out.
Whate ’er your age or sex is,
Every problem that perplexes,
Every ill that bores or vexes—
Have it out.
Have you griefs and feel like crying ?
Have it out.
Raging, laughing, sobbing, sighing—
Have it out.
Are you e’er inclined for swearing,
For desponding or despairing?
Let your passion get an airing—•
Have it out.
Is your pet corn burning, aching ?
Have it out.
Is a tooth your slumber breaking ?
Have it out.
Do you seek fresh care to borrow,
Brooding o’er some dark to : morrow?
Holds your heart a rooted sorrow ?
Have it out.
If your mistress acts unkindly,
Have it out.
Worship not the blind god blindly ;
Have it out.
Though the false one fair may be
Other girls are fair as she ;
There are good fish in the sea—
Have ’em out!
That osseous depos.it—
Have it out!
That skeleton in closet —
Have it out!
As you sail the ocean frisky, ■
With your “ innards ” feeling risky,
Even though it should be whisky—
Have it out !
Has your debtor turned elusive ?
Have it out!
Is your creditor abusive ?
Have it out!
In a quarrel with your Maker,
Your mahatma or your fakir,
Your butcher or your baker—
Have it out !
Have you differed with your neighbor ?
Have it out.
Does your “ mountain ” heave with labor?
Have it out.
Is your “ mouse ” a tender story,
Or a poem grim and gory ?
For ridicule or glory—
Have it out!
Does remorse or terror haunt you ?
Have it out.
Let no secret rise to daunt you.
Have it out.
Concealment’s too much trouble;
A lie bursts like a bubble ;
Truth is cheap, and wears just double :
Have it out!
In short, on all occasions
Have it out ;
Doubts, fears, wiles or provocations,
Have it out.
Live your life, full, up-to-date ;
Love your love and hate your hate ;
Never dally till “ too late ”
Have it out !
Christchurch, M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
THE CURLEW CRY.
O sweet pale night, thine own soft moon is flinging
Rays o’er the trees, where not a bird is singing—
(No nightingales are in this land, and pied
Songsters are sleeping till the morning tide.)
But stay —a plaintive note comes softly shrill
From out the .shadows of that facing hill.
It rises front the first low tone’s appeal
And wild entreaty fills the next (unseal
Your heart of pity, listener, for this
Sad soul in bondage !) till the long leaves kiss
Each other in the wondering that follows
That sound that cleaves them through with pain.
The hollows
Then breathe that note’s death in the first again
Repeated in the fading of the call.
What is this, seems to chill with grief, that all
Our blood stands sudden still in its warm rush,
And only forward moves when that the hush
That follows the lone cry is most complete.
(And yet that wail had something of the sweet
Hid in its plaintiveness, if well you note.)
Is there a gate of Hades hidden here,
Where through Eurydice’s lost cry doth float
Full of regret, and pain, and lonely fear ?
“ O, Orpheus, Orpheus ! O my love, again
If I could see thee, touch thee, then all pain
Would seem as nothing- -but that cannot he !
Not any more lives life for thee and me.
No more for us Spring’s sun, Spring’s flowers,
Spring’s tears —
Only thy music’s ghost is in my ears ;
Here among shadows—other ghosts—a ghost!
O lost my love, for ever lost, lost, . . . lost! ”

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 26, 1896.
Price, 6d,
THE LOGIC OF BRITISH CAPITAL.
“The leading Westralian mining co.’s contemplate making a big reduction in wages. In a speech made by Lord Castledown at a meeting of the West Australian Goldfields, Limited, last month,
here is another straw which shows how the wind is blowing. He said: ‘ The labor, so difficult a problem in Africa, one which has been the real cause of all the bloody fights now going on in
Mashonaland, will undoubtedly be solved in West Australia on different and more satisfactory lines. It may be that the white man will remain the sole factor in the labor question there, or it is
possible that some of the large laboring races may be imported from neighboring shores to work under the superintendence of the colonists.”— DAILY PAPER.
J, BULL: “My troubles about ‘ Australia for the Australians !’ Wot I says is, ‘ Australia for dividends on British capital.' Nigger labor means big dividends. If you weren’t
born a nigger — well, is that the fault of British capital, hey?"

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence" column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But ive
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Hindi and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drau'ings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 26, 1896.
Maoriland and its Prospects.
The Maoriland general election is due in a
few weeks, and already there has set in the
infuriated rush of candidates resolved to save
the country at any cost. The Seddon
Government goes into the contest heavily
handicapped by its own old age, and if it
wins, which The Bulletin regards as a
practical certainty, it will be the best testi-
monial which any Government has yet re-
ceived in any of the Seven Provinces. The
present M.L. Ministry has already served
through two Parliaments. It was created
—as the Ballance-Seddon Cabinet—in
January, 1891, and started business with a
large majority. It was returned again with
a still larger majority at the beginning of
1894—a wholly unprecedented circumstance,
seeing that the average Ministry has worn
out its welcome after an existence of three
years, and it is unusual even to get a second
lease of life by a small majority, while the
ordinary course of events is for the ordinary
Government to be thrown out with con-
tumely at the very first opportunity. As
a rule, the Ministry and the Opposition are
so much alike that the country hates most
the one it has lately seen the most of, and
throws out the party last in power, which-
ever party it is, simply because it has had
the best chance of making its ugliness visible.
If any ordinary Ministry did, by any pos-
sible chance, get six years’ continuous lease
of office, its chances at the polls, after such a
prolonged opportunity to exhibit its useless-
ness, would be absolutely nil, and even the
prospects of the Ministers retaining their
own seats would hardly be worth mentioning.
Under these circumstances, the fact that the
Seddon Ministry is admitted, even by its
opponents, to have a good prospect of a third
majority, stamps it as a wholly unusual
Ministry which either deserves better of the
country than any which went before it—or
has somehow contrived to make the country
think it does.
* * * *
In party politics the party out of office has
no chance to make any serious mistakes of
commission, because it has no opportunity to
do anything. Its business is mostly to howl
at the Government for not doing things
without showing, how they should he done.
If the Government does things which turn
out badly, the Opposition has to be wise
after the event, and show that it wouldn’t
have done them or would have done them
quite differently. If the Government does
them well, it shows, without taking any re-
sponsibility, how it would have done them a
lot better. If the Government shows a sur-
plus it can prove that the surplus ought to
have been larger, also that more money
should have been spent in certain directions,
and that taxation should have been reduced,
and that there is really no surplus at all. The
Maoriland Opposition has had six years of
this sort of thing. It is six years since it had
a chance to make any bad blunders of its
own ; and the Government, having been six
years in office, or more than twice as long as
the average period, has had every chance to
make twice as many blunders as usual.
Also, it has had time to lose twice as many
opportunities, and leave twice as many things
undone. The common or garden Cabinet
starts with one or two ideas only, and when
it uses them up —provided it survives all
other mishaps—it dies because it has nothing
left to promise the people which looks like an
adequate reason for its continuance in office.
The present Democratic Ministry has filled
in six years of hard work, and has used up
as many inspirations as would have served to
keep the old variety of politician in office for
a quarter of a century ; and still it has a
large, vigorous programme left. This says a
great deal for its vitality, at all events.
* * * *
The Seddon Government deserves well of
the people mainly for its energy and fear-
lessness. When it came into office there
were many questions waiting which could
only be settled by experiment. The average
politician endeavors to pass these difficulties
on to the next man ; he advocates trying the
experiment, but lets the next man do it. If
it turns out well, he gets part of the credit as
a persistent advocate of the measure who
didn’t take any chances ; if it turns out badly,
the responsibility is left to the other man.
The woman-suffrage question was looming
large in politics when the Democratic Minis-
try assumed office. It was obvious that,
some time or other, some Government must
tackle it and risk the consequences, and the
Seddon Ministry took the risk. It took an-
other huge risk in settling the Midland Rail-
way trouble—a matter which obviously had
to be seized with both hands sooner or later,
but which a weaker Government would have
passed on to the next man rather than stake
its existence on the matter. It did the same
with the Bank of New Zealand difficulty—
which, considering the size of the institution,
and the size of the crisis which had come
upon it, and the smallness of the community
which had to meet it, was quite as large a
financial problem as that which, in ’93, hope-
lessly staggered the Butcher Patterson Go-
vernment in Melbourne, and which the Dibbs
Government in Sydney only met when the
mischief was half-done. The manner in
which it was settled may not have
been the best or the wisest, but time
was short and the position was novel, and, on
the whole, the Seddon Ministry did better
than any other Australasian Ministry ever
did under like circumstances. There are any
number of people— The Budletin included
—who can see now, after 18 months con-
sideration, that the thing might have been
done better, but there are very few who, at
18 hours notice, would have done it so
courageously or so well. In establishing
the State Land Bank, also, the Seddon Cabinet
might have done better, as the more recent
and better-conceived South Australian insti-
tution has demonstrated, but it was neces-
sary that some one should lead the way, and
make the first experiment, and thereby
supply the experience for those who came
after. The Maoriland State Land Bank, so
far, is successful and solvent, and all the
Seven Provinces owe a debt of gratitude to
the Ministry which had the courage to take
the first step in this matter. If it hadn’t
done so the S.A. institution might have been
non-existent, and the proposed State Banks
of Victoria and N.S.W. might not even have
been mentioned.
* * * *
In addition to all this the present Maori-
land Government was the first to seriously
attack the question of breaking up the big
estates and providing land for the people. It
! ias gone into the land resumption and sub-
division business on a huge scale, has pro-
vided homes for a large number of people,
and despite the jeremiads about its “ ruinous
Socialistic policy ” it has made the business
yield a handsome profit to the Treasury.
Its loan-conversion policy is proving a suc-
cess, and causing a large reduction in the
interest burden, despite all manner of
prophecies to the contrary. It has
established the graduated land-tax whereby
the big estate-holders are heavily penalised,
and the further accumulation of huge areas
made practically impossible, and it has im-
posed an absentee-tax. It was the first
Government which sent Labor members to
the Upper House, and thus broke up the
ancient monopoly whereby that Chamber
was practically a House of Landlords. Also,
it practically abolished the nefarious “ truck
system ” ; passed a stringent Factory Act
which has wiped out unwholesome and in-
sanitary factories and workshops ; applied
the co-operative system successfully to public
works ; abolished the life tenure of members
of the Council; gave a half-holiday to shop-
assistants ; and led the way, as usual, with
the policy of old-age pensions. Its further
programme includes a State Fire Insurance
system, the fixing of maximum rates of
interest, a fair-rent scheme, State audit of
companies’ accounts, the . shutting out ot
Chinese and other undesirable immigrants,
Local Government reform, the adoption of
the Betterment principle, and many other
measures, the desirability of which is suffi-
ciently evidenced by the wrath of the lat
Man and his press organs.
* * * •
Maoriland .is the only province in Aus-
tralasia which is burdened with a huge war
debt, therefore the difficulty experienced
there in making ends meet is not such a
proof of financial incapacity as a similar diffi-
culty would be in more fortunate Australia.
Also, it had to set aside a large part of its
lands as provision for a native race too
numerous and warlike to be bounced out. of
its inheritance like the feeble Australian
aboriginals, consequently it never had the
magnificent land revenue which N.S.W. and
Queensland enjoyed. What land revenue
there was had been mostly dissipated long
before the Ballance-Seddon Government
came into office ; the largest territorial re-
venue it ever received in one year was
£364,000, which compares very badly with
the £2,000,000 that forms the average land
revenue of New South Wales. Also, the
Democratic Ministry took office at a time of
awful depression, when borrowing had been
carried to the last extremity ; and in the face
of all these difficulties it has achieved wonders.
It lias led the way in more Democratic re-
forms than any six of its predecessors, and it
has manfully faced all tlie accumulation of
problems left it by its time-serving predeces-
sors, and hardly ever attempted to gain ease
in its own time by the usual expedient of
passing its difficulties on to posterity.
Its huge financial experiments, hazardous as
they were deemed by all manner of alleged
experts, have turned out right so far as any
definite results have yet been arrived at, and
even financial experts cannot argue against
success.
* * * *
There have, of course, been mistakes and
failures in the Seddon Government’s career.
There are failures in the record of every
Government "which does anything and every
other Government as well, and the Maoriland
Ministry has done so much more than the
average, and most of it in ways where there
were few precedents to go upon and very
little past experience to serve as a guide,
that the only wonder is that the failures have
not been ten times as numerous as they are.
Progress of any kind involves the absolute
necessity of someone leading the way,
and no merely human politician can do
that without making mistakes which will
have to be rectified later on. The Seddon
Ministry, if it lives, will probably, sooner or
later, carry its social legislation too far and
make itself ridiculous by attempting the im-
possible, for the sufficient reason that every
great movement generally goes too far, and
in many matters no one can tell save by ex-
periment where the impossible really begins.
Its leader is fiercely autocratic in many
respects ; the Ward scandal seriously dam-
aged its prestige; and the resumption of
borrowing, evenonacomparativelysmallscale,
and the seizure of the local sinking-funds,
show that its finance is weak and, in times
of necessity, tolerably unprincipled. Against
these things, however, it has to be remem-
bered that tlie alternative is a Russell- Tory-
National Ass Ministry which would be equally
autocratic if it had a similar majority
behind it because Party Government is
constructed that way. Also the Russell
party is the one which borrowed ten millions
where Seddon borrows one, and whose
finance was much more shady and foolish
than Seddon s at its worst, and which can
show old financial scandals compared to which
the Ward episode sinks to comparative in-
significance. The alternative lies between
the Ministry whose enthusiasm for social re-
form may possibly carry it too far forward
and one which has no policy except undoing
what has already been done and going back
to the old stagnation. Considering all the
circumstances and possibilities of the case
the best course Maoriland can adopt is to
return the Seddon Government to office by
the biggest majority it can raise and risk the
consequences. A Ministry which can stand
six years of power without falling into utter
discredit is an institution which only comes
along once in a century or more, and the
country which throws it lightly away will
probably have cause to regret its action later
on and keep on regretting it for long after-
wards. Any Ministry, under the party sys-
tem, is only the less of two evils, but in this
case there seems such a vast disproportion
between the evils that there is no room for
hesitation.

We want fact-justice, evidence-justice, proof-
justice. Out with Suffield from his pigeon-
hole !
The Omnipotent Frock-Coat.
A smirking individual in a long black frock-coat
and wearing a belltopper of offensive glossiness
stood at the bar of Melb. City Court last week,
charged with indecent exposure in a city park
at midday. He was arrested on the spot, and
told the constable who locked him up that he
was guilty, and that, though lie was sorry for
what he had done, he really could not help
himself. Asking for leniency, prisoner’s at-
torney set forth that the crime was inexplicable
and, from its very nature, the act of a lunatic,
and then proceeded to call a number of
witnesses to character. These showed that the
black-coated Maedonaldite had lived for 20
years in the odor of sanctity, and had occu-
pied the position of Sunday-school superinten-
dent with great credit. It also showed
(which was far more material) that the man
suffered from dizziness and, on occasions, from
religious mania. The case concluding, the
P.M. remarked feelingly that it was an awful
thing for a man in the accused’s position,
etc., and lined him £3. The extraordinary
part of the sentence is that during the past 12
months lialf-a dozen cases of similar sort have
been before the same court, and that in the five
of those where convictions were obtained terms
of imprisonment varying from three to twelve
months have been ordered, only one prisoner
escaping without a flogging ! And in the first
six of tlie seven cases referred to accused were
all of the laboring class, one of those flogged
being a ragged, half-starved-looking boy of .15
years. At Melb. City Court the cranky mole-
skin “ exposer ” averages six months and ten
lashes, while the cranky, white-faced, Sabbath-
school boss is permitted to insult and scare
decent women for 60s. The proper place for
all sucli offenders is obviously the lunatic-
asylum rather than the gaol, and if the law.
with its administrators, were not a brute as
well as an ass, it would recognise that fact.
Still, as tilings stand, they show once again the
force of The Bulletin’s now cliestnuttv truism
that no long hat and frock-coat ever yet lay
beside the triangles. Again let it be yelled
through Australia— Tiie lash is for the man
in workman’s garb and for him only !
Old-Age Pensions.
The N.S.W. Parliamentary Committee on Old-
Age Pensions has sent in a report in which it
tackles this difficult question in rather a weak,
spirit, and with, apparently, a limited amount
of success. The gist of its recommendations is
that all single people over 60, who have resided
for fifteen years in the province, and whose
incomes do not exceed <£so a year, shall be
pensioned at the rate of 10s. per week, and
married couples at the rate of 15s. per week.
These proposals seem fair and reasonable; at
all events, the 15s. is none too extravagant.
The cost is reckoned at <£90,000 a year, and to
raise this amount it is proposed to legalise the
“ tote ” and charge per cent, on all moneys
passing through it, which is expected to pro;
duce <£35,000 ; to levy an “ entertainment and
sports’ tax ” of Id. per ticket, estimated at
£8000; to take £II,OOO from the asylum vote,
it being estimated that the pension system will
save that amount of the present charitable
expenditure; to annex for pension purposes the
revenue from billiard licenses (£8000) ; revenue
from fines for drunkenness and breaches of the
jicensing law (,£6000) ; and to draw £22,000


The Convict Butler.
from the general revenue. In view of these
proposals, The Bulletin respectfully offers the
opinion that the committee has made a bad
mess of a great and urgent question. In the
first place, it is reckoned that there are, in
N.S. Wales, nearly 53,000 people aged over
60, but by leaving out those with incomes of
more than £SO a year, and those who have been
resident less than 15 years in the province,
it is expected to bring the pensioners down to
4000 or a little less. This is such a stupendous
reduction that it is difficult to place much faith
in the estimate. At all events, even if the esti-
mate is correct, the figures are likely to increase
considerably within a few years after the estab-
lishment of the system, for N.S. Wales will
necessarily be recognised as a good place for an
elderly person with 12 or 13 years’ residence at
his credit to remain in and complete his qualifi-
cation. It is a noteworthy fact, by the way,
that in Maoriland, where it is proposed to fix
the pension age at 65 instead of 60, and to
insist on 20 instead of 15 years’ residence, the
annual cost of a very similar pension system for
a much smaller population has been estimated
as high as £150,000. Anyhow, the proportion
of people between 60 and 107 —the alleged age
of the oldest cornstalk inhabitant—wflio have
incomes of £SO a year in their own right is none
too large, and as people drop out of their rov-
ing habits with age, the proportion of them
who are qualified by 15 years’ residence is pro-
bably greater than might be supposed. As to
the means of raising the scanty £90,000 a year,
it is suggested that no less than £36,000 of it
is to come out of general revenue, and as the
revenue, in most cases, has no money to spare,
it will probably have to fill the gap with new
taxation, so the Pension Committee hasn’t ex-
plained where the money is to come from—lt
has only suggested that the Treasurer should
do the explanation for it, so far as this £36,000
is concerned. Also, the £II,OOO which it is
proposed to save out of the Charitable Vote is
another doubtful item ; the said vote isn’t nearly
large enough to cope with the aggregate dis-
tress, and even if the pressure upon it is
relieved to some extent by the Old Age Pension
system it will still be inadequate, and nothing
can fairly be spared out of it. The facts of the
case, in The Bulletin’s opinion, are that the
suggested pension system is much more likely
to cost £150,000 or even £200,000 a year than
£90,000, and that, considering the straiteued
condition of the Treasury, all the money must
be raised by new expedients. There are abun-
dant ways of raising it in addition to the
“ tote” and the proposed tax on entertainments.
A tax on bicycles, a graduated tax on estates
over £SOOO in value, newspaper postage, a tax
on patent medicines, absentee tax, tax on
foreign fire-insurance companies, and the annex-
tion by the State of unclaimed balances and
dividends are a few of the methods by which
the required result would be arrived at. If
they left a considerable margin in the meantime,
30 much the better. When Federation arrives
it will be hardly possible to draw provincial
distinctions, and if 15 years’ residence in any
part of the Federated provinces is then fixed as
the qualification, the pension Bill will probably
l go up 50 per cent, in one act. /

Teetotal Twaddle.
At the Victorian Alliance Anniversary (Argus
report) :
Mr. John Yale, the secretary, said the expenditure last
year on drink in this colony was <£3 2s. 9d. per head of the
population, which was 10s. per head less than the expendi-
ture of the mother country. The drink expenditure last
year was the lowest in the history of the colony, the
principal causes of which were the diminished spending
power of the people and the emigration of the male popu-
lation.
The logical conclusion, therefore, is that the
more males take themselves out of a country,
and the lower the wages paid to those who re-
main, the less drink will be consumed, and
(vide the usual Cold Tea arguments) the
greater the national prosperity ! Which con-
stitutes a paradoxical tangle more fatal to tee-
total tendencies than the alluring barmaid ; no
ordinary individual can unravel it and keep
sober, and no cold-water crank this paper ever
met could justify the abject hopelessness of the
position unless loaded up to the muzzle with
the most virplent of snake-juice. Alfred
Deakin was the next speaker, and got this off
his bosom:—
Every measure that tended to improve the conditions
of living tended towards the temperance of the com-
munity, and the village settlement measure, the measures
to deal with the mallee country, and the Factories and
Shops Act were of this character.
If that doesn’t entirely knock the bottom out
of the John Vale sample of teetotal logic it is
nobody’s business.
Lepers at the Back Door.
Says a Launceston correspondent: —
Twenty-nine more Chinamen have just landed in Tas-
mania. Sixteen had naturalisation-papers, and thirteen
paid the poll-tax of £lO. Five of the latter were vacci-
nated by the Government medical officer before being
allowed to land. And such a landing ! The file of weedy,
dirty - looking, undersized specimens of hopelessness
crawled from the deck of the lighter up on to the wharf,
each dragging his goods and chattels after him in bundles
of wretchedness tied up with cane and greasy string. An
acclimatised Chinaman took charge of the queer proces-
sion, and it crawled away up the street, a policeman
keeping well to windward. Before the cortege marched
sundry small boys, who rose to the occasion mer-
rily and assumed the role of drill - instructors.
That sextapedal monster of smiling yellow ugliness
with a freshly-vaccinated tail, crawling up an Austra-
lian street at the mocking invitation of a little
regiment of rosy-cheeked, clean-limbed white urchins
was, to say the least, suggestive. Meantime, the
Treasurer pockets £l3O, and the portly and eminently
respectable citizen smiles a smile of vacant imbecility.
Tasmania seems fated to be the back-door of Australia in
this as in other matters. A small boat can sail across
from Northern Tasmania to Southern Victoria in a couple
of short days, which may account for the apparent dis-
crepancy between the number of Yic. Chinamen and the
amount of poll-taxes credited. It may also, in a measure,
account for the often-noticed fact that many Melb. Chows
profess a surprisingly correct geographical knowledge of
“ Gipshelan’.” If the mainland authorities are not too
busy letting the leper in via the Northern Territory they
might direct a glance towards Vandemonia. And if it is
a Federal glance, so much the better.

The Protected Industries of N.S.W.
Dear Bulletin, —It is a strange commentary
on Freetrade as the alleged “settled policy ” of
N.S. Wales that almost every important indus-
try which the province possesses has been
established by, or assisted by, Protection in
one form or another. Early pastoral and agri-
cultural industry was subsidised by free grants
of land to start with and by the supply of com-
pulsory cheap convict labor, the convicts
answering all the purposes of slaves without
any preliminary outlay for purchase. The early
settlers had the country opened up for them by
cheap convict labor supplied by the State.
Great tracts of pasturage were also leased to
them at a mere peppercorn rental; they didn’t
have to buy the land—it was stolen for them
almost gratis by a paternal Government. The
pastoral industry was, and is, further subsidised
by the construction of railways which carry its
produce to the seaboard far below cost; the
general revenue and the railways of the non-
pastoral districts are taxed to the extent
of nearly £300,000 a year to thus subsi-
dise the wool interest. The beer and wine-
making industries grew up under tariff Protec-
tion, and Premier Reid allows them to remain
at least partially protected by the difference
between the Customs and excise duties. The
manufacture of spirits and tobacco is protected
in the same fashion. The sugar-industry was
created by Protection. The production of
biscuits, confectionery, butter, cheese, bacon,
hams, kerosene, galvanised iron, timber, fruit,
rope, doors and sashes, maizena, preserves,
candles, and cement come under the same cate-
gory —all these industries were helped along by
protective duties imposed and retained by suc-
cessive Freetrade Goverments. The press is
protected by a halfpenny postage duty on papers
from outside the province while those inside the
province go free ; this means, in most cases, 50
per cent, protection. In short, there are so
very few industries in New-South Wales which
haven’t been helped along by Protection that
the alleged “ settled policy ” is a mockery and
a melancholy lie—and it always has been so.—
J.A.H. ■
Dear Bulletin,—To show the absurdity and
danger of the fiscal plebiscite proposed by
M'Gowan, M.L.A., it is only necessary to re-
member a comparison noted by the late Walter
Bagehot, who points out that at the time of
the Crimean War English public opinion in 24
hours was able to turn out the Quaker (Aber-
deen) and put in the pugilist (Palmerston) to
rule and save the nation. On the other hand,
the man who suddenly stepped into Lincoln’s
shoes defied American public opinion for two
years, and laughed at impeachments. In
Britain power has passed from the sovereign to
a committee dependent on the vote of the
people’s representatives. America, on the other
hand, is ruled by a committee independent of
the people and their representatives during a
certain term. Does Mr. M'Gowen know that in
England the fiscal question was quickly decided
as soon as the people made up their minds ?
And until they do so here it is better to have
an election every six months than to enslave
public opinion for any fixed period. In some
Greek republics legislators passed laws and
decreed that those who proposed their repeal
should be put to death. When repeal became
a public necessity, as it always must in such
cases, the patriots who proposed it wore halters
around their own necks. Does M‘Gowen advo-
cate the halter ?—Y.
A country attorney sends The Bulletin the
following circular lately issued by the Incor-
porated Law Institute of New South Wales
2 Castlereagh Street, Sydney,
Sydney, September 15th, 1896.
Dear Sir,
The attention of the Council has been directed to the
practice, which apparently is growing up amongst counter
solicitors, of sending instructions to law-stationers in
Sydney to do conveyancing work, which is really of a pro-
fessional nature —as, for instance, the examination of
abstracts of title with the original deeds.
The Council considers that this practice is open to
objection on many grounds ; and that work of the kind
mentioned should be entrusted only to persons in the
regular employment of the country solicitor or his town
agent.
I am directed to call your attention to the matter, and
to ask your co-operation in putting an end to this undesir-
able practice.
I am, dear sir, faithfully yours,
J. P. CREED, Secretary.
“ I employ a law-stationer to do my searches and
engrossing, and I intend to continue doing so,”
remarks The Bulletin correspondent, “ unless
the Judges order otherwise. No doubt it would
suit Sydney agents very well indeed to have
this work done by themselves, for -which they
charge excessive fees, while not doing the work
half as well as a professional searcher. The
Law Society very largely concerns itself with
advancing the interests of Sydney solicitors,
and in its advocacy thereof often borders on
impertinence. A law-stationer charges 2s. 6d.
an hour for a search in the Deeds Office, a
Sydney solicitor at least as many guineas—and,
of course, the client has to pay. It is a well-
known fact that the Sydney agent gets 50 per
cent, of the costs (as drawn to scale), and this
means often that the poor country solicitor has
simply to take what he can get.”
Ed. Bulletin, — Permit me to disagree with
your theory of “even-handed justice,” as you
appear to understand it. Take, for instance,
the case of the Melb. Union Bank clerk who
came up the other day to receive sentence for
embezzlement. He pleaded for mercy, chiefly
on the ground that he had “ friends who would
help him in the uphill fight of the future,” but
Judge Chomley said “ the prisoner appeared
to have been very deliberate in his thefts, and
his offence could not possibly be treated as a
light one.” Then the Judge inflicted 9 months
hard labor upon the bank-clerk, which is about
the average penalty imposed upon any friend-
less person who “ shakes ” a pair of blankets.
Yet, perhaps, it was enough punishment tor
the clerk under the circumstances, if the object
of imprisonment is to deter the offender from
further crime. Justice in criminal law seems
to be properly regulated by social considera-
tions, despite democratic argument to the
contrary. The poor man who steals a pair of
blankets is such a small thief in comparison
with the embezzling clerk that he might be let
off with 24 hours’ imprisonment, but for the
fact that such a proportionately ample sentence
wouldn’t be calculated to keep him straight for
the future. Poverty, although not actually a
crime, is equivalent to a crime, and it increases
the necessary severity of the sentence accord-
ingly. A scale of punishment for robbery,
based on the money values (so to speak) of the
thefts without any regard for persons, would
be absurdly rough on the harassed individual
who borrows a modest amount from his em-
ployer “without mentioning of it.”—Yours, E.

The question as to who is a “ person in autho-
rity ” has given rise to a marked difference of
opinion among the N.S. W. Judges. Poisoner-
perjurer Dean’s lawyer, in the appeal made to
the Full Court, argued that the chairman of the
Dean Defence Committee was such a person,
and that Dean’s confession, having been obtained
through an inducement held out by that gentle-
man, could not legally be evidence against his
client. As is known, the Judges refused to
accept this view of the case and dismissed the
appeal. A case on all-fours was tried at
Grafton some time since, a young lady being
accused of having robbed her employer of
goods and money. At the first trial the jury
disagreed, and at the second prosecutrix swore
that accused had made a partial confession
when she (the witness) remarked on the accused
hesitating, “ Well, you had better tell me all.”
This brought defending solicitor Donaldson to
his feet to argue that as witness was a “person
in authority ” such a remark could only be con-
strued as an inducement, therefore any subse-
quent alleged confession by his client could not
be evidence. Hence he protested against it
going to the jury. Judge Cohen upheld the
attorney’s contention and the case broke down.
And so very likely would the case against
Dean break down—before the Privy Council.
Australia’s annual tribute to China of hysteri-
cal, parson-drunk girls still continues : last
week five misguided young women were
despatched to the Chow Mission Minotaur by
a white-faced crowd, who themselves stay at
home and guzzle the good things of this life.
The Ku-Cheng massacre of last year, so far
from scaring off the Australian female mission-
aries, seems to have turned a lot of falterers
into active workers—just as, when one woman
suicides in any unusual fashion, an epidemic of
the new felo de se immediately follows until it
gets “common.” Some years ago it became a
popular amusement for the grisettes of Paris
to jump off the topmost pinnacle of Notre Dame
cathedral and dash themselves to pieces on the
stones below. The practice was at length
stopped by the State prohibiting the publica-
tion of such suicides. Had any Australian
Govt, “savee” enough to award a twelve
months’ “ hard ” to anyone mentioning the
name of any woman in the capacity of a female
missionary before any congregation or assem-
blage, or any newspaper proprietor publishing
the same, there would be a sudden cessation of
the exodus of these shepherd-drunk women,
who would work off their superfluous enthusiasm
by banging a tambourine in the local “ Sal.,” or,
better still, by marrying stalwart policemen.
Sundry fossil members of the British Medical
Association have lately tried to pass a resolu-
tion bringing the words “ physician and sur-
geon,” on a professional door-plate, under the
head of advertising. Up to now, as a London
weekly remarks, young and struggling doctors
were expected to merely hang out their signs
and then starve until patients began to roll up.
The B.M.A. objects even to the sign. A saw-
bones is asked to rest content with billing his
bare name on the door, in order that the prac-
titioners with well-known names of their own
to work upon, or the surname of a well-known
relative, may be the only persons to get any
legitimate advertisement worth mentioning.
All professional associations that The Bulletin
ever heard of are actuated by much the same
conservative principle. It is called etiquette,
forsooth. Etiquette demands that a doctor
who, after earning a lucrative reputation in
Sydney, is advised to move to Melbourne for
the sake of his health, shall reject every temp-
tation to announce his arrival other than by a
respectable brass plate, and wait for his rivals
in the new city to tell their friends of the good
new man who has just opened an opposition shop.
Ed. Bulletin,— Your par from a N.S.W.
male school-teacher reminds me of what fol-
lows. I am one of nearly 10,000 N.S.W.
artisans whose employment was reduced from
full to half, from half to quarter, from quarter
to no time at all, on account of dulness of
trade. I have over 15 years’ experience, and
am said to be a first-class workman. My
salary was <£l7s per annum, it is now nil. I
have seven children of my own. I have never
lost one day from sickness, or whisky and late
hours. I have never refused political influence
—I never had any. I have given food to w'hoever
asked it; now I have to ask it for myself.
My clothes are patched from the collar to the
shoe-laces. I’d deal with Chinamen, but they
won’t give me credit. Does it seem just to you
that after 15 years’ experience (and being even
now considered first-class) I should have no
salary at all? Is there not something wrong
with the industrial system which allots such a
fate to the strong and capable artisans who do
the country’s hardest and most necessary work,
under the most unpleasant conditions, while
the stockbroker, the wild-cat promoter, the
parson, and a dozen other parasites thrive for
ever ?— Warrigal.
A Maoriland correspondent to The Bulletin : —
Re your mention of that extraordinary criminal, Butler,
and judge Holroyd’s sentence upon him. I was present
in an official capacity at his trial, at Dunedin in 1880, for
the murder of the Dewar family, husband, wife, and child,
and again at his subsequent trial for burglar}'. The
murder-trial w T as, in some respects, most remarkable. In
the first place, Butler was not defended by counsel, and
he had, without professional assistance, to face the minute
and tireless attack of a Crown Prosecutor like Haggitt.
I shall never forget the pertinacity of his defence, and the
uncanny, nervous way in which he chewed and chipped
his pencil as he followed and noted the evidence. He
gave one the impression of intense vitality.
The crime w'as a most extraordinary one. It was
committed so stealthily as to leave no clue; yet
the site of the murder was one of the most
densely - populated parts of Dunedin, the Dewars’
cottage being literally wedged in between three
other cottages and facing, just across Cumberland-
street, a row of inhabited dwelling-houses. I saw the
cottage in exactly the state it was in when the murder was
committed. The huge blood-splashes were fresh on the
walls of the tiny room in which the man, wife, and child
were done to death. And the marvel of the whole thing was
how an able-bodied man and his wife could have been
overpowered so suddenly and swiftly as not to make a
single cry for help possible, or at least audible to the hun-
dreds sleeping so near them. When, too, one saw the
weapon of destruction, the Dewars’ own axe, which must
have been brought from their own wood-bin, and from
the amount of blood on the walls one realised that many
heavy blows must have been necessary to complete the
hideous job, the marvel was only accentuated fourfold.
It became apparent to all that either chance singularly
favored the murderer in his operations or that one had to
deal with a criminal of the most brilliant ingenuity and
possessed of an unsurpassable power of cool calculation.
All motive seemed to be wanting. Nobody, including
Butler, was known to have any grudge against the Dewars.
They possessed nothing worth stealing or which could he
proved to have been stolen. What conceivable motive,
then, had Butler or anyone else in killing them ? This
was a serious difficulty where the circumstantial evidence
was inconclusive. And Butler made excellent use of it—
far better than nine out of ten counsel could have done
for him. No matter how' keen the public instinct as to
Butler’s guilt, there was really only one item of evidence
w'hich could be described as seriously incriminating him.
It was the condition of his clothes. On his shirt were
found hundreds of very minute blood-specks, many of
them no larger than a pin’s head. They were so numerous
and so w'idely scattered over the arms and front por-
tion of the shirt that it seemed impossible to account
for them by any of the ordinary contingencies of
life. This evidence was brought out in court late one
afternoon during the trial ; and it was most in-
structive and significant to note how Butler took
it. He did an immense deal of pencil-biting rapidly,
and w'ith evident sense of irritation and anxiety, and
complained that the police had “ sprung a mine on him.”
He asked to be allowed to examine the clothes in ques-
tion. His whole attitude was one of deeply intensified
interest. The presiding Judge told him he w r ould
certainly be allow’ed to examine any exhibit at the rising
of the court next day.
Butler’s quick intelligence and fertile imagination
soon gave him a defence on this point, amply
adequate to secure an acquittal even if it did not
prove his innocence. Butler discovered that, in addition
to the blood on the shirt, there were blood stains on the
coat and waistcoat he v T as alleged to have worn during
the murder. The size and position of these he carefully
noted, and when his speech for his defence came it was
v'orth hearing. He was adroit enough to call no witnesses.
Consequently he had, according to the local procedure,
the last word, in reply to the Crown Prosecutor. He said
that the whole case for the Crown rested on those blood
spots. What, to begin with, w r ere they to hang a man
for? Everyone had his clothing more or less so stained
through the innumerable petty accidents of life. A cut
from a razor, or a tramp such as he had when
lately moving through rough country, might be ample to
account for such an appearance.
But to pass on, he said, the theory of the Crown was self-
destructive. Blood was found on his coat, waistcoat and
shirt. The Crown said that the sleeves and front of the
shirt were so besprinkled with blood that it was evident
this came from the bodies as they were being chopped up
by the murderer. But if so, it was obvious that the
murderer had his coat off, and his waistcoat too. That
the Crown seemed to admit. But if it admitted this,
then how about the marks on the coat and waistcoat ?
They must evidently have come from the same source ;
the Crown witnesses, or some of them (said Butler)
admitted this. Then if so, it was clear (and he pro-
ceeded to a detailed and most ingenious treatment of
this portion of the evidence) that the coat and waistcoat
were on and being worn by the murderer at the time he
was committing the murder. Perhaps, however, some
might say that the murderer had deliberately torn off his
coat and waistcoat and proceeded to chop up his victims
like so much wood ; and that whilst the shirt was be-
sprinkled while worn, the coat and waistcoat were
spotted whilst hanging up. This was a ridiculous hypo-
thesis, because it implied a degree of deliberation and
preparation which would be obviously quite incredible.
The Crown, then (said Butler) was in this dilemma. Either
the coat and waistcoat were on, in which case there
should be no spots of blood on the shirt; or they were
off, which was for the reasons given a palpable absurdity,
or at least a gross improbability. This was his defence.
After he had spoken it was clear that he was certain ol
an acquittal. But Judge Williams’s charge made doubly
sure of this ; and looking back upon the result, with
twelve years’ experience, I think the Judge and jury were
right. Not that Butler’s innocence was clear : far be it
from me to say that. He was undoubtedly under the
gravest suspicion ; and the newspapers did not hesitate
to say his acquittal was a miscarriage of justice. Yet
it would have been going further than our tradi-
tions of justice have hitherto gone to convict Butler on
the evidence given. The single item of the blood-spots
was in itself insufficient, to my mind. Yet it is certain
that the evidence might have taken a different shape and
been much more dangerous to Butler had he been de-
prived, immediately on his arrest, of his coat and waist-
coat, which he was, by some extraordinary oversight,
allowed to wear for some days in gaol, and which may, in
the matter of blood-marks, have undergone, by accident
or design, many changes.
Butler’s subsequent trial for burglary was deprived
of its interest by his plea of guilty. He pleaded
guilty to a charge of arson and burglary at the
same time, the inmates of the house being very nearly
burned to death. Anyhow, for this batch of offences
(which included the theft of some church music from the
8.. C. Church—Butler was always fond of sacred music),
he was sentenced to 18 years’ penal servitude, a sentence
which in some small degree tended to appease the public
indignation on account of his acquittal of the murder.
Any notice of Butler and his doings would be incomplete
without mention of the on dit current in Dunedin at that
time about his previous exploits. He had been, I think,
about three weeks out of gaol when he was re-arrested foi
the murder of the Dewars. The term of his expired sen
tence was, I think, four years.
His crime was burglary, or rather several bur
glaries alleged to have been committed under the
following circumstances : Butler came to Dunedir
as the teacher of a Sunday -school in the bad
country of Otago, where he had been stopping. He
was introduced to the Hon. Mr. D (a wealthy anei
well-known Dunedinite) as an active and energetic
“worker” (as no doubt he was, though in a different
line). Mr. D met him on a Sunday afternoon and
(so the story ran) invited Butler to tea. The host had a
distinct leaning himself towards sanctity and Sunday-
schools, and he welcomed any “ good young man ” who
was “ proud to live in the service of the Lord and glad to
die in His army.” So Butler was well-treated and invited
to tea that Sabbath afternoon. No, he said, he could not
stop to tea. He had an engagement. Oh, well, his host
hoped to see him again, and wished him “ God speed” in
the good work he had set himself to do. And so they
parted. The church-bells were just ringing and Butler,
quietly sauntering from the stately and holy pre-
cincts of his host and well - wisher’s mansion,
noted that another ten minutes would see many
of his friends and fellow-workers praising the Lord in the
Lord’s House. He would let them assemble in peace.
For that day he would not join in the ordinary hymns
and chants of humanity, but would do his own little
private prayer alone.
And so Butler whiled away the few minutes
before the services began. And then with a mixture
of the sanctimonious and the cynical Mr. Butler, alias
Smith, alias Donnelly, alias &c., &c., &c., proceeded
to enter quietly and unobtrusively one house after
another of the absent worshippers—four houses in all—
and made a more or less valuable haul in each. In the
end Mr. Butler—l had almost written the Reverend Mr.
Butler—was convicted and sentenced as above recorded.
All of which, as I have said, shortly preceded the cele-
brated trial of what was known as “The Cumberland-
street Tragedy,” where man, wife, and babe were hurried
into oblivion by somebody’s ill-doing. Which somebody
is “to the jurors unknown,” though one day we may
know him.—P’lbbcemax X.
The me anest man, it seems, isn’t much
worse than the mean corporation. For the last 15
years a road-mender has been employed by Don-
caster (Vic.) shire at wages which have from time
to time been reduced from Bs. to ss. per day. Not
content with this, the Council decided at a recent
meeting that as the aged pulveriser was now over
70 and unable to pound bluestone with as much
vigor as heretofore, his services were not worth
more than 4s. a day, which sum the ancient
laborer is to receive until such time as the Council
decides to give him 3s. At this rate he has only
to live long enough to reach an age when he will
have to pay the shire for the pleasure of filling
holes with hunks of metal.

“In Mizzoura,” Nat. Goodwin’s second
effort at Sydney Lyceum, is such an immense im-
provement on the futile “ Nominee ” that the
selection of the latter as the introductory piece of
the season is hereby charitably ascribed to drink.
‘ Mizzoura ” has a distinctly American atmosphere
about it. It is redolent of the land of the wooden
ham and the huckleberry watch—the land where
Napoleon B. Scadder sells wooden oats to Hamil-
ton J. Morgan, and where every citizen has an
initial in the middle of his name. Two of the
scenes are laid in Joe Vernon’s “living-room,”
and the last one is located in Jim Radburn’s
“door-yard,” and, as there never was a “door-
yard ” in Australia, the result is distinctly educa-
tional. The play is a clever, pretty picture of life
in a woolly land, where the people work hard and
talk slowly, and get their beer from the “ drug-
store ” on account of Prohibition, and go out occa-
sionally on an honest, large-hearted impulse to
hang somebody, and think better of it afterwards.
Taking it all round, it is about the best drama
which Australia has seen since the advent of the
“ Silver King,” and the Sheriff is about the best
stage-hero that the Lyceum has contained up to
date. No one is in a hurry in this restful drama,
save the horse-thief when he is getting away, and
the huge person with the yard of beard when he
rises with his store-clothes on and harangues the
mob which has called round to hang its fallen idol,
the Sheriff. Sam and Dave, and Esrom and Cal
all worry peacefully through their summer exist-
ence. ’Liz’beth falls asleep standing right through
the piece; and nothing disturbs the placid calm
of Em’ly ; and Jim, when his free and enlightened
fellow-citizens are seen in the distance with a rope,
slowly writes out his resignation and puts his
collar away in his hat so that it will
be out of the way if he is hanged, and
he will, be able to find it again if he isn’t,
careful inspection having shown him that the said
collar isn’t quite exhausted and may do another
day. He also decides inwardly, even at this
crisis, that if he isn’t hanged, and if Kate resolves
to marry him instead of the train robber, he will
have a new verandah added to the house and keep
a hired gal. Jim, the sheriff, is the sort of man
that a tired, hurried world could lean against com-
fortably for a moment’s repose, and Missouri, like
New Mexico, seems to be the Land of Pretty
Soon, where Dan’l refuses to be troubled even by
an earthquake, and Hank is for ever at rest.
* • • •
The co. shows quite unexpected possibili-
ties in “Mizzoura.” The play suits it down to
the ground, and its nasal snuffle is in keeping with
its surroundings. Nat Goodwin’s Sheriff—the
strong, tired man with the chivalrous soul—is an
almost perfect piece of acting. There are touches
of comedy and poetry all through the character of
the Sheriff, and Goodwin scores at every point.
’Liz’beth, the tired girl, played by Miss Ethel
Browning, is probably second biggest success in
the piece, but the Colonel and Jo and Bill and
Dave—especially Jo and Dave, who are repre-
sented by Clarence Handyside and Louis Payne—
are all excellent, and there are many good points
about Em’ly and Mrs. Jo. The beauteous Maxine
Elliot does her part as Kate in a good ordinary
fashion, and Sam represents the only really depres-
sing feature in the show. There is much elaborate
stage realism about “Mizzoura,” which is hereby
recommended as a clever drama, full of good dia-
logue, and one which every member of the com-
munity ought to see for the good of his soul, if he
thinks he has one.
** * *
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) has re-opened for
a spell of “ Fun on the Bristol,” which old-time
shriek seems to be not so seriously dead as might
have been expected. The present version is not
very much worse than the original. William Cos-
grove’s widow hasn’t the unctuous voice of Johnny
Sheridan, but he is a very good widow none the
less. The most distinctly weak point of the show
is Wilfred Shine as Count Menaggio; that very
clever Irish comedian can’t even make a decent
beginning at being a broken-Italian comedian,
and he seems to be painfully aware of the fact.
There have been many Menaggios, beginning with
Knight Aston, who was distinctly the best, and
the misplaced Shine is far down the list. Also,
Jules Simonsen doesn’t even profess seriously to
be Richard Sparks. Otherwise, however, the co.,
especially Forde, Tom Cosgrove, Maude Lita and
Fanny Wiseman, is satisfactory in the main.
Forde and Tom Cosgrove are about the best Cran-
berry and son up to date. There is a lot of
musical talent in the co., and if something really
dreadful were done to one young man in the con-
cert-scene, and if some of the songs were a little
more novel, it would be good all through. As
regards the young man aforementioned, he should
be permanently exiled to Five Dock.
** * *
The Bulletin’s favorable opinion of the
present Criterion comedy has not deteriorated
during the past week. Mrs. Brough’s spectacled
she-father rather improves than otherwise on
further acquaintance, and the feeble shamblings
of that bogus parent, and her goneness at the
knees, and the way her glasses fly all over the
place when she is “ shook ” by the enraged colonel
for saying that she slept in the same dormitory
with his wife when they were at school, and her
general tendency to provide outrage and then
crawl under the table to avoid the consequences,
are matters which make this tired paper feel
better. Still it is doubtful if Mrs. Brough has
struck the best conception of the character. The
wretched _ mother in the long tail-coat could be
rendered in various ways, and the fact that Mrs.
Brough makes her a defeated impostor from the
vei 7 beginning looks like a mistake. If she
rushed into the pants and the coat with a cheerful
confidence in her ability to do the part, and broke
up gradually afterwards, instead of losing her pre-
sence of mind and appearing as a routed being
from the first, the effect might be even more con-
vincing than it is. A father who obviously wants
to run for the door at the very moment of his
arrival has his drawbacks. Still that distressed
hemale is full of good points, and the present
writer, never having been in her painful situation,
can t say for certain how it would naturally pan
°ut. A citizen whose clothes have all vanished
while he was in swimming, and who finds himself
on the beach with nothing but a belltopper, trying
to pass himself off as a merman, might throw
some light on the true inwardness of the situation.
