. * *
The Cimetographe, already much-described
in this column and spelled in many different
ways, is the latest addition to Rickards’
show at Sydney Tivoli. This latest kind of
’graphe enables any action or set of actions to be
photo’ed and reproduced life-size at any time
afterwards, so that the person who did it can
stand outside of himself, and see himself doing it,
and thereby realise what an awful spectacle he
looked when he did it. Therefore, the ’graphe
has a great mission to serve in this world, and
deserves encouragement.
** * *
R. D. Meagher, ex-lawyer and ex-M.L.A.,
started his Sydney lecturing season at the Opera
House last Monday. There was but a small
house, and to it the orator discoursed for
nearly two hours, mostly on the criminals he had
defended and who had been duly hanged. An
inspection of the dreary list suggests that almost
all the criminals whom the ex-advocate defended
were duly hanged. Mr. Meagher, as a lecturer, is
a very moderate success. There are many
sonorous adjectives in his discourse, and they are
often heaved in regardless of sense or sequence,
but they all sound like a big gong in the distance
on a still night. Thus, he speaks of Australia as
being on the “mighty threshold ” of a new life, and
a mighty threshold sounds as well as if thiscountry
was on the heroic oilcloth of a fresh epoch. Also,
the “ chaste portal ” of Federation is a good phrase,
and a chaste portal is better than an immortal
chimney-pot any day. The lecture is almost
wholly a dull, sordid recital of scenes in Darling-
hurst, not very well recited by a man with
painfully conspicuous hands, and it has penny
hunks of cheap pathos shoved in here and
there in a hard, plain fashion, as if the pathos
was a wooden plug, and there was a hole to fit it
into, and the lecture was a mallet. There are also
bits of gaol language here and there, and
the said language is mostly “ Blimy, Bill,”
and the like. There is no originality about
either “Blimy” or “Bill.” The pictures
the lecturer draws are not living or realistic ; and
they are not improved by the speaker’s habit of
closing his sentences with a sudden yelp, as if
someone had trodden unexpectedly on his tail.
They mostly end at the gallows ; and he follows
them with painful minuteness, and he and the
audience look down through the trap together
after them when they have dropped. On Monday
night almost the only occasion when the lecturer
got away from the subject of Darlinghurst and the
gallows was when he fled into a dreadful figure of
speech about “ the gladiator leaping into the Pan-
theon ” —at least it sounded like that; and the
remark was just about as probable as if he had
spoken of the omnibus leaping into the fried egg,
therefore it was a novelty and a relief. After
hearing Mr. Meagher lecture, the present writer no
longer wonders that so many of that very much-ex-
gentleman’s clients were found guilty; he came
within an ace of finding the doorkeeper guilty, as
he went down the stairs, and hanging him on the
spot.
m* * *
The young lady with the smile like a gleam
of sunshine, whose portrait appears on page 10, is
Miss Lily Titheradge, of the Brough Comedy Co.,
the promising daughter of her father. The said
father has been for many years such an established
favorite wherever the old 8.8. combination was
known, that it is quite needless to be more definite.
Miss Lily Titheradge is very young—so young
that The Bulletin doesn’t profess to be sure of
her age, and her stage experience practically dates
from the co.’s recent tour in Queensland. Never-
theless, she has given promise of so much ability
that her future success seems certain. This paper
isn’t prepared to swear that she is faultlessly beau-
tiful, or that her sweet voice actually “ lays over ”
the angel choirs, or that her shoes are smaller than
No. 2’s, but it hereby backs her bright smile for a
reasonable sum against every other bright smile
on this continent.
** * *
“ Tattersall ” Adams’ Sydney Palace of
Varieties is rapidly approaching completion under
the direction of Architect Backhouse, and the
opening will take place in about two months’ time.
The new temple, the interior decoration of which
is sumptuous, will have seating accommodation
for 1500. A spacious promenade and lounge, in
which refreshments will be served, is attached to
the dress-circle, and will doubtless prove a boon
to the haughty patrons of that portion of the
house. Phil. Goatcher is lessee. It is not gener-
ally known, but it is nevertheless a fact, that Phil,
is closely related to George Washington, who
achieved some fame in the United States a con-
siderable time ago. This fact in itself is a suffi-
cient guarantee that matters affecting the brevity
of the skirts of female performers at the Palace
will be dealt with from a thoroughly disinterested
standpoint.
** * *
At Melb. Bijou, last Saturday, a more-or-
less amateur co., leavened by George Leopold,
gave a spirited revival of “Girofle-Girofla.” Old
warrior Leopold led the stage forces in person,
Harcourt Lee had control of the musical depart-
ment, and the show altogether seemed good enough
to run at popular prices for more than its adver-
tised four nights. Should this fact have happened
to get generally known in the meantime, Lecocq’s
opera will, no doubt, be carried right through the
current week, or even further. Melbourne has
almost forgotten the Frenchy plot of “Girofle-
Girofla. ” This is the tale of two twin-duplicate
sisters who are to marry respectively a mild,
gentle tenor, and a rampageous baritone Moor.
Owing to a temporary annexation of one sister by
pirates, the other sister, who amounts to precisely
the same blushing spinster, is compelled to marry
the Moor after committing matrimony with her
own appointed tenor, lest the dark heathen
should draw a semi-circular saw and lay the family
in ruins. The situation, therefore, threatens to
grow embarrassing until the first sister returns
from the pirates, and each husband is furnished
with a full allotment of wife.
* * * *
It says a good deal for the principal
amateurs concerned that George Leopold, in his
original character of the bald-headed father,
doesn’t appear like one acting alone in the wilder-
ness. The tenor, George Tutton, shapes well in
all respects, barring his feet, and shows more
stage intelligence than many full professional tenor
mummers. Miss Hesford Angus, as a tall, im-
posing mother-in-law, is quite at home in her
part, and Miss Mabelle Davidson, who played the
pair of twins on the first night, is a first-class
amateur prima-donna. She presents a nice appear-
ance and manner, in addition to a pleasing voice,
clear enunciation, and a highly seductive smile.
In fact, if the young lady watches herself care-
fully and keeps her smile under wise control,
she ought to be heard of anon. Ernest Fitts,
having a fine deep singing and speaking voice for
the noisy character of Mourzouk the Moor, counts
among the successes of the show. Other people
are not of much individual importance, so their
crimes may be overlooked. The orchestra is
pretty good, and the chorus capital. Also, the
few sweet girl-graduates in tights wear them to
advantage, but unfortunately there was one pair
unoccupied on Saturday. The missing damsel
had ventured to put ’em on at dress-rehearsals,
and yet when the awful night came she didn’t
arrive with it. The legs were willing, but the
spirit was weak.
** * *
After a furious run, faith in “ A Trip to
Chinatown” is abating somewhat at the Princess’s
as regards seats at five shillings, but at anything
under that price the American entertainment sells
quite as freely as ever. A second edition, so-called,
which came along on Saturday, proved to be the
same old first edition trimmed with fresh songs.
The fresh songs were generally older and fuller of
christv-minstrel sentiment than those they re-
placed, and the ballad about “ Molly Riley,”
warbled in all seriousness by the very large gentle-
man from behind a. capacious shirt-front didn’t
win much approbation in front of the house. The
singer’s artistic brothers and sisters on the stage
received “ Molly Riley ” with great applause, all
the, same. Nothing in “A Trip to Chinatown”
strikes one more forcibly than the patient cheer-
fulness with which the performers sit around and
express admiration for their own little concert.
They remind one of the children who are said to
“ enjoy it better than a pantomime.”
** * *
“ Tommy Atkins,” the second of George
Rignold’s*new melodramas from London, was to
succeed “ Called Back,” at Melb. Royal, on Wed-
nesday of this week. The manager himself will
be conspicuously absent from the cast of this huge
military undertaking, but a brass band and other
extra details too numerous to mention have been
engaged to fill the gap. The policy of changing
the bill in the middle of a week was a success in
the case of “Silver King” during Westmacott’s
season at the same house, and perhaps “ Tommy
Atkins ” will bring George Rignold a well-
deserved change of luck. It depends a good deal
on the cable liar. The Bulletin, in Mr. R.’s
place, would make private arrangements to pro-
cure a crisis at Constantinople and a war-scare of
the most jingo character.
** * *
The Yaidis Sisters, two muscular little
women in blue, are the latest attraction at the
Opera House. Their agrial gymnastics are new
and original, insofar that they perform on a
revolving double trapeze, brilliant with colored
electric-lighted lamps. Also, they shine person-
ally in the matter of being able to suspend them-
selves from a tricky little mouthpiece by their
teeth and spin around at a dizzy rate. Likewise,
one of the sisters dives into a net, and impresses
any beholders who haven’t seen graceful Madame
Antonio fall backwards from a much greater
height. By the way, Madame is to reappear at
the Exhibition Building next month.
** * *
About the last stage appearance of Madame
Simonsen (who died suddenly on Saturday night
last) happened at Melb. Opera House, some six or
seven years ago, when she played Maritana for
her benefit, There was a big muster of former
admirers—among them Parkes, who had come to
Melbourne for the Federation Conference—and
buxom Fanny’s performance showed good cause
and reason for the rally. Even then, at 55 years
of age, she sang much of the music fetchingly,
and many traces of endearing young charms were
apparent in her acting. “ Veri I first married my
Fanny she vas a very pritty girl,” Martin Simon-
sen used to say in his familiar discourses upon
“vimmin” in general. No doubt about it. A
better dramatic artist than any of her children,
Madame was the mother of at least one very
pretty girl and delightful vocalist in Francas
Saville, now almost world-famous. She was the
last of the old Melbourne brigade of operatic
singing mistresses, and many pupils* past and
present, must have been shocked to hear of her
sudden demise.

Ex-Premier Dibbs, when sworn in the
other night as Captain-Commandant of N.S.W.

Rand wick course, on Thursday last, was
notable for the almost entire absence of the
Hebraic element, owing to the Day of Atonement
clashing with the race-meeting. The Gentile
usually does his atoning vicariously, but the
Israelite, who has to forego “ skinning the lamb ”
for a whole day, does a penance to which the raw
peas of the early pilgrims were a mere circum-
stance.
The death of the veteran Mr. Thomas
Buckland, late president of the Bank of N.S.W.,
and for nearly half-a-century a director of that
big institution, removes a clear-headed and able
citizen. Mr. Buckland was most exact when con-
trolling the money of others, as he did in his day
to perhaps a greater extent than any other
N.S.W. resident, but in his personal dealings
there was no more considerate or generous man.
Julia Batten, the Kilmore (Vic.) post-
mistress, convicted of embezzling savings-bank
deposits by means of forged signatures, is rather a
“fetching” woman with golden hair, and her
bright eyes seem to have pierced the court in a
tender spot. Bair Julia got away with 12 months,
a decidedly light reproof for committing forgery
at Govt, expense. Twelve months of such absence
from the man who ate the sugar—there must have
been a man ; it is only for love that spinsters steal
on a large scale—ought to make his heart grow
fonder, if he happens to have one concealed about
his person.
The other day Baron Von Norbeck, a
distinguished Austrian scientist, while exploring
on the island of Gualdalcanar, Solomon Islands,
was attacked by natives and fatally wounded. A
midshipman, two seamen and a native guide of the
party were also killed, and a petty officer has since
died of his wounds ; also, seven other members of
the party were more or less wounded. Absurd
attempts to preserve secresy about the matter on
the part of the Austrian authorities have led to
the formation of perhaps ill-grounded suspicions
that the “ attack by natives ” was connected with
another more or less misty alleged occurrence : it
is said that some weeks ago a seaman of the Alba-
tross struck an officer, and that after a court-
martial he was taken out of Sydney Harbor and
hanged to the ship’s yard-arm.
Verdon’s name was De Verdon, but lie
dropped the De on entering Democratic politics.
His brother, the barrister, stuck to it, and he had
another quasi-pastoral brother well-known at the
Albion. The j ust-deceased banker was the most
intensely clubbable Melbourner. This, however,
only dated from the loss of his wife, years ago.
With her he delighted to live at his Collins-street
banking-house in the city heart. Then he lived
at the Melb. Club, and was its unfailing dinner-
bell as he toddled up Collins-street eastward at
six o’clock. Verdon, not a snob, regretted the
decline of the gentlemanly element in politics.
Like Bob Lowe, he wanted the horny-handed to
fight by deputy through men of culchaw like him-
self, Roebuck and Pearson. Thus he could never
see the game of politics as worth fighting, unless
as in the Parliaments of his time, when a Charles
Jardine Don was an anomaly.
Henry Lawson’s poems get nearly three
pages of notice and quotation from R. Le Gal-
lienne in the English Idles. Neither Le Gallienne
nor the Idles has much critical reputation, but
they butter beautifully, and the magazine has
standing quite good enough to make such space-
allotment a plume in Lawson’s hat, while its
popularity assures a splendid and very unusual
advt. Le Gallienne quotes liberally from “Days
when the World was Wide,” “Faces in the
Street,” and “Star of Australasia,” and supplies
some rather lazy comment to the effect that Aus-
tralia’s ‘(mighty imagination and great heart”
have again escaped into literature, in “ a volume
to console one for the tantalising postponement of
Kipling’s sea-ballads.” Lawson’s “is essentially
music for the people.”
Melb. demise : Octogenarian Pastor James
Taylor. He passed three stages : Presbyterian,
Congregational, Baptist. While young, he became
one of the most eminent English dissenting min-
isters. His health broke down, but he defied the
medicos. Forty years ago he came to Melb., a
lean, withered little atomy, but reared a mighty
“cause” at Collins-street Baptist Church, which
he built, replacing a wooden, ramshackle one.
Undoubtedly recognised as the leading intellectual
Melb. clergyman, he was picked to begin the
Sunday theatre-services, at the Royal. Keenly
evangelical, what was the sensation when a scandal
arose in his congregation ! He was accused of
trifling with the wife of a deacon. A committee
of inquiry was headed by Pastor Poole, afterwards
Age sub-editor, who had such a narrow escape
in the Ly-ee-moon. It was cut short by Taylor’s
resignation. The committee put to him the in-
quiry, “ Was there actual transgression ? ” but the
answer is buried.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The Vic. Leg. Council’s Committee of Inquiry,
appointed to find excuses for not passing the Turner
Government’s Banking Bill, is plodding along with mild
gradualness. If it can’t show good reasons for rejecting
the measure outright, the next best thing is to fill in so
much time with the inquiry that the bill will be
crowded out at the end of the session ; which is probably
the reason for the gradualness. The Committee persis-
tently interrogates auditors and managers of banks which
survived the boom, but even among them the results so
far don’t amount to much. The Bank of Australasia man
and others object strongly to the proposed special audit
on application of a certain proportion of shareholders, on
the very reasonable ground that the agitation to get the
necessary quota of shareholders to sign the application
might create a panic. It being sufficiently proved that
the audit system can’t remain where it is, the obvious
remedy is to create a regular independent State
audit, which won’t require any agitation or any
panic to bring it about. One accountant sug-
gested that a special audit should be granted on the
application of one-tenth of the shareholders, provided
they have “ submitted questions to the directors and
failed to get satisfactory answers.” As the shareholders
always get satisfactory answers right up to the day before
the burst, the only trouble being that the satisfactory
answers are often quite untrue, this is a beautifully futile
idea. The same witness moaned about “ the danger to
managers and directors in the clause providing that they
should be prosecuted for distributing dividends out of
other moneys than profits. Nothing was said by anybody
about the danger to shareholders and creditors.
Several accountants protested against the State
demanding special qualifications from auditors : “ No
Government Board could demand better qualifications
than those insisted on by the Vie. Institute of Accoun-
tants.” Later it transpired that one-half the members
of the institute hadn’t been required to pass any examina-
tion at all. Anyhow, if the Government is only going to
demand from accountants the qualifications which
they already possess, what harm will it do to the accoun-
tants? The National Bank man complained bitterly
about the clause prohibiting banks making advances to
their own directors, and added that his bank had rigidly
abstained from making advances to its own directors.
Why he should object to not being allowed to do what
he never does is another professional mystery. Mr.
Gyles Turner, of the twice-reconstructed Commercial of
Australia, protested against the clause enacting that
all banks should balance on the same day—so that
the bank which is balancing couldn’t inflate its cash
reserves by borrowing from the one that isn’t. As a
matter of fact they never did such a thing. All his bank
did, said Turner, was to lend £lOO,OOO to the Mercantile on
11th January and call it up again on 3rd March. The
Mercantile balanced on Ist Feb., so this loan enabled it to
inflate its balance-sheet, and to deceive its shareholders
into the belief that business was looking up when it wasn’t,
and it apparently borrowed the money for that express
purpose. Gyles Turner, however, says the story is an
absolute misrepresentation ; all the Commercial did was
to lend the dying institution a lot of money before its
balance and get it back afterwards. And Gyles “isn’t
aware ” that the “ balancing trick ” is ever done ; he never
heard of it, anyhow.
* * * *
The select committee of Vic. Leg. Council had
better, for the sake of decency, keep the name of Jabez
Balfour out of its precious enquiry into the Companies
Act Amendment Bill. One of its selected witnesses in-
formed it that the law as it stands against directors at
present is sufficiently stringent, and “ under it Jabez
Balfour had been sentenced quite recently to 14 years im-
prisonment.” Is this impudence or merely ignorance?
The financial law which struck J.B. was a vastly different
thing to the financial law advised upon by barristers in
Melbourne. Will any tall-hatted witness look The Bulle-
tin straight in the face and say that the robbery which
has gone unpunished in Smellbourne, because there was
“ no case” against the robbers, would have gone unpun-
ished under English interpretation of the English law—or
that Jabez Balfour would have come to prison through
running his group of companies in Smellbourne aforesaid ?
The Vic. companies’ law “ as it stands ” —or as it is admin-
istered—wouldn’t land Barrabbas in “quod”—not if he
were a chairman of directors.
• * * *
One objection to the new Vie. Companies Act
has something in it. Huddart Parker and Co., Howard
Smith and Sons, and other such “ private ” firms argue
that the publication of balance-sheets by companies which
don’t comprise more than 20 shareholders is certainly not
needed in the general public interests. On the principle
that secresy is guaranteed for income-tax returns, the
Amendment Bill should draw a distinction between com-
panies whose business represents the sole income, perhaps,
of a few partners, and the common or garden kind of
companies in which hundreds of shareholders make specu-
lative investments without being in a position to look at
the books or get any idea as to how things are being
managed.
» * « *
The committee of inquiry appointed by M.L.
Leg. Council to inquire into the Bank of N.Z.’s affairs re-
ports that ex-Treas. Ward gained no advantage, and, so
far as the evidence shows, attempted to gain none by the
Colonial Bank amalgamation, and that there was no proof
of any corruption or improper conduct on the part of the
Government. As the Leg. Council committee—which is
quite a different thing from the one appointed by the
House of Representatives—represents to a great extent
the Fat Man and anti-Seddon element, this certificate of
character is worth something.
* * * *
A week or two back an aged Bank of N.Z.
shareholder was sued for calls at Auckland. When de-
fendant’s name was called an ancient wreck got up with
difficulty and made his statement. “ I am 74 years of
age,” he said, “ and am unable to work through a paralytic
stroke. I saved up the money to buy these shares so as
to have a provision for my old age. I have paid two calls,
and have no more money.” Then the Court ordered the
sad Methuselah to pay £53 11s. 4d. and £5 Bs. costs. The
private-bank system is a great institution. Had the
B.N.Z. been a State bank it could never have cracked till
the country cracked.
• * * *
According to one witness at the Maoriland
Banking Inquiry, the total losses written off by the B.N.Z.
during its troubled existence of 35 years were over
£4,000,000.
* • • *
The vast, unwieldy concern, the Modern Per-
manent Building and Investment Society (Melbourne),
doesn’t look much better. The loss for the year ended
31st July is £11,431, compared with £2630 loss for the
previous year and £13,800 the year before, therefore
the concern is losing almost as much as it did
before it made an arrangement with its creditors, and
when the outlay for interest was £13,000 more than it is
now. The actual income (£21,289) is £4OOO less than it
was in ’95, which isn’t especially cheering. The Modern
is a concern which would have been solid and prosperous
if it hadn’t got the borrowed-money curse so badly. It
was built on £200,000 of shareholders’ money and
£500,000 of deposits, debentures, &c.,_ most of them Eng-
lish, and the interest on these weighs it down badly.
The report states that the assets haven’t been written
down and still appear at their book value, which is in-
teresting. Loans to the nominal value of £656,000 yield
£20,045, equal to a little over 3 per cent., and it would
seem that £40,000 worth of property was sold during the
year, and didn’t fetch the book value by £7900. Under
these conditions the said book values look rather un-
reliable.
• * * *
Victorian Permanent Building Society (Melb.)
shows a loss of £7439 for the year ended 31st July. Pre-
vious year the loss was £BlO7, and the year before £15,809,
so things are getting better in a certain limited sense.
Something over £400,000 of borrowed money is what is
the matter with the Victorian. The gross receipts are
£7991, and the interest on borrowed money alone is
£9404, and, taking things all round, the miserable concern
earns less than half its outlay. Loans to the alleged
value of £590,000 yield an average of £1 ss. per cent, per
anhum, so, on the interest return, it would look as if the
present value of this £590,000 of alleged securities was
something like £150,000. What improvement there is
arises wholly from reduction in interest and working ex-
penses ; the revenue keeps going down with a sickly thud.
It has dropped now to one-half of what it was two years
ago, and even then it wasn’t much.

Commercial Bank of Australia’s .£lO preference
shares now stand at about £3 ss. Considering that they
are guaranteed a 2 per cent. div. for three years and 3£
per cent, afterwards, and will receive as much more div.
as the profits allow, this isn't much of a result, though it
is a good improvement on last year, when these shares
wandered between £1 2s. and £2 15s. Colonial (Melb.)
£9 15s. preference shares are offered vainly at £3 ss.
Against this, National (Melb.) are actually at a handsome
premium, and Bank of Victoria are nearly at par. Also,
National Bank deposits have sold in large quantities at
20s. Id. in the £. The saddest-looking Sydney bank
stock is the A. J.S. (£7 10s. paid up), selling at £llls. 6d.
Fifteen months ago they touched £2 Bs. According to
Monday’s quotations the A. J.S. per cent, deposits were
only worth about 15s. —a lower figure even than those of
the Commercial of Australia with their reduced rate of
interest—and A. J.S. inscribed 41 per cent, deposits were
offering at 12s. 6d., which looks like a bad case of want of
confidence. As for Queensland National deposits, the
bottom seems to have fallen out of the market, and buyers
offer 10s. in the £.

Queensland Deposit Bank, which reconstructed
a few years ago, announces that it will probably ask for a
reduction of interest on its 4)4 per cent, deposits on the
general ground that business can’t be profitably conducted
at present rates.
* * * *
Sauerbeck, the well-known statistician, reports
that the average prices of 45 principal commodities
touched the very lowest figure on record in June, ’96.
They were lower than even in Jan. and Feb., ’95, which
were formerly the record months for cheapness, and were
vastly lower than in Jan., ’94. This is rather rough on
the N.S.W. Foreign-trade party, which professes that the
reason why things are no cheaper now than in the Pro-
tectionist era is that prices have gone up in Ilingland.
And they haven’t gone up ; they’ve gone down.
♦ * * *
The City of Melbourne Bank directors charged
with issuing false balance-sheets “were spared the
indignity of being placed in the dock, and were
accommodated with seats behind their counsel.” The
frock-coat again ! The poor devil of a baker’s carter
charged with embezzling 30s. he has collected for his
master, is never “ spared the indignity”—for he weareth
not a long hat. The Bulletin is not here arguing that
the bank-director should be put behind the spikes—far
from it. It is arguing that no man whose guilt has yet to
be proved should be placed there. “ Once accuse a
man of a crime and people will be ready to believe
him guilty. Place the most honest and pleasing face
behind these rails, and it at once becomes a face that
expresses every passion,” said Dailey on a memorable
occasion.
* * * *
A recent sale of Queen-street (Brisbane) pro-
perty realised £320 a foot, considered high nowadays.
During boom-time £lOOO a foot was not uncommon, and
Townsville allotments have before now realised £250 a foot.
* * * *
Ramornie Meat Works, Clarence district
(N.S.W.), now having ceased work for this season after
seven months’ active operations, put through 19,030 head
of cattle during that time. The output exceeds that of
any previous season, and the bulk of it goes to fill a
French army contract, said contract expiring in '97. The
meat-extract goes to Liebig, who puts it up in neatly-
labelled pots and sells it at 1600 per cent, profit. The
waste meat from which the extract is derived until re-
cently went to the pigs. As some hundreds of pounds of
extract are now made weekly, every lb. of which brings
65., it will be seen that the owners of these works are in
for a good thing out of waste products alone.
* * * *
Melb. City Council, the other day, accepted a
tender of 7s. Bd. a day for man, horse and dray. The last
contractor received 4s. lid. The strength of these Tory
institutions in our time is in the fact that they know when
to give way.

There is a Chinese insurance co. in Sydney with
a comprehensive scope. It insures against death, acci-
dent, fire and marine losses, and there is a special clause
in its ideographed policy entitling the parents of the
insured to £3OO should he be hanged for killing a
European in self-defence, while £2OO is the recompense
given to the relatives of an ins ired Chow who is murdered
by a European. Also, the fortunate policy-holder is
entitled to receive £3 a week during incarceration for an
offence for which the insurance co. considers the bar-
barian beak has unjustly inflicted punishment.
* * * *
The Amos G.M. Co. (N.S.W.)—The half-year’s
report and balance-sheet is a sorrowful document. Cash
in 8.N.5.W., Sydney, £1 18s. 5d ; A.J.S. Bank, Blayney,
2s. 9d. The mine was let on tribute to one of the pro-
moters, who soon had the good sense to abandon it.
“ Before taking this course (to wind up) your directors
deemed it advisable to make an attempt to dispose of the
mine in London (the usual course) and with this view
applied for, and obtained, six months’ suspension of
labor-conditions, and also secured a report of the mine.”
The present co. to receive either £10,090 cash for the
whole mine, or £2OO cash and one-third of the shares in
any new co. fully paid up, “ present co. not to be liable
for any expense.” A wise precaution. Two-thirds for
£2OO (and shares), but £lO,OOO for the whole !
* * * _ *
The chances are that already many a prospector
is cursing the Australian Star, which has been the means
of landing him in the Clarence River district. Has any
goldfield ever been found in Australia which would justify
the sensational statements made in these articles ? The
boom seems to have been written-up by the Star’s ship-
ping-reporter. Sydney Shipping News compliments the
writer of the sensation on his mining lore, and the Star,
with becoming modesty, reprints the par. The wild
statements in the article and the headlines about “a
mountain of gold,” &c., have caused numbers of men to
rush to the field. The Star man talks of a gold belt 150
miles long, of huge hunks of gold—“ one boulder of ex-
ceptional richness was sold for 395.” Most of the wonder-
fully rich things seem to have been found either on Mr.
Sain See’s selection or very near it. Great stress is put on
the information that the only route to Yugilbar, the
station on which the rich reefs are found, is by way of
Grafton—to which Mr. John See’s steamers run, by the
way. The present writer has been to the district twice, by
different routes, neither by way of Grafton. As to the
remark that a great mistake was made in landing the
Taylor’s Reward machinery at Woolgoolga(Coff's Harbor),
and that it also should have gone to Grafton : that would
have meant about 100 miles more sea-carriage and 50
miles by land, against less than 10 miles from Coff’sHarbor.

* * * *
Experts representing mining syndicates, notably
from Broken Hill and Sydney, have hern testing the
reefs on Warrata and the Albert gold-fields, N.S.W., but
none of the shows have changed hands. A ton of stone
from the Brilliant lease, treated at Ballarat school of
mines recently, went 23dwts. It was bagged by Capt.
Langdon on behalf of a Broken Hill syndicate. The
leaseholders were offered £5OO for the property, but
refused. Welsh and party, on same line, had their first
trial crushing recently of 12 tons, treated at the local
battery. Result, 14>40z. Property considered one of the
best on the field. Other promising claims in the vicinity
of Welsh’s, but very little work done. The Warratta
(the original prospecting claim), assisted by a Melb. syn-
dicate to the amount of £2OOO, is systematically develop-
ing, and some rich specimens discovered. On Bendigo-
absurd to have another Bendigo !—S.E. of Warratta, the
most promising claims are Howes’ Reward, Western
Star and Carbine. Reef runs north-westerly with under-
lay east. Three crushings from Howes —25 tons for 60z.;
50 tons for 2140 z. ; 50 tons for 3oz. ; and 20 tons seconds
for loz. per ton. Adjoining the Western Star is the Black
Prince, which has had a trial crushing of 10 tons for a
return of to the ton. About 500yds. South on the
same line ofreef is the Carbine, 50 tons at grass awaiting
treatment. Holders say it will go 2oz. per ton. Charge
for carting and treating at the battery 355. per ton.
Nearest railwav to field is at Tarrawingee, 140 miles dis-
tant. Cost of'living, etc., 130 per cent, above Sydney
prices.

Henry Copeland’s energetic support of Fat
Man’s claim on the gold found on his private land should
be effectually set at rest by the Privy Council decision of
February, 1877. By that decision all such gold is the
Crown’s.

Cobar Mining Co., better known as the Chesney,
is re-organising to work the property on a bigger scale.
It has a very large area, next to the Cobar gold-mines.
A solid working capital would probably make it a big
mine, and that it should have been for so many years
neglected is not to the credit of the Melb. mining men
who control it.

Woodstock mine, N.S.W. : “ The directors have

* * * *
G. D. Meudell, on W.A. goldfields : —“ln two
things alone the Govt, has failed lamentably—in not pro-
viding water, and in blocking the railway from Esperance
Bay to Coolgardie. Eventually Esperance must get the
railway. ... A few influential funerals in Perth
would be a public boon just now. When the fields are
properly represented in Parliament, this national work
will be accomp’'-hed. After seeing W.A., one is not
quite sure whether to follow the banner of Briggs (Age)
or Daly (Argus). The former was correct in his strictures,
the latter accurate in his facts. The proper course seems
to lie between damning W.A. fields and damning them
with faint praise. This much one must put on record :
the amount of work, especially prospecting work, done is
stupendous. At Kalgoorlie a manager thinks nothing of
sinking four, five and six shafts on a lease, when in Tas.
or Vic. one would be considered sufficient. I expected to
count shafts by the half-dozen at Kalgoorlie, but found
them in fifties. The map gives but a faint idea of the
number of mines and the amount of work they are doing
both above and below. (Then where is all the gold ?)
Serious mistakes have been made in starting to treat ore
with imperfect machinery. . . . Add to this the
accursed over-capitalisation of British co’s, and one need
not be a prophet to predict that very few of the British
mines will pay divs. till Australians buy them from the
liquidators. W.A. wants patience and people. The gold
is there, and will talk for itself by and by.”
* * * *
Broad Arrow, W.A., is about the only mining
place in Westralia which has no cause to complain of lack
of water. There is a rush in a small way to Smithfield,
right on the Arrow Lake, and a good deal of new ground
is being taken up there. The Kingsley Hall, just floated,
is the only show of any importance yet, but it is a pro-
mising district, and in a year’s time is likely to become a
big place.
• * * *
Bayley’s amalgamation scheme, if ever perfected,
will not be very welcome to No. 1 South shareholders. It
is proposed to amalgamate the South and the Reward,
and the Browne and Lyon people are trying to run in their
two leases —Ford’s Hill and No. 1 North. The South, with
a capital of £120,000, is not much good as it stands, but it
is the only mine of the four which might pay dividends
with a reasonable capital. The Everard lease struck a
good patch a while ago, but is not working on the
gold now. It is possibly being left to show the directors
when they come over.

From a W.A. reliable mining-man to the Bul-
letin : “ Nothing sensational in the way of mining to re-
port. Burbank’s, lam told, are raising stone superior to
anything they have yet had. Menzies crushings are
keeping up an average of two and three ounces. One or
two batteries will start at Black Flag shortly on good pay-
able stone. Some decent shows have been lately opened
up at Widjimoola, between Coolgardie and Norseman.
Hundreds of acres have been taken up by a knowing
crowd, and several purchases have already been made.
The day for ‘ wild-cats ’ has passed. Most of the Eng-
lish and foreign buyers here now want to see something
for their money. Pity that a more honest set was not
sent out in the first instance. The curse of the country
now is the enormous amount of money being lavished on
rank duffers.”

* * * *
Ed. Bulletin, —A recent par. re an historic
meeting of London shareholders fn a much-puffed mine
recalls a few incidents that occurred in connection with
the visits of experts during the flotation of that alleged
mine in London. A high official in the co. used to
accompany each expert down the mine, or round the
surface, as the case might be. The first to arrive was
a man who runs a paper on the field, and fancies his
knowledge of mining a bit. After inspecting the drive
and commenting on the reef, he remarked, “ Of course,
you will make this your main hauling-shaft ? ” “Yes,”
said the official. The shaft was 6x3. and on top of a
quartz-blow about 50ft. high 1 The next to come was the
chairman of a big land-syndicate—they hang upon his
every mining’ utterance in London. After the usual in-
spection of stone from a small leader on the footwall, and
one of the drives, he paralysed the official by asking where
the reef was. The official fell against the side
of the drive and gasped out, “ You are stand-
ing in it.” Accompanying this expert was another
who, on being asked if he was going below, replied that
he didn’t care about going down a shaft that anyone else
sank, but if he sunk it himself, of course, he would go
down the rope hand-over-hand. I afterwards heard him
pronouncing the concern a grand show, and enlarging on
the quality and size of the reef at the 100 ft. level, a place
to which he hadn’t the courage to go. The next to come
was a well-known red-headed personage. The manager
stepped on to the ladder and asked Mr. Coppertop if he
was coming down in the bucket. He said, “ Oh, no ; I’ll
take your word for what is below.” Fancy taking the
word of a manager for what was in a mine in which he
held a big interest! It was chiefly on the report of these
alleged experts that the mine was floated. —Tambo.

* * * *
The directors of the Great Boulder, according
to Z. Lane, than whom, now, there is no greater W.A.
mining authority in this world, and probably few in the
next., are going to sink the deepest shaft in W.A. —they
have ordered the manager to go down 1000 ft. “ Here we
are working,” writes a Bulletin correspondent, “ a strange
formation, carrying great gold. We are at all times in
great straits for want of water, and are all afraid to sink
for it, i.e., on the lode formation. Do the lodes penetrate
the harder country, which must exist below, or do they
not—that is the grand secret.”

Brilliant Deep Levels, Charters Towers, has, to
the great joy of the plucky local residents concerned,
struck at nearly 2400 ft. a reef formation in the shaft
showing gold. This is the deepest point at which gold
has been found, or sought for, up North.

« * * *
It is stated in Melb. that a co. has held 269 aches
of auriferous land at Costerfield (Vic.) for five years with-
out fulfilling any labor conditions worth mentioning, and
for 12 months without fulfilling any labor conditions at all.
The Age adds that the matter having been brought to
the notice of the Minister of Mines, he has ordered the co.
to fulfil the labor covenants under pain of forfeiture. He
hasn’t forfeited the leases, as provided by the statute, you
observe, for what (if the allegation is true) constitutes a
glaring breach of the law—merely ordered the co. not to
break the law any more. Why not apply this principle all
round ? When a poor man is found stealing a blanket,
let the Chief Secretary write him a polite note on a huge
sheet of paper, drawing his attention to the fact that if he
goes on stealing blankets it will really be necessary to
prosecute him, dontcherknow.

* » * •
Dear Bulletin,—ifo your “ Wild-Cat ” remarks
on assays as applied to mining prospectuses. There is no
doubt the assay system will always be a fraud. However,
here is a plan used for the last 10 years, which I have
found to work well. If in breaking quartz I wish to
know what it is like, after firing a shot and having cleared
away the rougher stuff, I gather up the smaller stuff from
the floor and for a guide simply pan it off, but for closer
work have it assayed. And, again, to try a paddock of
stone no better plan can be adopted than to make a dish-
ful from the very top of the heap, and try it as above.—
Tomingley.
♦ * * *
In W.A. recently, an imported mining-manager
took charge of what he reckoned to be his claim, put on
a man, and worked it industriously for three months.
Then a wild-eyed prospector emerged from the ground or
the scrub demanding : “ What the bianky do you
mean by spoiling my show ? ” The manager then found
he had “made a bloomer”—and moved on. At latest
dates he was still moving on in search of his own particu-
lar plot.

Australia’s least extravagant Governors
(The Bulletin likes this way of putting it) live at
the extreme east and west of the continent. One
of them, the other day, wanted wine for Govern-
ment House, and wrote to several merchants
for quotations. A low fellow who had been
approached before calmly replied that he was
quite sure his firm could not supply anything
cheap enough ! The other Gov.’s experience of
“ this damnable democracy ” differs slightly. He
wanted a kangaroo to hop picturesquely about the
grounds of Govt. House, and, hearing of a man
who owned a very fine educated marsupial, wrote
and asked him to call. The Govt. House crowd
was delighted with the animal’s performance, and
V.R.’s representative was lured for once to in-
cautious enthusiasm. “I tell you what,” he said
to the proud owner, “I’ll give you a pound for

sweater.”

such a fine animal I’ll give you 255.” With a

him, and he turned. “ You seem to have made a

wanted ’’—exploding—“ WAS A BLANK!

* * * *
Echuca (Vic.) was much distressed at the
thoughtlessness of its municipal authorities in not
offering a reception, “or any sort of welcome
demonstration, ” to Gov. Brassey and party when
they passed through the town last week. Barring
the railway officials and a number of school
children, there was nobody on the platform to
hooroo the vice-regal train on its arrival. Conse-
quently “Uncle Tom,’’after bowing gravely to the
porters and the children, took his wife for'a quiet
walk as far as the neighboring sawmills to fill in
time before resuming his journey. Local corres-
pondents of the Melb. dailies detected a sad
expression on His Excellency’s face, as though he
had reckoned on a glass of champagne and a bis-
cuit, and they seem to think that the Mayor and
corporation ought to be locked up for insulting
behaviour.

* * * *
Holy Adelaide again ! The other day Mr.
Hartley, chief inspector of schools, fell off his
bicycle and was severely injured, whereupon the
Education Minister advertised that
He would be glad if any teacher of the State schools
who feels so disposed would, to-day, one minute before or
a minute after regular school hours, write on the black-
board and permit as many children as are willing to do so
to repeat with him these words, or words to a similar
effect:—“Our Father which art in Heaven, grant that
our dear master and beloved friend, Mr. Hartley, may be
restored to health.”
As if the majestic God of the universe could be
expected to alter the course of Nature at the
behest of a number of mumbling school-children,
in the ostensible interests of a school-inspector.
Mr. Hartlev has since died.

charged against him, lie was even a more extraor-
dinary ruffian than represented last week. For
the road from Lambton turns past the house
where he lived ; and he might have waited for his
alleged victim at a score of convenient lonely
spots. Why he should let her pass him, then
dodge round in front to sit on the gates of the
house she lived in, and finally wait to attack her
until she was right home, and could holler for help
with the certainty of getting it at once, puzzles
mortal understanding ? Why didn’t he delay a
little longer, till he got her on the front-door mat ?
* * * *
The allegation that new evidence is re-
quired in the Suffield case before an enquiry can
be held is merely a departmental fiction. N.S. W.
Criminal Law Amendment Act, under which an
enquiry is asked for, says nothing about new evi-
deuce. It says that a prisoner is entitled to
MERE COMMON SENSE.
CLANCY (a month in the foorce) : “Be
gob, sergeant, there’s Big Mick an'
Dwyer killin’ each other; shure,
hadn’t we better -.”
SERGT. O’HOOLIGAN : “Aisey, Clancy ‘
me boy, aisey. Sure, ye have a
lot to l'arn in the foorce yet. Just
wait until they’re done, and they’ll
go down quite quiet.”
enquiry if “ any doubt or question arises as to his
guilt.” The matter of Suffield s guilt is crammed
with doubts and questions. And if the Act really
did demand new evidence, there is plenty of it—
as to the peculiarities of ex-Judge Windeyer, who
tried Suffield’s case. A great deal more is publicly
known of Mr. Windever’s temperament and
mental qualities than was known half-a-dozen
years ago, and the bulk of this added knowledge
makes the justice of Suffield’s conviction more
questionable than ever.
* *
In one way Suffield did himself harm at
his trial. His “ statement ” (it was before the
days of sworn evidence by prisoners) consisted of
a declamatory speech dwelling with some passion
upon the way he had been treated at the police-
court and the refusal of bail; and declaring that
he was one well-known wherever he "had lived to
be courteous and gentle towards women. He
seemed specially angry at the suggestion that he
could, as alleged, have thrown a stick or a stone at
prosecutrix, or used any violence towards her.
And, finally, he appeared to lose his head, and
wound up without saying any one of the things
he wanted to say re the fact that his clothes had
been examined by the police, and their spotless
state was incompatible with the charges. In con-
cluding, lie said : “As to this accusation, I spit
upon it ! ” All which, even though it may have
been a perfectly honest expression of indignation
at a false charge, produced a bad impression,
Photo, by Falk.
Miss LILY TITHERADGE, of the Brough Comedy Co.
whereas the girl had made a good impression—as
girls so often do.
Judge Windeyer afterwards said that he had
closely watched Suffield’s demeanor and was sure
it was that of a guilty man. It does not, how-
ever, follow that because the Judge’s instinct was
right in the Dean case it was right also in the
Suffield case. For, as the world knows, Win-
deyerian instinct was sadly at fault in (for in-
stance) the recent Butler case.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,— Be Suffield being kept in
prison through-refusing to petition the Crown for
mercy, it may not be generally known that there
is a man in the debtors’ prison at Darlinghurst,
where he has been imprisoned for four years and
six months through refusing to file his petition in
bankruptcy. He was committed to gaol by Judge
Windeyer for non-compliance with an order for
payment of alimony to his wife. He can obtain
his freedom at any moment by simply filing his
schedule ; hut this he refuses to do, as he considers
the order made an unjust one, and nothing will
make him unbend. Unless Parliament interferes
I am absolutely certain that he will spend the
remainder of his life in gaol—or in the lunatic
asylum, to which his incarceration is fast driving
him. It appears the (alleged) Justice Department
can’t, or won’t, do anything to get the man
released.—H.B.
* * * *
Three Tas. papers have been landed for
libels recently—damages being awarded in each
case—the Zeehan Herald, Hobart Clipper,
and, greatest of all, the Launceston Daily
Telegraph, against which Capt. Strachan got
the local record verdict of .£2OOO and costs.
This trial was a raking-up of old, ill-founded
reminiscences, and the article was the pro-
duction of a Government messenger, who labored
under the delusion that the gallant captain was
dead. The efforts of defending counsel went to
try and prove that plaintiff was as alleged, but as
defendants had withdrawn their first plea of justi-
fication, counsel had to be constantly reminded
they were transgressing.

On Sunday mornings,
in Russell-street, Melbourne,
between Bourkeand Lons-
dale-streets, half the
Chinese population of Lilly-
buck-stleet will be found
intently watching some
twenty of their number
endeavoring to master the
intricacies of the wheel.
The Chow appears to take
most kindly to the bike,
and the half-dozen or so
who are able to ride act as
instructors to the pedallers.
Falls are frequent, but no-
body laughs except the lan-
guid larrikins at the street
corners, the Chinaman him-
self and all his saddle-colored
progeny regarding the wheel
as a joss-given instrument to
be regarded with respect.

* * * *
The “ Christian Endeavourers,” who were
allowed to disfigure with a cant phrase the engine-
front of the people’s train which brought them to
Sydney, and who in their holy fanaticism paraded
the city aggressively sporting the badge of their
weird clan, were a God-forsaken-looking lot if
God’s chosen are to be judged by appearances.
The excited faces of the gaunt, dowdy, flat-
chested women, aglow with that “ spiritual en-
thusiasm ” which would ride rough-shod over all
obstacles and force the Great Ter-uth on all sinful
outsiders, give a disagreeable shock to unemo-
tional persons, who stare and wonder at the mean-
ing of it all. The men, an unhealthy crew, badly
in need of exercise in the fresh air and sunlight,
most of them with weak eyes and wearing glasses,
and always surrounded by the women, whose
cloaks and gamps they carried.
TheEndeavourers could best be studied at Quong
Tart’s restaurant, between one and two o clock.
The wily Quong makes special terms for religious
bodies ; but a lot of the saints, not satisfied with
tea and scones at “cut” rates, demanded bread
and butter thrown in. As it was not in the bond,
the attendants charged extra, whereat the godly
wailed loudly at the pay-desk, settling under pro-
test, while some actually asked for receipts for the
amounts pending enquiry !
* * * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph seems to have
secured that rare bird, a holy reporter. Here are
some slabs from his description of the local
Christian Endeavour pow-wow. First, as to the
arrival of the saints from abroad :
From the carriage windows red and white bannerets
fluttered like lance-pennons in a cavalry-charge. Inside,
the Endeavourers’ song of recognition fills each compai t-
ment, and the strains float through the windows, and are
caught up by the eager watchers on the platform. Travel-
stained and weary, as they necessarily are, the visiting
Endeavourers have lost none of their spiritual enthusiasm.
Their eyes are bright, and their faces are radiant with
eager life. As they alight, their hands are grasped and
worthy words of welcome uttered, whilst the gentler
members, both visitors and welcomers, rush into that
embrace without which no function, spiritual or other-
wise, appears to be complete.
Yum, yum !
Mr. William White was leader of the prayer-meeting,
and fully 200 Endeavourers had torn themselves from com-
fortable couches to take part in devotional exercise. An
excellent tone pervaded the meeting. Prayers were
short, crisp and pointed ; the hymns were bright and re-
freshing; and the whole proceedings had an elevating
effect.
Obviously an expert in prayer. But, after all,
these are” the days of specialism. Presently he
proceeds to talk of “the denominational rallies
in the churches of each sect, and trustfully says

MEETING.
that the Endeavourers “were filled with the fire of
holy enthusiasm ” before the meeting was closed :
No one could listen to the stirring sentences of the Rev.
H T Bun-ess of Adelaide, on the absolute necessity tor
“ whole-hearted ness ” without feeling their hearts moved
within them. The most unimaginative and phlegmatic.
Endeavourer must have realised that life was worth living,
could he only catch “ the revival spirit which the Rev.
A. R. ’Edgar! &c., &e.
Also ~
The Rev .J. H. Mullens delivered a vigorous address on
“Pentecostal Outpourings,” and was followed by the
leader and the Rev. Silas Mead, both of whom were in the
spirit and spoke with mighty power Not a minute was
wasted Hymns and prayers followed each other rapidly,
and were pointed with. spiritual fervor which found a
ready response in the hearts of all present. _ }

ANOTHER CHAPPIE: “ Well—just after she passed, I saw her look round.”
During a recent visit of a Govt, inspector —
a doctor—to an Australian meat-preserving works
he “ cut down ” five tuberculous cattle out of a
mob of 70, and out of another lot of 118 he con-
demned nine! The question arises as to what
becomes of tuberculous beasts in the absence of
the inspector.
* * * *
Dr. N. A. Cobb, of Sydney, a short time
back cut up the eatable part of three bunches of
nice-looking celery, as far as it was tender, and,
on washing the same, obtained nearly 300 thread-
worms, belonging to six different species. I hread-
worms include the Trichina, the parasite that
causes trichinosis, or pork measles ; Filaria, the
worm-parasite found in the blood of colored races;
and other wormy parasites of man.
The spirit of the
Inquisition still sur-
vives. In a country-
district a poor, hard-
working widow, wish-
ing to get the charge
of some boys from the
Neglected Children's
Department, applied
to her parish priest
for the necessary re-
ference as to charac-
ter. He refused to
give it, on the ground
that one of her chil-
dren was an atheist,
and bullied her for
bringing him up as
one. She forwarded
the letter and form
to her bishop, but re-
ceived no answer.
This sort of thing
helps to persuade one
that the Church and
a decent form of so-
ciety cannot possibly
co-exist. Given such
a spirit in such times
as ours, what were
the chances of that
supposed atheistic
son, nay, of the ortho-
dox mother herself,
in auto-da-fe epochs|?

Another item
(says a Bulletin cor-
respondent) that
helped to make St.
Patrick’s Fair, Melb.,
a success. Hearing
that the N. Melb.
faithful were arrang-
ing a fair for their own
church for about the
same time, Arch-
bishop Carr visited
them and said: “You
N. Melbourne people
help us with St.
Patrick’s Fair first,
and then we’ll come
and help you.” They
did so, and still
the half-finished walls at N. Melb. remain half-
hidden by huge advertising hoardings. Of course
his lordship did not say when he would come.
* * * *
Barrister Lowers contributes a witty
article to the Vic. law students’ quarterly, the
Summons, on the drafting, passing and effects of
the Vic. Legal Amalgamation Act (said to
have been passed to give a prominent Vic.
boomster solicitor a leg-up for a judgeship). He
certainly voices the opinions of the junior bar,
but it is rather singular that the junior bar
couldn’t see the injustice of excluding W.A. solici-
tors from Vic. at the inception of the Act when
no Victorians wanted to go to W. A., while now
the Act is anathema, when the whole Vic. bar
wants to but can’t bolt into W.A. There s a sort
of poetic justice about the thing, that softens its
asperity in the eyes of laymen.

interference of Gov. Gerard Smith in a mattei of

his Excellency’s aide-de-camp, to “go to h .
Maorilanders demand that Admiral Bridge
shall station his squadron in their waters for some

have the advantage of meeting Ins officers, the
influence of the local New Woman ! Even her
large, splay-footed native policeman has palled on

“Go to h—l!’’
The Gov. and the station-master
Were discussing a disaster,
Both their pulses beating faster
As their voices rose and fell,
When the'master of the station
(Not to say, the situation)
Breathed the sudden imprecation—
“ Go to h—l!
Go to h—l! ”
He cried in indignation—
“Go to h—l! ”
“ Go to h — l!’’
His Excellency dispersing,
Quite unfitted for conversing
With the man he’d left a-cursing,
Sent a hade-de-kongy swell
To demand an explanation
From the master of the station,
Who renewed his exhortation —
“ Go to h—l;
Go to h—l! ”
His sultry exhortation —
“ Go to h —l! ”
“Go to h—l! ”
They didn’t drop “ down under,
But' to Perth they rode in wonder,
Looking lilacker than the thunder,
So the daily papers tell,
Where they lodged an information
’Gainst that master of the station,
Which consigned him to damnation,
“ Go to h—l!
Go to h—l! ”
Inch-along in degradation
Down to ’ell !
“ Save the Gov. ! ”
’Tis the prayer of saint and sinner
At a loyal public dinner,
May his Nibs be crowned a winner
In the Land of Light and Love,
And when rulers grave and portly
Get an answer given shortly,
May it always be a courtly
“ Go above !
Go above ! ”
A gentle, dulcet, courtly
“ Go above !”

He stands before me robed in rags and dirt.
How many months since last he changed his shirt i
His swag—a fetid roll of filthy rags—
Is wrapt in bits of blanket, patched with bags,
Where vermin of the lower orders swarm,
And help on frosty nights to keep him warm.
He’ll beg, and lie, and thieve, but toil he shirks ;
A thing of his description never works.
Along with being mean, he’s hard and. cruel ,
He’d rob a corpse or steal a sick man’s gruel.
No pleasure can he find in men or books :
His only joys—short stages and good cooks.

Dear Bulletin,— ls the preference by
Anglo-Saxon women for churlish men a proof of
superiority or of degeneration? It is generally
accepted that one of the signs of civilisation is the
deference displayed by men to women, despite the
tact that a marked resemblance between the
primitive woman and her more highly-developed
sister lies in their mutual contempt for excessively
courteous men. The whole tenor of George
Kgertons ‘Keynotes'’ bears out this. There
is a lot of sound knowledge of this feminine
trait in the navvy s advice to his mate : “Kick
hell out of her, Bill, and she’ll love you all the

A WOMAN’S LETTER
~ Tl Sydney, Sept. 21, 1890.
My Dear Myee—
Randwick, as a dress-show, was fluttering and
breezy, clearly foretelling that summer-gowns are
to be built on “ ventilation gossamer ” principles.
Owing to the tropical weather, so fashionable in
London and Paris this summer, no end of frivolous
chiffons have been devised to be cool and comfort-
able in. While the thermometer in the head-
centres of style kept within bounds, we obediently
followed our leaders and sweltered in heavy velvets
and such, with a temperature over the century.
But when they tasted that sort of thing themselves
down under,” they gasped with horror, and now
perspiring womankind rejoices in coolth.
Grass-lawn is very much “in methinks we
are all going to be weary of that fabric. The
mere man might consider it a sort of glorified
brown holland, but let him wait till he has to foot
the bill ! Chine silks glistened on many of the
best-dressed at Randwick ; the colored blouse and
v s ¥ rt is too useful to be utterly
abolished, and still adorns many who ought to be
tired of it by now. Hats were huge and most
fantastic a Derby Day sensation was the nine
guineas worth of headgear adorning one of Syd-
ney s richest women. As an investment of capi-
tal it was a success, but as a tile it was a failure.
i\o hat could be pretty with all -that money in it.
Sydneys own vice - regalities dressed with
aggressive plainness all through. Lady Hampden
failed to respond to sartorial challenges, and
heeded not the most violent color-onslaughts on
the part of her subjects. The regal-looking Lady
Uormanston mixed blue with green in an up-to-
date gown. Gov. Brassey’s daughter-in-law tries
to model herself on “ Hop’s ” social-gimlet lady as
much as her youth and fresh looks will per-
niit; and will succeed in time, no doubt.
J best-dressed woman in Sydney,” Mrs.
Charles Fairfax, looked like a pretty, fuzzy
little I rench doll. Maxine Elliot loomed majestic
on the lawn ; she had a grass-cloth dress, a violet-
strewn hat, and her sister Gertrude. Mrs. W. R.
. llson exhibited several different costumes —all
in good taste. The wife of the only Melb.-made
bart. strove to look well-dressed, but—her dress-
maker had not helped her. The Madden girls
looked plump and apple-cheeked enough to satisfy
even pa himself, who has so often made public
confession of his adoration of the buxom. Ladv
understudied her youngest daughter,
i A. of Three” booms along much more
cheerfully than might be expected of a woman in
her depressing position* There is much original
light humor m Mrs. Brough’s frock-coat, and
when she forgets she hasn’t a lap and lets the
photos, drop between her knees the audience laugh
all they know how. Her three girls also keep up
their spirits wonderfully, considering that their
admirers are trying obstacles to meet with in life’s
common way—although Carey in a steward’s
jacket, Majeroni in a lobster shell, and Harry Hill
in a gaudy blazer, helped to make a pretty scene
in Act 11., in a room very girlily adorned with
ribbon-bows and odds and ends.
All the gowns are girly, like the room. Aquila
and Vesta robe themselves almost alike, while
Cassiopeia seems to go off “on her own ” and
tints. Winifred Austin,
the hydney amateur, is going to make an actress ;
she is vivacious and has an uncommonly effective
eyebrow-lift—theaverage player has about as much
expression m that part of her phiz as if she had
gummed a penny moustache over each optic. Lily
f itheradges pretty arms show out of her simple
sieeves m a nice sort of way. Sir Port and wife
upholster themselves quite appropriately as the
military swell and partner, and Miss Temple is
most artistically untidy as the slavey.
f iT h \ Cr i‘ d ,-°\ has ad the week been full of Melb.
folk who looked hard at each other and coughed
pensively when Mrs. B. delivered the stern moral
of the piece and apostrophised the Petticoat as
the source of all true feminine dignity and in-
fluence. Mrs Murgatroyd’s quailing before the
stony glare of the Medusa-like Port made each
secret hankerer after bloomers clutch metaphori •
cally at her skirts lest they should vanish utterly,
lor Melb. women are going in for-and into—
. the other things ” much more than their Sydney
sisters. Down South the bloomer-clad wheel-
woman is distinctly visible, but she—l supposeone
must still call her “she”—hasn’t yet shown up
much m Sydney. y
It is the bicycle that is doing it, of course. A
at dikes and bikeresses shows me
that the Sydney girl finds the pastime far, far from
£ a L , A woman can’t possibly be enjoying her-
self when she is painfully cramping her knees in
an impossible angle, grabbing at the handle-bars
like a drowning man at a straw, and propelling
herself about the country by a series of jerks and
wobbles-and she shouldn’t try and “ bluff” any-
body that she is enjoying herself. Some days ago
1 saw an energetic woman working her passage
along a suburban road in a high wind. She was
carrying her bike much more than her bike was
carrying her. With her hat firmly tied on with a
handkerchief, and an expression of craven fear on
her face she trundled along working her jaws
vigorously on a mouthful of chewing-gum. In
Melb. the ardent cycliste often throws aside her
prejudice and petticoats together and flies over the
smooth tram-blocks like swift Camilla o’er the un-
= m V orn - • B V t ? properly-built habit andl
slender figure m a high saddle enable the skirted
rider to defy criticism anywhere. Mind you I
said a slender figure. J 9
The gum-chewing habit seems spreading beyond l
cycling circles. Here and there in the theatres
a monotonous munching go on steadily
from act to act. Some women seem to like finish-
ing their dinner in the dress-circle. I would sus-
pect them of ham-sandwiches in a hand-bag, or
pork-crackling, only they are usually those to
whom the true inwardness of a ham sandwich is a
thing accurst. There is one point in favor of the
quiet chew—it stops chatter, and the dress-circle:
jaw now works entirely on the silent system.
. T hat wh ite satin gown of the Murgatroyd
girls, and the wild struggles of the society reporters-
to get it different each time it is described, recall
tae moan of a “leather of Daughters,” who writes
agitatedly to a Cooktown paper complaining that
the papers will describe his—and others’—daugh-
ters dresses at public gatherings; consequently,
the gowns get as “ lienspeckle ’ as their owners,
j e F lrls as^£ continually for more millinery to
the detriment of the family funds. “ Miss Mont-
morencx Johnson chose blue and green and looked
sweet to a degree.” There is a lot of poetry in a
line like that. The word “chose” brings up a
vision of a jaded faded lady’s maid humbly laying
the contents of a luxurious wardrobe before her
haughty mistress, who reclines on an easy chair
and capriciously lets her fancy lead her whither it
will among the shimmer and glimmer of satin and
pearls. In reality, the lady who looked sweet to
a degree has generally worked up the dress at
home herself, assisted by a seamstress at sweated
rates and meals-with-the-family.
. Walker, the Concord millionairess—even
m these hard times, she is credited with .£50,000
a year celebrated the racing-season by inviting
sassiety to meet together in the Exhibition,
a ' va ?t expense with crimson carnations.
All the pinks in Victoria and S.A. had been sent
up for the festival per train, and the ballroom was
one large and vivid blush. The hostess herself
helped on the effect by wearing brocade of an en-
sanguined hue, and the guests at supper fed in an
encarnadined glow that recalled the Terror sun-
sets in the Potter-Corday revolution. It was a
pity that the decorations could not have been re-
tained for another evening and a charity ball fixed
up, as then others besides the Nicest People might
have gazed at the gorgeousness. Miss Walker
who has given £3OO to send a quartette of N.S.W.
mounted riflemen ’Oine next yen' o display their
prowess before the eyes of royalty at the Islington
Tournament, will leave for the Big Smoke herself
m Eeb. next.
Juvenile Sydney gathered in force at Moore
lark on Friday and Saturday at the Public
School Athletic Association sports. Aspiring
Cornstalks swarmed over the grand stand and
grounds generally, hung enraptured over the
railings of the vice-regal pen, and discommoded
the Gov. by dropping peanut shells down his
neck. There was a miscellaneous show of assorted
sports going on altogether, like a made-in-Germany
toy with a windmill, a horse-race and a railway-
train, all wound up to go at once. Centipede-like
battalions of thin-legged small boys exercised
before His Ex’s eye, and hundreds of art-muslined
girls vigorously swung clubs and dumb-bells. The
1 orest Lodge school-girls, in Kate Greenaway
frocks, were the smartest at the Maypole dance.
Vast quantities of spindly black stockings were re-
vealed, enabling me to prophesy that the locally-
raised ballet of the near future will have to depend
on cotton wool for its calves.
The Society of Artists’dance for children hap-
pened at Ciiff’s rooms on Friday evening.
Crowds of infantile Jacks and Jills and Pagliacci
and cooks and shepherdesses processed solemnly
round the room under the guiding hand of Miss
1 heo. Cowan, watched some conjuring tricks, de-
voured much light refreshments, and went home
to dream of the hobgoblins and bogiemen sketched
for them by artists Souter and Mahony. The in-
nocent-looking Miss Mult was clad in white
muslin and a realistic spider, there were several
Chinatown girls and Jap. dolls, and an entire
family of both sexes came in the raiment of
Southern plantation coons. The microscopic leg-
gings of a Tom Thumb stockrider were very natty,
and a black fairy with elongated limbs looked
rather like a South Sea belle arrayed for a siva.
Among the “Miss Muffits ” and “ Gipsies ” at
:a fancy-dress ball it is rather hard to hud some-
thing that half-a-dozen other girls arc not wearing
also. At a recent shivoo in a somewhat tropical
region, a girl struck on something original—at least
the costume hasn’t been worn outside Eden. She
•decided to appear as “Foliage,’’and fixed upabodice
and fichu of Indian moss and skirt of long plan-
tain leaves. The robe was “ sweet to a degree,”
and the wearer was having a good time until the
greenery began to curl and wilt with the heat. Her
alarmed and resourceful mother took her hastily
outside and applied the garden water - pot,
which freshened her up again for a time.
On drying, however, the leaves shrivelled with
great rapidity, and in two minutes the girl had no
more visible foliage than an autumn oak. The
onlookers called aloud on her ma to take her away,
and the victim was conveyed home in a table-
cloth.
In order to vary the monotony of praise and
pool-scooping au naturel, the Christian Endeavor
Convention has been whooping up festive little
games of various sorts during the week. For in-
stance, a party of Endeavorers visited South Head
by moonlight. The worst of S. Head as an even-
ing resort is that the lighthouse rays have an. in-
convenient way of “stalking” a strolling couple
and shedding a sudden and embarrassing brilli-
ance on the proceedings. But, that apart, it is a
place where one may experience a refreshing time,
and the sightseers Arcadianly wandered hand-in-
hand about the cliffs, artlessly warbling hymns,
and settled themselves in pairs in secluded nooks
and corners, endeavoring to shelter themselves
from the wind behind large umbrellas. Same old
endeavor!
Farewell meetings for ten missionaries for the
China Inland Mission are to be held next week.
J he apostles will go and speak the Terruth among
a benighted and wholly-reluctant heathen com-
munity who will horrify the missionaries by chin-
chinning Joss and refusing to leave off when re-
quested. The extent to which the lady apostles
will shock all the conservative notions of Chinese
decorum by Western modes of dress and behaviour
is harder to calculate, owing to the difficulty of the
language, but it counts as a factor in the massacre-
raising. The unmarried woman—whether Protes-
tant missionary or It. C. nun—is abhorred by Celes-
tials as something against Nature, and it is an
uneasy feeling that the “ fankai ” may spread
celibacy among his womenkind which makes John
hate her so.
Amid “great enthusiasm,” the N.S.W. National
Guard has been formed, and now will only want
.Nellie Stewart singing a soprano song, and Elton
m a tight uniform, wnh black buttons down his
pants, to be a whole comic opera. At the School
of Arts meeting, ex-Premier Dibbs arose and care-
fully explained that the Govt, paid all exes, of
uniforms, rifles and ammunition, and then the
cheering became quite wild, and the meeting
settled down to the serious business of choosing
what it is to wear as a livery.
Men always have the best of it in this
world. Fancy any Government fitting out a
band of women for any cause whatever and
turning them loose in a dressmaker’s show-
room with a paternal smile and “wear what
you please ! ” J ust cast your eye over the costume
in which the gallant hundred are going to
nationally guard us : “ The tunic is scarlet, with
blue facings; the trousers blue, with white
gaiters ; headgear, a shako, with N.G. inscribed
in front.” A member of committee exhibited a
sample get-up, and then the meeting walked
round the exhibit and gave opinions that “it
wants a little taken up here, dear,” or “ much too
full in the gathers, love.” The uniform, as a
whole, was approved of, but, in a burst of patriotic
economy, the defenders of their country resolved
to be self-sacrificing and suppressed the gaiters.
W. H. Paling & Go. send a waltz, “ Broomlea,” by
Bessie King, a fine succession of crashing chords.
Sydney girls have a knack of turning out brisk
and tuneful waltzes, and “ Broomlea ” is the very
thing for the soulful young man at the p;ano at
the suburban dance.
( Smart audiences do not seem to find life in
Mizzoura” as exciting as the gilded saloons of
pleasure in gilt-edged and utterly untrue-to-life
dramas of the “ Camille ” and “ Tosca ” type, for
they do not rush to visit the idvllic village of
Bowling Green very eagerly. The weak spot in
the play is the principal female character. A
woman cannot look beyond her own “ fatal
beauty ” to find a cause for any trouble that occurs
generally is a weak character. And in this case
she is a snob as well. The magnificent Maxine is
rather too overwhelming as Kate Vernon. With
her surpassing physical attractions, she seems as
much out of place in the blacksmith’s parlor as
Venus of Milo with her arms on would look serving
m a ninepenny tea-room. The part is a misfit for
Maxine, but her gowns aren’t. They are simple
print and zephyr, but in them she looks queenly
even to massiveness. Her black hair is parted
a l K j brought down over her ears with a little blob
of hair over each temple. A vivid scarlet sun-
bonnet is the most noticeable feature of sister Ger-
trude’s performance. That really deliciously lazy
little Lizbeth is artistic even to having the pink
ribbons of her wedding-dress all carefully showing
the creases where they had been rolled up. She
could easily find a “ stiddy job ” after marriage
letting down that coat for Dave, especially at the
sleeves.
Among the Nicest People the latest craze is the
Zoo party. Led thither by Capt. Ferguson on
Monday in race-week, they studied zoology from
the living subjects at the Gardens, and drank tea
in tents erected for the purpose on the grounds.
Hie rich and liberal host (who had previously
largely contributed to the funds, now at a low
ebb) undertook all exes., and it was a very nice
party indeed. And now some people are grumb-
ling thatitis because the party-giverisan A.D.C.,
and said to have <£15,000 a year, that he is
allowed to swell the receipts for one afternoon by
treating his friends to a gratis sight of the monkey
cages, and standing the racket every time they
want to hear the lion roar. The above “ some
people ” indignantly declare that, in some way,
the “ Rocks push ” are being deprived of their
rights as citizens when Zoo parties are given.
But when you come to think of it, there is abso-
lutely no reason why the “ Rocks ” or any other
push shouldn’t go and do likewise. It would
increase the gate, and cause a big rise in the flour-
trade, if they went and fed the bears with buns
when the Nicest People are surveying the
menagerie.
A club with a side entrance ” is considered
useful from a man’s point of view, and very likely
if men associates roll up in large numbers to the
(*' \\ Sidney Ladies C lub they will explain where
the convenience comes in. The ladies are wel-
coming men to the new club now in course of pro-
motion—but men don’t welcome women to their
clubs ; in fact, a man’s definition of a club is “ a
place where you get away from women.”
Two of the three big Sydney clubs only open
then' doors to women once in a blue moon ; the
third is never entered by petticoats at all. When
woman gets her club she will be less exclusive.
Hitherto the average woman’s club has consisted
of a puff-box, a tea-pot and bread and butter. The
new club seems to want man as a sort of sandwich.
Writes my Melb. correspondent :
“ Lord Richard Neville, acting as private-secre-
tary to Gov. Brassey in place of Wallington
departed, has managed to get himself seriously
dishked by at least one Melb. pressman. It seems
that the hireling scribe called upon R. Neville for
some every-day morsel of vice-regal intelligence,
and was told in effect to wait outside on the mat
until Ins lordship should be pleased to whistle for
him. At which the baffled interviewer took offence
and went away to swear, instead of stopping* to
swear on the premises. He was foolish in remov-
ing his indignation whilst it was hot. Neville
doesn’t look as though he had ever heard any bad
language of importance, so a whiff of bitter impre-
cation from an evening journalist might have
blown his fragile form back to England.
Ihe orrible tale of the Sydney victim who
tried corrosive sublimate for her freckles, and
shed those blemishes along with her skin, recalls
the experience of the Gippsland (Vic.) man whose
hair came off holus-bolus in his hand after an
application or two of Yankee color-restorer.
Nevertheless, for the sake of our national looks,
enlightened face-massage should be encouraged in
Melbourne, where, as globe-trotters tell us, the
prettiest faces are marred by frowns and conse-
quent crows’-feet—the effect of the city’s glare and
gritty dust. Judging by the congested condition
of the lift-traffic, one such establishment, near the
Collins-street entrance to the Block, already wages
vigorous war with the thorns in the facial flesh of
a fashionable clientele.
“ Cleanliness and friction, plus a holy horror of
chemicals and make-up, are the cardinal doctrines
of the scientific face-masseur. All the beauty-
men of the last visiting English cricket eleven
swore by face-massage. And well they might !
Their healthy, brown complexions and sleek, un-
lmed faces won them feminine admiration by the
ton, unlike their opponents, who ran the whole
gamut of objectionable complexion-shades from
leathery yellow to black, and seemed to wither up
under the sun’s rays for want of cutieular sap.
Crov. Hopetoun, glowing from the hands of his
masseur, was not a ruddy young man, but a week
up country, beyond coo-ee of his rubber, left him
as limp and sallow as a played-out yellow kid-
glove.
‘ r I he coming Mayor of Melbourne means to cut
an unprecedented dash during his term of office,
and already the future potentate has begun to
make many preparations to that end. He is spend-
ing money upon alterations and additions at his
private residence, situated near the city, so that
some of the more select ‘ functions ’ may be held
there, m a somewhat garden-party form! Mean-
while, the mighty host that is to be has not made
an absolute certainty of the billet.
Warblesoine Lord Shaftesbury, according to
the report of some people, who probably invented
it, says he is off ‘ Home,’ heartily sick of trying to
please everybody (without success) at Melb. Govt.
House. All the same, he professes a strong attach-
ment to ‘ bush life,’ and declares that if he had the
capital to spare he would invest it in a station and
be a real Australian squattah.

“ The most remarked features at the Mayor of
Melbourne s recent hop were a parson in dress-suit
and simple worldly cravat, and a number of gaudy
gallant defenders. These latter excited frequent
curses by wearing spurs which wrought much
mischief, tearing gowns and even trousers. In

ins precious boot-ornament into a pretty girl’s—
ahem ! —leg, and the victim had to retire to the
