Austral Salon is making efforts to re-capture
the fairly-“ cultured ” essence which used to flavor
its earliest assemblings. Meetings now are run on
afternoon-tea-and-a-little-music lines. Within 30
minutes after opening the room is crammed and
ventilation nowhere. “Bringing a friend’’ has
spread to such proportions that two committee-
members now keep the gate and vigorously de-
mand a sight of paid-subscription tickets.
“The Brasseys and the Clarkes are ‘thick.’
Everyone remembers the jealousy between Mrs
Hopetoun and Mrs. Clarke, and the final good-
riddance kiss publicly administered at Spencer-
street station on the departure of the high-spirited
Governoress. But Mrs. Brassey and Mrs. Clarke
have much in common, including maturity of age
and a hankering after well-advertised benevol-
ences.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

■drives a London hansom.
Marcus Clarke loved the Jewry, but it was
a Gentile who tore the gas-fittings out of his
house.
One of the Adelaide University professors
is stated to be fully qualified to instruct bullock-
drivers in the art of objurgation.
Speaker Abbott will be 54, and ex-Judge
Windeyer 62, on Sept. 29. Ex-Chief Justice Vil-
leneuve Smith (Tas.) will be 77 Oct. 3.
The greasy address presented to Gov.
Brassey by the Deniliquin Town Council con-
tained seven “ Lordships ” in 20 lines.
Colonel Madley, the new S.A. Police Com-
missioner, was head-inaster of St. -Tames’ model
and training school, Sydney, till 1873, when he
left for Adelaide.
If the name be any criterion, the rule
appointing only Englishmen to R.C. bishoprics in
Maoriland has been broken through, Father
George Lenehan being the new Bishop of Auck-
land.
A Westralian patriot tells an Imperial
Governor to “go to hell,” and is abused for his
disloyalty. Rubbish ! the man is obviously only
a loyal citizen anxious for a further extension of
British influence.
Odd, or perhaps not, that the obituary
advt. of Knight Verdon omitted all his titles,
being simply “George Frederick Vernon.” As
with Graham Berry—casting his titled slough to
fight the election.
At the sale of Parkes’ books a “Slang
Dictionary,” which can be bought new in Sydney
for 3s. 6d., fetched 12s. 6d. And, judging from a
number of the deceased politician’s public appear-
ances, it must have been well used.
Cardinal Moran considers it impudence and
insolence for Anglican Bishops in Australia to
sign “ F. G. Melbourne,” “ S. S. Sydney,” or “J.
R. Adelaide,” and then he claps a big cross in
front of his own humble _monniker.
The unrelenting way in which old Francis
Longmore has waged war against Victorian land-
monopoly, had its origin in the fact that when he
was a boy his people were evicted from their hold-
ing in the North of Ireland and left without
shelter on the roadside.
A well-known Western Vic. doctor, who
suffers from speech - impediment, enters local
store :“ A p-pound of su-sh-sh ” . Shopman
(wishing to help him out): “Sugar, sir?” Dr.
(briskly): “ No, you damn fool; how can I shoot
d-d-ducks with sugar ? ”
Peebles, the American spiritualistic lec-
turer, is coming here next year. When he toured
Australia some years ago, Alfred Deakin was
showing some mediumistic powers, and Peebles
has published an interesting communication from
Edgar Atheling, which purported to be received
through the mediumship of our Alfred.

The father of “ Mystery-of-a-Hansom-Cab ”
Fergus Hume died the other day in Dunedin
(M.L.), of which city Fergus is a native. Hume
p'ere, a physician, was a mental specialist, and,
after a long experience in hospitals, established a
private asylum of his own. He passed away full
of years (73) and good “copy,” leaving, besides
Fergus, a son, now civil-engineering on Westralian
fields, and three daughters, the youngest of whom
(Mrs. Willeby Hawthorne) is getting noticed in
London as a soprano.
Anarchist Andrews has on the stocks a
magnum, opus in the shape of a poem of 120 pp.
or thereabouts, in Miltonic blank verse. The sub-
ject may be perhaps best described as “ Rag-
narok.” The title is “ Apollyon.” He has finished
writing it, and, with his indomitable energy in
the face of what, to ordinary men, would be im-
possibilities, he is slowly printing artd stereo-
typing it with what he calls “ a home-made
stereotyping plant.” Poverty, deadly sickness,
social ostracism seem only, in his case, to be
stimulants to great work.
THE GOLDEN WEST.
SON (who has a craving for information): “Say, dad, what makes the sky so yellow this evening?”
THE OLD MAN “ Oh, probably they're opening-up another phenomenal Westralian gold-mine.”
Millionaire Gray, who lately died in Ade-
laide, aged 89, claims to have built the first house
in that city. Its roof was of reeds, and the rafters
and other timber came from the scrub near by. He
is believed to have saved legacy-duty by splitting
up his property among his grand-children, the
progressive succession-duty in S.A. being collected
on the individual bequest, and not on the total
estate.
The re-marriage of
ex - Governor Bowen,
now well on to 80, illus-
trates the juvenility of
feeling and fancy sooften
found in dilettanti lit-
terateurs. The man who
has to scribble for bread
or who believes he has
a literary mission may
wear his brains to shreds
and dies like the average
of his race, but the self-
complacent book-loving,
book-making literary tri-
fler remains green and
sappy when the rest of
his generation is fit only
for firewood. Fifty years
ago Bowen presented
himself as an author, not
in any aggressive mood
or with a pretence that
the public demanded
anything from him, but
purely as a small mark of
respect for everybody.
When affairs were
gloomyin Queensland he
filled up his time there
as Governor inseribbling
couplets and stringing
commonplaces together
under the name of his-
tory. His hobby was to
dress his acquaintances
with Greek titles and
compare them with clas-
sical prototypes. His
then wif-j was a Greek,
and tuo branches of her
maiden name—Diaman-
tina and Roma—he fixed
indelibly on the Queens-
land map. But he was
always young, always
self-complacent, always
fond of book and pen,
and whatever breaks
came his way he was always ready to begin life
anew. Also, he always deeply admired the fair
sex, and, as Melbourne knows, had a distinct
preference for the Titian-red variety.
Working at a (Vic.) suburban stone-quarry
and crushing-yard are a baronet, a sth Wrangier
(Cambridge), the heir to an earldom and an ex-
major of Royal Artillery. The owner expresses
himself as “ ’aving a sort o’ likin’ for toffs.” He
“ allers gives ’em a show,” as he “ was a toff his-
self, oncet on a time, down Vitechapel way.”
Premier Seddon is alleged to be gradually
collapsing. Like Turner, of Victoria, Seddon is
“a terror for work,” and is Premier, Treasurer,
Minister for Works, ditto for Defence, and ditto
for Native Affairs all in one. As a result, “the
once bright countenance is growing haggard and
weary-looking, the bulky stature becomes more
shaky every day, and the frequent occasions on
which he is confined to bed are unmistakeable
signs of a physical break-down.” Seddon has
made no money out of politics, but then no reason-
ably honest man ever does.
Chief Justice Darley and new-Justice
Cohen seem to echo one another’s legal opinions on
the N.S.W. Bench. Simpson and Manning keep
well together in general views, and Stephen loves
to go alone.
Ballington Booth, the rebel son of his
father, is reported to have joined the Presbyterian
Church in Chicago. Why not revert to the ori-
ginal family business of pawnbroking? A pious
“ uncle,” who charged not more than 20 per shent,
would fill a long-felt want.
Pastor Fitchett is (said to be) annoyed
over the authorship of his Argus apotheoses of
slaughter, “Deeds that won the Empire,” tran-
spiring. Yet Stead has been cracking them up.
As Vincent Crummies says, “ It is surprising how
these things get into the papers ! ”
When Frederick Sargood bestrode a fiery
steed, at a recent review, a scurrilous rag essayed
to describe him as the Hero of the War Horse,
but, somehow, the phrase read Hero of the Ware-
house. _ Sargood is said to have been dissuaded
with difficulty from bringing the printer up before
the Bar of the ’Ouse.
King O’Malley, the windy Yank, who is
always “ hurling ” large masses of the dictionary
at his fellow-members, recalls Pat Coglin, who sat
in S.A. Assembly years ago, and once voted for
free newspaper postage because of “ the inex-
plicable delight with which people read these beau-
tiful dictatorial literary documents ! ”
Frank Cates had a famous grandmother,
Lady Charlotte Bacon—the “lanthe” to whom
Byron dedicated his “ Childe Harold.” She lived
to a great age, and visited these parts some years
ago. Melb. Govt. House was a haunt of Cates’ in
the Lochs’ days; he was connected with that
dynasty through his mother—a Villiers.
Mr. James Martin, ex-M.P. (N.S.W.),
bought at auction, at the recent sale, nearly all
the personal jewellery of Henry Parkes and his
first and second wives ; also, much of the presenta-
tion-plate, trowels, and mallets, and the big
Broken Hill silver spade, presented on the occa-
sion of turning the first sod of the B.H. water-
works.
The statement that Tynan was lying drunk
in bed, when the police burst into his room,
sounds very like a newspaper embellishment in-
tended to bring Oirish “patriotism” into disre-
pute. The Drunken Dynamiter and his Deluded
Devotees. A Boomster on the Burst. Tynan
takes care of Number One, &c. &c. Good head-
lines for the London hevenink papers.
All the obituaries of the late George
Verdon, C. 8., K.C.M.G., omitted mention of his
first job in Victoria, when he sold shirts in a small
outfitter’s shop in Williamstown, button-holing
the passing seamen in the genuine “ Vanter buy a
veskit ” fashion. After a time young Verdon
showed such business aptitude in seducing the
hardy salts into purchases of Crimean apparel that
he was offered a billet in a local shipchandler s,
which he filled with credit. The tidings of his
prowess in the realms of commerce soon travelled
to Melb., and Verdon ultimately became shipping-
clerk in Grice Summer’s, at which point the great
dailies take up his biography.
James Anderson Hartley, head of S.A.
Education Department, died in Adelaide If
of the results of a bicycle accident. Tireless,
brainy, enthusiastic —he was a born d'oniime. He
initiated in 1875 the present S.A. Public-schou
system and lived to see it reach the democratic
ideal—free, secular, and compulsory. He was for
ever proud of his victories over the Tory crowd lr
the early days, who, led by the Register, damnec
him and all his works. Was independent because
he was fairly wealthy, and in no fe£r of being
“unemployed”; there are many good stories
extant of his reception of meddlesome politicians
and ministers. Hartley died at the age of 52.
Henri Kowalski, the brilliant pianist and
composer, whose farewell concert occurs this
(Saturday) evening, is the fruit of two emigra-
tions—on the father’s side, Polish, on the mother s.
Irish. As both the nationalities which meet m
him are most musical, and possess a literature of
music more profoundly pathetic than any other
national music, except, perhaps, that of the negro—
to whom the future of music belongs, according ti-
the great Dvorak —it is not surprising that Kowal-
ski took to music as a duck to water. Young Kovt-
alski was a choir-boy in the Madeleine Chapel, and
sang there at the marriage of Napoleon and
Eugenie. He belonged to the opera chorus
between ’53 and ’57, and, at the same time,
studied in the Conservatoire, He began his
career as pianist and composer in Paris, and made
a great success with a little composition callec
“La Premevere.” Since then his compositions
are numberless, and one, the “ Marche Hongroise.
has had perhaps a larger sale than any other mus>-
cal composition published. The lucky publisher,
who bought the copyright for £2O, gave his
daughter a marriage dot of .£BOOO from the first
profits. The M.H. has been pirated in all countries,
and strummed on thousands of pianos by millions
of fingers to hundreds of millions of ears. Kowae
ski has composed operas, oratorios, piano music,
cantatas, concerts, orchestral pieces. With our
Marcus Clarke he did “Moustique,” a comic-
opera which very nearly hit the bull’s - eye
“ Moustique ” was first produced in Brus-
sels at the Royal Alcazar Theatre in 1883,
translated into French from Marcus Clarke's
libretto. Kowalski has the Chopin tradition
direct from the great but melancholy master's
pupils, Fontana and the Princess Marcelling
Czartoryska, and few players now alive can inte?
Sret the ethereal Polish pianist’s works like
iowalski. He has played all over the world, always
with greatsuccess, before courts and princes, and be-
fore history-makers like Bismarck (at Biarritz in ’67
He visited the United States with Madame Pate;,
Faure (baritone), Madame Carvallho, the creatrie*
of Marguerite in “ Faust,” and Naudin (tenor-
all great names. Played, too, with Hans von
Bulow, the son-in-law of Lizst and former
husband of Madame Wagner, who, Kowalski
relates, on producing one of Wagner’s com-
positions in Philadelphia, put on the programme,
“offered by the brother-in-law of the composer.”
After a tour of the United States with the
great Sarasate he returned to France during war-
time and was nominated to Leprowski’s con*
while the country was still in a state of defem -
HENRI KOWALSKI.
Photo, by Falk.
Under the auspices of M‘Mahon, Kowalski gav*
concerts in aid of the wounded and raised >£6oliL
He was decorated with orders, which he neve-
wears, but which would astonish the C.M.G.*
and make the K.C.M.G.’s green with envy. Was
the first Commissaire-General appointed in Francs,
with Victor Hugo and Louis Blanc, to the
French National Fete in 1879. Kowalski ha.-
played in Sydney at 170 charity concerts, anc
deserves to have his own benefit a bumper. He
has created a distinct school of pianoforte with a
direct apostolic succession from Chopin himseli.
His pupils are legion, and Europe is full of them.
Socially he is the soul of wit, enjoyment, and elo-
quence. Iks strongly resents being asked to play
in social gatherings ; the writer of this has found
it best to throw open the piano and let nature take
its course. This stratagem has never been known u>
fail. Kowalski was correspondent for Figaro..
Paris, during the Melbourne Exhibition, and is
author of a book, “A Travers I’Am erique,” which
had a good sale. Was conductor of the Sydney
Philharmonic and gave the “ Mary Magdelene
by Massenet for the first time, and the “ Spectra
Bride,” by Dvorak. He engaged Madame Melba
to sing in the “ Messiah ” in March, 1885, the only
time she sang in oratorio here.

RELIGIOUS INSTRUCTION' IN PUBLIC
, SCHOOIiS.
The Primate has addressed the following circu-
lar to the Anglican clergy on the subject of reli-
gious instruction in public schools:—“Rev. and
Dear Sits —The time has come round for me to
urge upon your attention and sympathy the work
of special religious instruction in public schools,
as carried cn by the committee appointed for that
purpose. The report presented to ilie' Synod at
the recent session, whilst indicating encouraging
features of the work, records a debit balance of
£T7 p>» Dd, and points to the offertory as an op-
portunity for the members of the Church at large
to contribute small sums. ' I therefore Invite you
and your churchwardens to give us, without fail,
the proceeds of at least one offertory, and I
would suggest Sunday, October 4th, or some other
Sunday in that month, as may be convenient to
you If a complete offertory cannot be spared,
.will you cause a special appeal'to be made, and
allow a definite proportion of .it,\ say one-half or
one-third, to be devoted to the work. Or, if you
prefer to do so, will you distribute amongst the
members of your congregation small envelopes,
duly endorsed, and request that offerings may be
made in them on any Sunday in' October. This
plan is suggested with a view of; minimising the
effect upon your regular offertory.—l am, etc..
Wrn. Sz., Sydney "

Helpless upon a Friendless Sea!
Who, in taking passage upon a great oceanic
steamer, does not feel a thrill of exultation over
her magnificent power. Against her the Storm
King may hurl his elemental forces, nor pierce her
armour, nor stop her onward course.
But let me describe a scene when, one morning
in mid-ocean, the came an alarm from the pilot
house followed by a cry : “ The ship’s rudder is
lost!” From the confident expression, con-
sternation came to every face. The wheelman
being helpless to direct her course, the vessel was
at the mercy of wind and wave.
The captain had been negligent—the hangings
of the rudder were allowed to wear weak, and
suddenly it had dropped deep into the sea !
Strong in intellect, in physical vigor, in energy
and in ambition, man confronts, undaunted, gi-
gantic tasks and commands applause for his mag-
nificent achievements. But, all unexpectedly, an
alarm comes —the rudder of his constitution is
gone. He has been careless of its preservation ;
mentalstrain, nervous excitement, irregular habits,
overwork, have destroyed the action of his kidneys
and liver. This would not occur were Warner’s
Safe Cure used to maintain vigour. And even
now it may restore vitality to those organs and
give back to the man that which will lead him to
the haven of his ambition. — The Traveller. #
WILLIAM FLEMING,
.-MERCHANT.-'

POLITICAL
POINTS
Last financial year the S.A. Govt, only got
£34,066 probate and succession duty, but this year
the Treasurer, after observing the age and physi-
cal condition of local capitalists, looks for £60,000.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne at Qui-
rindi : “I favor a land-tax, but would allow the
producer some profit to enable him to pay the
tax.” Lyne’s colleague, ex-Treas. See, at Yass :
“If I am a member of the next Ministry I will
repeal the land-tax.” Both utterances in the one
month !
Gov. Hampden’s refusal to attend the
Eight-Hours celebration at Rosehill arose, no
doubt, from his misunderstanding the significance
of the,onting ; but until the wurrkin-man learns to
enjoy his own special holiday in a workmanlike
way, without the meaningless patronage of Govt.-
’ouse, he will be always in danger of being similarly
misunderstood.
Premier Reid alleges that lie
was perfectly justified, on Freetrade
principles, in giving the famous Gun-
dagai bridge contract to a N.S.W.
contractor at a higher rate because he
was a local man, and thus making the
province pay for assisting local in-
dustry. “Freetrade,” says G.H.R.,
“ means free choice. We had a free «
choice in the case under notice, and we
exercised it.” Then, supposing the
N.S.W. Government freely chose to
shut out foreign imports altogether ?
Freetrade means free choice, and free
choice practically means liberty to do
anything. On G. H. Reid’s version
of the matter, a 500 per cent, tariff,
imposed by the free choice of G. H.
Reid, would be Freetrade. That is
just it: anything done by a party
that calls itself Freetrade, and lives
on alleged Freetrade, is Freetrade.
‘‘ Turncoat Bill ” Copley has not pleased
S.A. Oppositionists. This and another circum-
stance accounts for the rumor that John Downer,
whom he deposed, will succeed him. The task of
leading the assorted crowd composing S.A. Oppo-
sition is a thankless ope, anyway.
Montmartre :—No wonder the authorities
want to get rid of the Yarra-bankers. The man
still holds forth there who, at the red-flag Sunday-
afternoon pitch, advocated hurling Gov. Hope-
toun into the river. On the same occasion he said,
“ I hear glorious news, friends—the death of that
dog Maeßain.”
N.S.W. National Ass brays wildly to Leg.
Bouncillors to kill or emasculate the Factories
Bill. China, Japan, and N.S.W. are now about
the only manufacturing countries without Factory
Acts, and if N.S. Wales isn’t quick about it she
will be left alone with China, as J apan is hope-
fully considering the matter.
As there seems to be an Irish conspiracy
and dynamite atrocity boom now starting, non-
bigoted and impartial Australians should insist on
being supplied with a better press cable-service
than that which their daily newspapers provide.
The press cable-business is now practically a
monopoly, the news-items all filtering through one
channel; and what that means is obvious.
Dalrymple, Minister for Public Instruc-
tion, maintained vehemently in Bananaland As-
sembly the other day that all attempts to fix
wages by legislation were utterly futile and
impossible. Whereon Dunsford, M.L.A., ejacu-
lated : “ Don’t you fix them in your department ? ”
To which Hoolan added : “ And didn’t we fix our
own?” Whereat the gentlemen who had just
assessed their own value at £3OO per annum
laughed heartily.
Queensland has passed its own particular
kind of Federation Bill, and though it isn t very
like that of the other provinces, still it brings
Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria, S.A., and Tas-
mania into a sort of staggery line. Its goose-
step is woefully out of perspective, it is true, but
still it professes to be a line, and it is more like
one than a rainbow. As Westralia, apparently,
isn’t going to move, the other five provinces will
probably go on without her, and 'the election of
convention delegates is talked of for Nov. or Feb.
or thereabouts.
After all, it will be desperately hard luck
if there isn’t another N.S.W. surplus next year.
G. Reid has an artful dodge up his sleeve. This
year the 12 months’ land and : income taxes are to
be collected in one instalment. Next year they
are to be levied in two instalments, and the first
one will be collected before June, so that 18
months’ land and income taxes will appear in one
year's revenue. With 18 months’ tax in one year
and some of the diminishing duties still in force—
the last of them doesn’t cease to yield revenue till
1900 —there should be hope.
In answer to a question in Parliament,
Vic. Attorney-General Isaacs said that Crown
briefs were allotted to “ poor but competent bar-
risters.” It seems to strain such a category to
include in it a Queenslander whose practice has
been such as to have caused him once to have
refused a judgeship.
Pressure is being applied to Jacob Gar-
rard, N.S.W. Education Minister, to make him
induce the Premier to begin Leg. Assembly busi-
ness with prayer. Prayer has maintained its place
in the Commons, because members in time for
prayer have a choice of seats. The holy blather-
skite who proposes prayer for N.S.W. Assembly
will have to tack on some similar advantage.
The N.S.W. Old-age Pension Committee
reckons the number of necessitous people over 60
and qualified by 15 years’ residence in the pro
vince at about 4COO. Kirton, M.L.A., who has
taken up the question in Victoria, reckons there
are in that province 15,000 people above 60 in poor
circumstances. If the figures are much the same,
which seems probable, in N.S.W., it appears a
large order to assume that only 4000 will establish
claims on the pension-fund.
Bathurst (N.S.W.) has started a shriek on
behalf of its claims to be the Federal capital.
Bathurst has evidently heard that the Federal
city ought to be “ central,” and being more or less
central, so far as N.S.W. is concerned, it reckons
it fills the bill. There are now three N.S.W.
centres —Sydney, Albury and Bathurst —setting
up as the Federal metropolis, and there are
rumors of Goulburn chipping in, and not one of
them seems to consider that any other province
can possibly be in the running.

Iminigration logic from Bananaland As-
sembly : “ Every new colonist contributes £6 16s.
4d. in taxes to public revenue, and is governed at
a cost of £4 15s. 10d., leaving a balance of £2 os.
6d. Wherefore 10,000 persons immigrating mean
an increase in revenue balance of £20,000.”
Wherefore also the introduction of 1,000,000 pau-
pers, drunks, Chows, Hindoos, signifies also
£20,000,000 in the colonial treasuries, and peace
and prosperity for ever and ever—Amen !
Beni. Hoare, an Age leader-writer, has
raised the ire of Max Hirseh by publishing a Pro-
tectionist leaflet entitled “ A German Invasion.”
Max says he is libelled, though not mentioned by
name. The concluding sentence :
The prop of all national life is industry. The man who
will imperil local Industry in order to promote foreign
Trade is always either fatuous or false ; and when he
employs German gold to desolate Australian hearths and
homes, he is a creature which the national stomach
should expel as vomit.

Vic. Government as a sweater. It lias
accepted a tender for cutting a track from Clark -
ville toward Bendoc at A 5 3s. 4d. a mile. The
departmental estimate was Al 3 a mile for a nine-
mile track, i.e., =£ll7, on the basis that it would
take three men 17 weeks to do the job, or £2 6s.
a week per man. But, at the accepted £5 3s. 4d.
per mile, for 17 weeks’ work each man would get
,£l5 10s. as his share on the buttygang basis. And
yet politicians aver that there is no need for a
legislated minimum wage.
Victoria badly needs to adopt the Maori-
land system of buying up large estates and cutting
them into small aveus for farming settlement, either
on the leasehold principle, or as freeholds with a
law limiting the amount of land to be held by
one owner, so that the small areas won’t be
swallowed up to make more big estates. The
big squatting estates matter comparatively little
as yet in N.S.W. and Queensland where popula-
tion is thin, but the Cabbage-Garden has nearly
four times as many people to the square mile as
N.S.W., and 22 times as many as Queensland, and
already her people are beginning to slop over into
the adjacent provinces from sheer want of avail-
able soil. The enormous prices paid for agricul-
tural land in Victoria prove that the land-famine
has already set in. North-western Vic. is badly
watered ; the east is mostly a mass of mountain
ranges difficult of access, and the big landholder
occupies altogether too much of the fertile Central
district, and blocks progress.
The latest theory is
that Sultan Abdul-Hamid is
a homicidal maniac, and that
the Armenian atrocities and
other horrors are a natural
result of his malady. The
House of Othman has been
pretty bad in its head for a
long time. The last Sultan,
Murad, fell into idiocy and
disappeared—no one really
knows whether he is dead or
alive. Abdul-Medjid, of the
Crimean War period, was a
mild and cranky melancholica
with a strain of weak genius
in him somewhere, and a
feeble desire to die and be
a whitewashed saint in a
better land. Before him a
long procession of casual
Selims and Solymans were
more or less demented, right
back to the days of Amurath
the Terrible, who was a
mixture of the hero and the
melodramatic butcher, and
Selim the Sot, who was a
fat, hilarious lunatic of the
public-house variety. Bar-
ring its one great genius,
Mahmoud 11., the Othmans
can scarcely be said to have
been quite right in the head
for two centuries.

If the Vie. One-Adult-One-Vote Bil 1
becomes law it will probably —even though it only
applies to the Assembly —make things more or less
sultry for the Upper House. It will necessarily
lead to the return of a somewhat different

48 Rich Landlords to a finish is a great and
beautiful possibility.

If the cable reports are to be relied upon,
the Maoriland Old-Age Pension scheme seems to
have gone under for the present, and the Seddon
Govt, has been considerably defeated by its own
party. It wasn’t in any sense a Tory victory.
The Democratic party wanted to extend the
pension system even further than the Government
pro posed, and the Govt, approved of the idea in
the abstract, but asked its supporters to go slow in
view of the money-liability involved. The House
carried its point, however, whereupon the Ministry
seems to have told it to run the business itself and
gone out to gnash its teeth in the lobby. This
lamentable ruction has probably wrecked, for the
present session, about the greatest and best idea
on the Govt, programme.
In a leader, in its issue of September 19,
Sydney Telegraph broke even its fine record as a
garbler of statistics. It sets forth that, from June
>94, to June, ’95, N.S.W. gained 31,710 of popula’
tionj, and from the latter date to June, ’96, the
population increased by 21,620 ; total increase for
two years, 53,330. This is the biggest increase for
the two years among the Australasian provinces,
and the Telegraph remarks joyfully that “the
period, it will be observed, is that in which the
Freetrade policy has been established in this pro-
vince.” The Freetrade policy, as everybody
knows, only began to be established in the latter
part of ’95. The first year quoted was a year of
Protection, and then the population increased
10,000 more than in the second, or Freetrade year.
IN THE SAME BOAT.
SPINSTER (to unemployed, who wonts “somethin’ to cat”) : But you
don’t look too old to get work.”
UNEMPLOYED : “ An’ you don’t look too old to get married—hut ’taint
what WE thinks as matters.”
In fact, the shoddy organ annexes the splendid
population results shown under Protection, and
credits them to the opposite policy, and then
whoops.
Caldwell, of S.A. Assembly, wants a tax on
bicycles. The bike-tax is a valuable source of
revenue in France, and it seems a promising sub-
ject to put a duty on. One of The Bulletin’s
favorite financial theories is that the proceeds of
certain taxes should be set apart for the construc-
tion of railways and other public works, so that
/they won’t be dependent either on borrowed
money or on the uncertain and unreliable surplus,
and with this end in view it proposes some day to
write an article on “Neglected Sources of
Revenue.” A few items are: an annual tax on
bicycles, racehorses, jockeys, bookmakers, pianos,
patent medicines, carriages, buildings over a cer-
tain height, percentage on the “ tote,” ditto on
Tattersall’s sweeps (which could be legalised on
Tatt. paying over a reasonable share of the profits
as the price of his monopoly), unclaimed deposits
and dividends, tax on lawyers, foreign bagmen,
foreign fire insurance companies, foreign proprie-
tary life ditto, foreign banks, and on absentees,
proceeds of State salt monopoly, &c., &c.


The very mist which rises in the morning
from Parsons’ famous Flaked Oatmeal is superior
to that of the ordinary brands of breakfast stuff,
and it is doubtless in part the public appreciation
of this fine food which has moved the firm to
occupy large new premises at 321-7 Kent-st.,
Sydney, with six floors available for the rnanu.
facture of Parsons’ specialties. Here are the jam-
boiling room, with a capacity of 10 tons per day ;
the tin-making shop ; the fruit-cooling chamber;
the apparatus and space for making sauce and
pickles and cordial and cocoa, and all the other
necessaries and luxuries which the great mouth of
the public yearns for—when made by Parsons.
Hordern’s is an institution, but Parsons builds
the constitution.*

In N.S.W., where a special effort is being
made to put down the legitimate pub.—which
pays a license fee, and has to sell decent liquor,
and must keep order, and close at certain fixed
hours, and preserve some semblance of cleanliness
you may still raise Cain and snakes at all hours
of the day or night, and seven days a week, or
eight if you can find them, simpiy by calling
yourself a “club.’ A club pays no license; it
hasn’t to provide eating or sleeping accommoda-
tion ; it may sell any ki rid of liquor it pleases at
any hour it pleases ; it may burst the Day of Rest
into as many pieces as it likes; it isn’t police-
inspected—the members may habitually sit around
with nothing on if they want to ; in fact, there are
very few things that a club may not do. And yet
the public ignorance is so great that something
like a fourth of the male community spends its
Sundays trying to get in at the back door of the
hotel to buy some sweet-oil for a horse.
The humorlessness of dogma. The Search
Light, Melb.’s new church organ, describes itself
on its own posters as “ the most suggestive paper
in Australia.”
Pew : “ Why is the preaching of the Gos-
pel of Christ not worthy to engross a man’s life,
even as law, physics, journalism, or politics ?
Pastor Pitchett must surely give the cream of his
work to editing the Review oe Reviews ; also
Southern Cross, and compiling the ‘ Deeds that
Won the Empire,’besides being president of the
Wesleyan Ladies’ College. Yet he is in the job of
converting the world and saving souls. Is this
only worthy the fag-end of a man’s mind ? Can
he just throw in a little bit of Jesus Christ as a
side-show ? To be sure, a politician thinks he can
govern a nation with the remains of a mind im-
mersed in his own twopenny-halfpenny business.
Even he can be excused, rather than a divine.”

At Poverty Point.
with no artist is the profession so much wrapped up in
the whole life as with the musician. Music is to its aver-
age devotee something more than a religion—almost a
fanaticism. Witness the deathbed wish of Madam Fanny
Simon sen, when her one desire was that a copy of the
Faust score might be buried with her. Such an expression
would appear ludicrous in a painter or a writer.
Theorbo : “ Fannie Simonsen was a bitterly-disappointed
woman, her only bright spot the triumph of her charming
daughter, Frances Saville. When Madame Simonsen
came to Australia she was an artless, simple creature, and
slowly developed into the business-woman. One thing
that stuck to her through life was a contempt for dress
and extravagance. She ought to have died wealthy, but
dogged ill-luck pursued her. At first she was only the
Madame Musin, to the fiddling of her husband, but Lyster
diagnosed her powers. He played her off against Lucy
Escott. What a furore over the rival casts of ‘ L’Afri-
caine,’ when Simonsen and Beaumont palpably beat
Escott and Squires ! Beaumont had then immense public
sympathy, through having only just lost his eye, in the
shooting-accident, at the hands of Lyster’s brother, not
Fred. It was quite immense for Fanny to create the
Grand Duchess as she did, without Beaumont and Farley,
the unapproachable Bourn. Madame’s business powers
were evinced when she ransacked Milan, Rome, Venice,
Naples, Florence, Turin, for singers, and fetched her A 1
Italian Opera Co But the game was dead. Even Wil-
liamson and Musgrove’s fine quintette, with such blazing
novelties as Pagliacci and Cavalleria Rusticana, did not
answer too well. The worst frost of the Simonsens was
over the ghastly Spanish Students, for whom, however,
Madame was in no way responsible. The drudgery of
teaching was hateful to her. After retiring from the
boards she flashed for a night as Valentine, in “The
Huguenots,” through Madame Ciuti’s illness. Madame
Simonsen had never acted it before, took it at a moment’s
notice, and quite triumphed.”
The tale goes that George Musgrove attended the
Lohmann benefit performance of “ Othello,” at Covent
Garden Theatre, expecting to find amateurs only on the
job. lago struck him as being a phenomenally fine
amateur until he looked at his programme and discovered
that veteran Hermann Vezin was playing the part of the
ancient. Mussy, strange to say, had never before seen
Vezin.
Miss Mabelle Davidson, the amateur Girofle-Girofla at
Melb. Bijou, is a Bendigo girl who made her debut as a
principal in the Lyric Club performance of “ Marjorie ”
two or three years ago. Miss Mabelle on that occasion
played Cicely in smart, taking style, with rather more
assurance than the average pro., and her success under
the circumstances was remarkable. The damsel is anxious
to take to the stage, and the Firm will possibly cast its
eye in her direction.
Many people who knew Frank Cates merely as a some-
what rigid juvenile in comedy are quite unaware that he
was a splendid mimic—in fact, a better mimic than
juvenile.
Haresfoot: “ Cates was plunged in the financial bog
these 15 years. Couldn't walk the Block without ‘ Er—
Mr. Katz—jutht one word.’ ”
Amusing to notice that Melb. Age duly reported the
Saturday evening performance of “Trip to Chinatown,”
at which a flashlight photo, of the auditorium was taken
from the stage, without a word of reference to this special
incident. If the photo, had been taken for the wearisome
Leader instead of the Australasian, then would David
Syme’s daily organ have printed half-a-column concern-
ing the little mutual admiration trick.
Next time that a first-class performance of a clever and
expensively-produced comedy (sans music-hall songs) is
happening at Melb. Princess’s, why not take a flashlight
photo, of the half-empty benches, and hang it beside the
painful proof of public soft-headedness recorded in the
“ Chinatown ” picture ?

The audience is most tickled by the flick of the drayman’s

Rignold has not been suggested as a possible candidate
for Svengarlic honors—nathless his Paolo Macari has
many points in common with the unwashed Hebrew.
The foreign accent, the mysterious influences exerted
over the crazy Pauline, and his tragic end.
Melb. managers begin to find that they need no hired
claque when Chief Sec. Peacock visits the theatre. The
other evening, at the Princess’, the politician broke out
into such fiendish cachinnation at Conor’s reference to
Duffy and high-kicking telephone girls, that hysterics
seemed imminent. Manager Mitchell afterwards offered
him a box for the first night of his new burlesque, with
the idea, probably, of ensuring a successful start. The
next thing will be (in big type) “The laughing Peacock,
to-night, by special arrangement.”
Geo. Coppin’s visit to Adelaide recalls the fact that he
built the first Adelaide theatre in 1848, and himself took a
hand with the spade in digging foundations, afterwards
acting with G. V. Brooke. Subsequently Coppin made a
fortune out of the Exchange Hotel, Adelaide (still stand-
ing, by the way), but dropped it in mining.
Veteran George Coppin, visiting Adelaide with the
Maggie Moore Co., struck an old friend in Ebenezer Ward,
M.L.C. Ward was with Coppin (when the latter starred
with G. V. Brooke) about 40 years ago, and left him to
embrace journalism and politics in Adelaide. “ Ebby ”is
now one of the leading windbags of the Stomach-Party,
and is a greater actor in politics than ever he was on the
stage.
The puzzled patron of “ A Trip to Chinatown” is the
Chinaman.
A new ad. is perforce a stroke of genius. Harry Conor
notifies, by shop-window placards, that he buys his (what-
ever it is) there. As many hands for gloves as Briareus.
It has not taken Ada Crossley long to work herself into
the first flight of oratorio singers in England. She is to
appear with Ella Russell, Edward Lloyd, and Santley in
the “Elijah,” selected for interpretation by Liverpool
Philharmonic Society.
“ Tommy ” (T. Mandeno) Jackson, well to the front as a
singer in London, is a Maorilander, and a son of old Sam
Jackson, who for many years had a snug legal business in
Auckland. Tom had arrived at man’s estate before it was
recognised that he had a voice worth cultivating—indeed,
he had begun business as an auctioneer, and it is said
that the mellifluosity of his “ going—going—gone ! ”
struck the delicate ear of Henry Brett, and led to the
giving of advice which altered the young man’s career.
Jackson is a good-looking splendidly-built fellow.
Engineering-Demonstrator Mann, of Melb. Univ., is
being inundated with orders for cinematographe motors
and hew plates from pros, who intend touring with them
through Australasia.

Shipped by the Light of the Moon,” originally played
by the Girards in America, seems to be quite new to
Hingland, although G. R. Sims wrote it. Geo. Walton is
taking “ Skipped ” on a provincial tour, and Sims’ own
particular organ, the Referee, rises to explain that “this
play is described as an electric farce, with music.” How
little they know of Australian theatrical doings in the
little island ! Not long ago the Referee spoke of Nellie
Stewart as “ a young actress with considerable knowledge
of stage business.”

The father of millionaire Winans, now the deer-forester
and crofter-expeller of Scotland, was a Philadelphia iron-
master, and made his pile constructing the St. Peters-
burg-Moscow railway for Czar Nicholas, who settled the
engineering disputes by drawing a straight line on the
map and giving Winans a free fist. The existing Winans
is the princely benefactor of the Alhambra ladies, under
the rule of Melbourner Moul. His father, conspicuous in
a red shirt, superintended the disembarkation of the
railway material at St. Petersburg-.
Libbey, of “ Chinatown,” has a music-publishing busi-
new in New York, and carries a letter (he will show it to
you on the slightest provocation) from the composer of
“ After the Ball,” in which he is mentioned as the man to
whom all credit is due for its success—a testimonial that
fits the ponderous humorist all over.

thing for life ! ”

Rochefoucauld says that ‘ ‘ we never attain
the objects of our ambition, except at times and
under circumstances which render them of no
avail.” This must have been the bitter feeling of
the residents of Pentridge Gaol, who were all in
bed and asleep at the time that the walls collapsed,
and left an opening 300 yards wide, with nobody
to take advantage of the occasion.
A Judge in the witness-box is like a doctor
ill, when he has to call in another. Turveydrop
J udge Redmond Barry appeared once in the box
at Melb. Supreme Court, and was ridiculously
nervous, totally failing in the speak-up he en-
joined on others. Same with beak Call, in similar
circumstances, whereat Bobbydom rejoiced, be-
cause he was peculiarly offensive in his speak-up.
Melbourne ’Erald had a really great burst
of unconscious humor, the other day, touching the
slaughter of Armenians. It commenced with—
“ The Turk. Shall we endure him ? ” and ended
as follows : —“ If Australia is to voice her indigna-
tion there must be a beginning somewhere. Shall
it not be in the Queen City of the South ? Let
Mayor Strong consider and answer ! ” The voice
of the writer, as he called upon poor old Strong to
“ consider and answer ! ” must have struck terror
to his own soul.
The case of a woman, charged at Fitzroy
(Melb.) with stealing goods from Eoyand Gibson’s
store, doesn’t seem calculated to soften the quality
of mercy in storekeepers. Early one Saturday
evening this female was caught stealing a pair of
boots. She pleaded hard for leniency, also she
was “respectably dressed,” so a member of the
-firm had pity and let her go. Later on, she ex-
pressed her gratitude by stealing some more boots
—three pairs this time. Given into custody, the
hardened offender got off with 14 days.
Ed. Bulletin, —Theology in the back-
blocks. When the Salvation Army came out to
Bowen Downs this shearing the Captain was
advised to go to the nap.-school to “ try her luck
for a few bob. ” She asked one man for money as
he looked at his cards, and he said, “Dwell
the peter ! I’ve got a nibble for two and if I get
’em I’ll give you a boxer.” Another member of
the “school” informed her that she was “a
Jonah.” She seemed rather flabbergasted at these
sayings, but they couldn’t choke her off. A wool-
shed is a great place for subscriptions. The
general opinion is that good wages are earned at
shearing, but the long waits for sheds and ex-
penses for travelling are never considered.—Shell-
back.
Some bones with a history attached were
dropped across the other day at Nowra (N.S.W.).
Nowra, in 1866, was a thinly-peopled spot on the
outer rim of nowhere in particular, and the person
who stepped out of it into the adjacent hills stood
a fair chance of being lost. A girl named Emily
Bourne stepped out and was lost so utterly that
no trace of her could be discovered. Then after
30 years, civilization overtook her bones, or some
bones that were believed to be hers, and the other
day the people who ought to have been her descen-
dants gave her decent burial. Possibly in another
three or four or five centuries civilization in Aus-
tralia will have overtaken the last of the accumu-
lated arrears of bones that lie ahead of it, and this
will cease to be the Great Disappearance Land.
Meanwhile, however, pursuit is slow and the
bones keep well ahead.
Opposition-leader Lyne and Premier Reid,
bobbing heads at one another across the social
table, and assuring a miscellaneous audience that
although they differ in politics they regard each
otherpersonallyas high-intentioned, noble-hearted,
white-conscienced patriots, provoke the vulgar to
exclaim, “What amiable humbugs!” Do poli-
ticians take note of the atmosphere in which they
live ? They are met like rivals in business-like
rivals in law or in divinity. They are—the Aus-
tralian brand, anyway—almost without exception,
traducers and defamers of one another’s intentions.
Opinions may differ about acts, and do so rightly;
but the air of Parliament is thick with the slander
of motives and the imputation of conscious crime.
When, then, leaders of these blatant jousts oleo-
margarine one another out of doors, the oily stuff
smells like condemned butcher’s beef on its way to
burial in Moore Park.
SPORTING NOTIONS .
“Monico” to The Bulletin from London :
“The rubber match was won on the first day when, in an
hour and a half’s play, the Englishmen knocked up 69 for
Grace’s wicket. The batsmen had an easy pitch, the
bowlers a slippery turf and a greasy ball, so what might
be expected happened. On the second day 24 wickets fell
for an average of 16J runs, and on the last 15 for 4i runs
apiece. England 84, Australia 44, fairly represent the
strength of the two teams on a sticky wicket when the
visitors are trying to play correct cricket—as they did on
this occasion. You might as well play for the ‘ spot-
stroke ’ at French billiards.
“ There was but one way to get runs on such a
wicket. The ball must be met, as it pitched, sharp and
hard with the full force of the bat, before it had time to
break and shoot off. Those who tried this —Trott,
M’Kibbin, and Trumble—showed a semblance of scoring.
A couple of bats of the Graham or Stoddart type might
have knocked up the required total in an hour or so. But
the Victorian is out of form, and the ‘weekly average
list ’ has killed whatever dash any of the others had.
“ Griffon was the failure of the match. His two
wickets cost 64 runs in an innings of 145. At his first try
he was in half-an-hour for a ‘duck’—and Clem Hill’s
wicket. He dropped Peel from a ‘ spoony ’ catch, fielded
otherwise sciatically, and in the second innings left the
wickets as soon as he could, decently or indecently, get
out. The best thing he did was to catch Abel one-
handed, and this he discounted by a playful pretence to
immediately throw down the other batsman’s wicket. It
was just such an emanation of misplaced waggishness on
his part that cost Hill’s wicket and killed all hope of a
fairly even first innings score.
“ The Oval on the second day might have been
likened to an immense signet-ring, of which the thou-
sands of ground spectators formed the circlet and the
shabby old pavilion the much-worn oblong centre-stone
set between the thickness of the two shelter sheds. ‘ All
the Australians in London were there,’ and many men
known to Australia, i.e., ex-Gov. Jersey, George Anson,
and George Musgrove, Alf. Plumpton, in a straw hat
with colors and badge ; Melb. Exhibition Lavater, and
doctors, prize-fighters, miners, journalists, pensioners,
brokers. When, Jon the third day, five English wickets
fell for 24, leaving 111 between Trott’s team and victory,
those who had witnessed in anguish the 18 and 53 feebly-
earned totals at Lords’ knew the case to be hopeless.
Grace opened with Richardson to Iredale, but on a dead
wicket Tom’s deliveries lacked ‘ whip,’ and were phiyed
with such confidence as to almost rouse hope in some
breasts. Wily W.G. saw the fast bowling was useless, off
went the Surrey ‘pro.,’ on went Peel, and down went
Australia’s hopes and wickets : I—o,1 —0, 2 —3, 3 —7, 4 —7
6—ll, 6—ll, 7—14, B—l 9, 9—35, 10—44 !
“ Immediately Abel ended the match by catch-
ing M‘Kibbin, short slip, the yelling crowd burst across
the ground from either side converging on the pavilion-
gate where the police had a tough job to keep a lane for
the players to enter through. And there the thousands
stood for an hour cheering England, Australia, individual
players, but omitting to do justice to the real winner of
the rubber-match for Grace and Co.—i.e., Jupiter Pluvius.
“After an important match the crowd swarm
the ground and call for the more popular men until they
appear, acknowledge the salutations, and retire. They
patter for Abel thus : ‘ Bob—Bob—Bob—Bob-Bob,’ to at
least the amount of a ‘ tenner ’ in five minutes. The first
Australian called for was ‘ Trum-bull, Trum-bull,* and
he with ‘Trott — Trott — Trott,’ ad lib., was received with
great cheering. The position of control has made Trott,
and he has made a good captain. His handling of his
bowling and placing of his field in this match were widely
and highly spoken of. Popular with the cricket crowds,
he does his best to encourage his bats to get the value of
their wickets, and he himself got out to a brilliant catch
of Brockwell’s when trying to force and save the game.
“A rope only divides the playing-grounds in
England from the surrounding spectators, who, at the
interval, saunter on to the turf and indulge in an al
fresco lunch. On August bank-holidav the results of this
picnicking strewed the Oval with bottles and lunch-
wrappers, and delayed the resumption of the Surrey-
Yorkshire match.
“Kelly, as a wicket-keeper, is a 50 per cent,
improved man, and Johns, who gets little chance, cannot
hope to excel him. Witness the N.S.W. man’s one bye
(a leg, too) in a score of 145 as against Lilley’s 9 sundries
in 119. As a bat at a pinch he is dogged, though clumsy,
and behind the ‘ sticks ’ as keen as a fox-terrier with the
pluck of a Blackham, to whom many English cricketers
now. conipare. him —but they forget. His stumping of
Ranjitsinghi in the match just over was worthy of the
prince of wicket-keepers himself.
“The‘pros.’ having proved they cannot be
done without, and drawn public attention to the well-
paid ‘amateur-pro.,’ iqeekly said they ‘ were sorry and
would never be naughty again,’ and received forgiveness.
Lohmann’s apology came later, but was more humbly
penitent. Gunn has not yet ‘ eaten the leek,’ and is not
likely to.”
* » * *
“ The third test-match, ” writes an Australian
abroad to Tiie Bulletin, “proved the inability of the
ninth Australian Eleven to play on a bad wicket. The
stand made by Darling and Iredale in their first innings
was as unexpected as brilliant. How that pair ever made
up their minds to try and get runs and chance their
wickets will always be a source of mystery. They had
never done such a thing before, and in their second ven-
ture never attempted it.
“ Trumble’s bowling throughout the third test-
match was greatly admired by experts. He kept a
splendid length throughout, and, with the sticky wicket
to help him, made the ball do marvels. Though his
average, 12 wickets for 89, is not quite as good as either
Hearne’s or Peel’s, it must be remembered that the Vic.
‘ six-foot length ’ had to take his turn on the soft, easy
wicket of the Monday evening, and that throughout he
was bowling against men who had far more experience of
sticky wickets than the Australians had.
“ During the Canterbury cricket-week, of which
the Australian match occupied the last three days, a
historically ancient custom is kept up by giving on two
evenings theatrical performances. These are done by
well-known local amateurs, who belong to a society called
from time immemorial, “ The Old Stagers,” though, as
just now they are all very young men, and as Irene Van-
brugh (who was in Australia with Toole) was leading lady,
the title has lost its significance. On the second night, a
specially-written epilogue was gone through—poorly
written and of slight interest till suddenly came these
words, or something like them, given with tremendous
effect:
‘ But what is this that we to-night hail here ?
Why, ’tis the famous Team from far Australia.’
Then, round the O.P. corner, Trott’s head shyly appeared,
and it was evident that there was some difficulty in getting
him to take his cue. Simultaneously, however, he and
Harry Musgrove, looking very unhappy, were shot on, and
two or three more struggled after them. It was a sight
for the gods, but the appearances on the stage of this very
worthy team should be reserved for their friends only.
They are too much for public enthusiasm to stand.
“Iredale ran himself out in the most foolish
manner after his excellent first innings in the test-match
at the Oval. He and Darling had run four for a bye, and,
with a silly desire to make the fifth he started on a
hopeless attempt. Giffen in the same innings ran out
young Clem. Hill by calling a second run that was not
probable, though perhaps just possible. This team has
on more than one occasion destroyed big chances by
faulty running between wickets.
“Clem Hill is called ‘Kruger,’ because of an
alleged likeness. Manager Musgrove is boiled right down
to'Ger’—soft'g.’
“ In considering the conduct of the professionals
in re the third test-match, it must be remembered that
as far back as June after the first of the series they asked
for the extra £lO, but were refused. That at Manchester
they asked for it before the match, and were told that the
matter would be considered, but had since heard nothing.
The Surrey executive knew all about these applica-
tions, for they had carpeted Richardson over the matter,
and told him that he ought to be proud to play ‘ for the
honor of it,’ and Richardson had replied that he had
discovered honor would not keep his wife and children,
and the applications for an increase were sent in as
soon as the invitations to play were received.”
• * * *
“An Australian Traveller” writes from Lon-
don:—“Dear Bulletin, —Do not speak crossly to the
Eleven because of the third test-match. They were really
not so much to blame. Says London Sun, 12th Aug.: —
‘ The Australians took their gruel like men. As a team
they are a great team, but on a bad wicket could never
hope to beat the chosen of this country. They fought
with stubbornness and determination to the very end,
and the memory of their game struggle will linger with
us for years. In fielding and bowling they were our
equals ; in batting, on such a wicket, our inferiors.'
“I saw it all, and the game was won on the
toss. Grace and Jackson put on 69 runs the first day on
a slow, but easy, wicket. And those 69 were the 66 by
which England won. ‘No man,’ said Murdoch, whose
soul was sad, to me, ‘ no man will make runs against J. T.
Hearne and Peel on that wicket. And no team in the
world would make 100.’
“ I never saw men so disappointed. Jones was
the only man who took his turn with a smile. “ I’ll be
bowled,” he said when he went to face the music, “ but
I’m past participled if I’m bowled blocking.” Hearne
and Peel bowled a fair record, but nothing can ever lessen
the memory of Trumble’s display with the ball, and
M'Kibbin’s with both bat and ball.
“ After the wicket began to dry—that is, when
play was resumed on Tuesday morning—England made in
all 160 runs for 19 wickets, and Australia 163 for 20.
Wherefore, I say, the 69 runs got when the wicket was
easy won the match. Grace’s smile could be heard for
hours afterwards. You’ll be glad to notice that the
worms—professional worms —have turned. Certainly
they are trying to wriggle back, but they’ve had their
portraits taken meanwhile, and their position will pro-
bably be fully discussed now.
‘ ‘ Australians are abused for taking their share
of the gate—so per cent, of the ground-money and none
of the stand. Do tell me, am I right in saying that Eng-
lish teams in Australia get 75 per cent, and a shave of the
stand ? ”
# * * *
No bowler ever did better than did Turner in
22 Bathurst v. English Eleven, 1881. Going on when
three English wickets had fallen in the first innings, he
bagged the remaining 7, and in the Britishers’ second
innings took all 10 ; 17 consecutive wickets.
* * * *
In the three test-matches C. Hill totalled only
30 runs ; 5 runs per innings.
* * * *
Blackham’s testimonial deserves a response all
over Australasia. David Syme starts off liberally, as a
souvenir of Blackham senior, so long Age overseer.
Cricket is a poor game, as crutch instead of staff, without
a Govt, billet, like Giffen or “ Felix” Horan.
* » * *
The Australian Cricket Annual is to far too
great an extent a compilation of stale news and common-
place remarks. For although good cricketers contribute
articles, these articles only show that a good cricketer can
be an execrable writer. Mr. Garrett, in his weak intro-
duction, hashes up facts which are trite to every school-
boy. The article on “The Australian Season” is a
boarding-school girl’s scream : “ Sadness that sends every
muscle in one’s body quivering and eyes welling with
tears in the hour of some great defeat.” “ Eyes welling
w'ith tears ” ! Is the Australian cricketer a pious little
miss who has been forcefully kissed by a bold, bad man ?
He is typically a thick-hided, lumpy-headed individual,
who, when he gets a big licking, damns his own bad and
the other side’s good luck, curses the eyes and tongues of
the blanky umpires, drinks several hard drinks, ana vows
to get level in the return-match. Another perfervid
shriek (about bygone matches) : “ What abiding ravishing
reminiscences are entwined.” This man evidently
rooms ” with a cricket-bat. More goosey-gander :
“ Rueful, tearful eyes to see the sunshine shadowed by a
sudden rise of thick clouds upon the horizon.” It is an
old maid talking to her curate.
♦ * * ♦
W. It. Wilson’s lot for the Yic. Spring meetings
grows less dangerous in appearance. Nada’s mishaps
have discounted her Flemington prospects, and Strath-
spey and Bedford were last week scratched for the two
Cups. Strathspey was rather a favorite whisper among
,men-in-the-street.
* • * *
The defunct Malua was an odd-made horse in-
asmuch as his hind-quarters didn’t seem natural to the
rest of him. The same peculiarity has been noticeable in
some of his get—Malolo, for instance.
Although Malua won a Melbourne and an Aus-
tralian Cup (2J mile) he never proved himself a stayer of
the Carbine stamp. Carbine, as everybody knows,
carried his 10stj 51b. lengths in front of the Melb. Cup
field for the last three furlongs or so. Malua couldn’t
have done that, but, all the same, Inglis’s champion was a
great horse with a wonderful rush which always “ came
off” at the right moment when business was meant. No
other animal in Australian racing history achieved such a
wide range of successes in big handicaps—a Newmarket,
an Adelaide Cup, Melbourne Cup, and Australian Cup.
Then, being put to the “sticks,” he won the V.R.C.
Grand Nat. Hurdle Race, after which the veteran showed
a flash of his old brilliancy by taking the Geelong Cup
with 9st. 41b. up. It used to be said that Malua’s rush
spoilt his jockey, Alec. Robertson, for riding other horses
by tempting him to wait too long with them. Trenton’s
defeat for Melb. Cup which Sheet Anchor won was a case
•in point. The stout Maorilander had a mere trifle of
•7st. 121 b. in the saddle, yet poor Robertson kept the
“dead bird” back until the winning-post was near instead
of giving him a chance to win on his stamina. 'Trenton,
Hit and well, finished only third to Sheet Anchor and
Grace Darling, through being ridden on the Malua system.
Next year, with a dotty leg and a lot of superfluous fat
•on him, he carried another couple of stone into second
place behind Arsenal.

the Le Var case. To all racing-men it was patent that

knew this and so did not ride his mount out to the bitter
•end. To censure Messrs. Clark and Payten and suspend
the jockey was Draconian indeed.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *

Mr. Hordern’s reported explanation as to the
scratching of The Skipper, like most explanations, had
been better left unsaid. No aspersions were east on his
trainer—the public blamed Mr. Hordern, and him alone.
Mr. Hordern acknowledges that he had made up his
mind on Friday to scratch the horse, and no excuses as
to business engagements can alter the fact that he
allowed the public to go on backing The Skipper until
the last moment on Saturday. If Mr. Hordern was too
busy to go to Earnshaw on Friday, a sixpenny wire or
one of his army of employes could have brought Earn-
shaw to him.
* * * *
The Steeplechase at Rand wick on Saturday was
even a greater farce than the Derby. Half-a-mile from
the start Confusion was actually pulled into a trot, and
Chris, the hard puller, was reeling and smashing his
fences for want of a leader to show him over. Reckless
was the only horse who did not fall, and finished a furlong
ahead of old Bushman, who was remounted smartly by
Hendricks after falling over the last fence. Five falls and
two horses killed is a record for even the Randwick
Steeplechase course.
* * » *
The past A.J.O. meeting can be summed up
briefly: “ Bookies boomed, backers broke.” In-and-out
running, sometimes intentional, at others the pure cussed-
ness of the noble race’orse, utterly befogged backers, and
the only redeeming feature of the meeting was the brilliant
and consistent form displayed by Newhaven, whose vic-
tories have rightly enough placed him in position of first
favorite for Melb. Derby and Cup. In the Derby the
only animal which seems likely to trouble him is Resolute,
but to carry 7st. 131 b. to victory in the Cup will test his
marvellous powers to the uttermost.
Randwick Plate, with which are associated such
names as Le Grand, Nordenfeldt, Trident, Abercorn, and
Melos, was won by the runty Tir6 in smin. 52sec., thus
exceeding time-limit by 7sec., and so securing only half
stakes. Pity that a time-limit is not fixed for all races a
mile and a half and over, thus preventing long-distance
races revolving themselves into sprints, as was the case in
Derby and Waverley Handicap.
Lochiel came out on top in the list of winning
sires with three winners to his credit, whilst Carbine,
Newminster, Robert the Devil, and Splendor dead-heated
with two apiece.
Throughout Randwick meeting the horseman-
ship displayed by the majority of the jockeys was exe-
crable. Little Fielder cleverly handled Steward and Loch
Leigh, whilst James Gough showed some of his old bril-
liance in squeezing Royal Rose home for Squatter’s Han-
dicap.
Whatever the cause—handicapper, plethora of
racing, or bad luck —owners seem to fight shy of Rand-
wick. For the A. J.C. Sept, meeting to be held there to-
day (Saturday) but 67 entries have been received for five
races. Head-quarters of turf in N.S.W. Evidently a
screw loose somewhere. Messrs. Long and Hordern have
nominated freely, and perhaps a few races will fall to
their representatives.
* * * »
S.A. football season ended Sept. 12. South
Adelaide club secured top place with 16 wins and two
drawn games, Norwood second. The undefeated Souths
were so strong that Adelaide football-matches last season
degenerated into a farce. The once-famous Port
Adelaide team comes absolutely last on the list. Next
year the S.A.F.A. intends to experiment with district
clubs, a final desperate attempt to fight the rival game of
lacrosse, which has increased in popularity by leaps and
bounds.
* * * *
It only needs a few trots like last Monday’s
handicap at Brighton to give the sport a big fillip about
Sydney. The long-looked-for match between Huon Junior
and Hattie S for £2OO a-side proved a fiasco, Huon cutting
out his mile in 2.24 J, and distancing the mare in the first
heat. The handicap proved one of the best exhibitions
of trotting yet seen about Sydney. Creole led till turning
into the straight for the last time, when Regina passed
him, but Huon Junior, getting out of the block, came
like a flash and beat Regina on the post by a head. Both
horse and mare finished under the whip like a couple of
'bus horses, amidst excitement seldom seen in Australia
over a trotting race.
* * * *
Saturday’s Melb. football-matches did not, after
all, settle the Premiership, Fitzroy’s defeat of Colling-
wood, South’s win against P. Melb., leaving Collingwood
and South equal at the top of the list. Collingwood’s
match was, perhaps, one of the most even and exciting
ever seen in Melb., even the hitherto uninterested public
going wild with enthusiasm. The first half and
third quarter finished with the scores even, and when,
towards the finish, Fitzroy led by a goal, Collingwood’s
play was desperate and excellent. They failed, however,
in goal-kicking, and the game ended—Fitzroy, 4g. 4b.;
Collingwood, 3g. Bb. To-day (26th) South and Colling-
wood play off for the premiership. The result is a toss-
up, but South should win.
How about a draw in the play-off ? What a
mighty temptation for another and a greater match with
correspondingly-increased interest and “ gate 1 ”
* * * *
C. H. Herbert, of Ballarat, carried off the Vic.
10-mile harrier championship in winning by a quarter of a
mile from Fryer, who was half that distance ahead of Tom
Blake, the Carlton footballer, the ’95 champion. Fryer
jumped into runner-up’s place from being 42nd last year.
This is Herbert’s second win, for in 1894 he finished first,
100yds. ahead of Flack, who won several events at the
recent Greek Olympian sports.
* * * *
Wally Kerr, one of thfc smallest and most re-
markable of Australian cyclists, is coming on to the
racing-track again. Kerr gave up racing some time ago,
but the news of Gaudaur, aged 39, winning a champion
sculling-contest has persuaded him that a man is never
too old to race.
* * * *
A ghastly cycling story comes from America.
Griebler started one of 13 in a half-mile race at Lima, Ohio.
Before half a lap had been run Kimble and Stevens came
down, Sarger ran broadside into Bald and then fell.
Griebler, in his superhuman effort to avoid the fallen
riders and machines, burst a blood vessel, became insen-
sible, dashed into the four-foot embankment enclosing
the track, pitched off headlong, and was picked up dying.
His left ear was torn off, his skull fractured, and blood
was flowing from his mouth and nose. No cricketer has
yet been killed upon Sydney Cricket Ground, despite
the innumerable matches played there, out the new
cement track will not. be down long before some cyclist
will peg out there.
* * * •
John S. Johnson, one of the crack American
flyers, and the speedy Barden are half-promised for Aus-
tralia in Nov. and Dec. respectively. Johnson was very
discontented with English racing, the purses being “ so
small that you wanted a microscope to find them.” Stories
of Martin’s winnings will doubtless draw a few good men
here before long.
* * * *
The American Wheelman, after remarking that
Jack Parsons “talks with a decided British accent,”
gives its interview with Parsons verbatim : “ Parsons said
Johnson and I became very good chums over in England,
you know, and when they were putting up such bloody
small purses we decided to come to America and ride the
rest of the season, and then go to Australia, where the
season promises to be very brilliant. The statement made
by Walford, the manager of Zimmerman, to the effect
that he paid me £25 to allow the American to win is a
Weedin’ lie, you know, as I never received a cent, and
when Zimmerman won he won honestly. Zimmerman was
very popular in Australia, which is not the case with
Martin, who, as I understand it, recently tried to punch
one of the officials, you know.” Parsons could not talk
about Martin this way in Australia, because Martin is
universally recognised as the gamest and most honest
rider who has yet appeared upon our tracks. As for
punching people, Martin has done so, and very vigorously,
too; but he only took a manly stand when set upon by
some “ gun ”or bully. Martin’s reputation is built upon
walloping cyclists at cycling—he badly walloped Parsons,
tor instance.
* * * *
The latest American yarn is that cyclist Ander-
son, paced by a locomotive and running on a boarded
track laid between the rails, did a mile in lmin. 3sec.
Anderson used a 92 gear.
* * * *
Stanbury s sudden departure after his defeat
by Gaudaur indicates not in the slightest degree that he
was satisfied that he lost the race on his merits,” remarks
a Bulletin correspondent. “ The Hawkesburv man, long
before the match, arranged, win or lose, to sail on a cer-
tain date.
* * * *
The all-conquering cycle, In order to gain the
subscnptions of racing cyclists for training on its track,
bt. lUlda (Vic.) C.C. has prepared a special pitch outside
the ground altogether, and ukased that all cricket
practice shall take place on this, so that the sprinting
wheelmen need have no fear of flying balls. The coolness
with which the cricketers are ordered off their own ground
to make way for the sport with the most cash in it is
sublime.
* * * •
The day of the Bendigo-to-Melb. road-race,
pedestrians in the latter city were startled by meeting a
hatless youth, attired only in a sleeveless jersey, cycling
?“ oes 0 esa n d almost legless knickerbockers, sauntering'down
the Block with a huge 17 on his back. At first he was
thought to be Nudity Apostle Macdonald. The apparition,
followed by a host of yelling small bovs, threaded his
way through unfrequented rights-of-way and untrafficked
streets to where the race was to finish, and there awaited
the competitor to whom he had confided his bike on
dropping out at Kyneton and coming to Melb. by train.

tor Worlds Championship, is not regarded as a boss
by the Hunter River critics, but Peter Kemp, who
has him in hand, avows that he is as fine a sculler as he
J-ever met—and Peter has seen heaps of champions

Coolgardie Race Club meeting was a great
success, £12,000 being put through the totalisator. Next
>s be a very swagger affair—gold trophv with
±lOOO added—and several horses have already been
brought from t’other side with a view of annexing this

* * * *
The only sport that ex-clmmpion Bill Beach
now indulges in is pigeon-shooting. He is only a fair
shot, which signifies that he does not lose much money at
the game. It is the good pigeon-shot that loses—the
deceased Owen Friend, for instance, has lost as much as
±l4OO in one afternoon on Sydney ground backing him-
self on the first bird. And there are two other Svdneyites,
now of middle age and in poor case, who lost most of
their big inherited fortunes (one £BO,OOO and the other,
perhaps, £00,000) by backing gun against bird.
A quick shot is F. B. Bloomfield, who, on
Sydney ground last week, shared the Ladies’ Bracelets
with five others, and took the Boswell’s gun prize by
arrangement with W. A. McLennan. Bloomfield anil
M‘Lennan each shot 20 birds in succession before Coining
to terms. Bloomfield grassed his bird very often within
a foot or two of the trap, and much of his shooting was of
true exhibition order.
The Hordern Shield, valued at =BlOO, was won
by Mr. C. H. Kerry in fine style with a Greener gun,
Kerry’s was a popular win, because he already had one-
leg in, and thus the downing of his 21st bird made the
shield his. He received quite an ovation.
What A. W. Eales is thought of as a pigeon-
shot is evidenced by the fact that, although shooting from
31yds., and in comparatively poor form, none of the books
would take less than 2 to 1 on the first barrel.
* * * *
“Useful Hints about Bicycles and Tyres ” has
recently been published by W. J. C. Elliott, who probably
knows quite as much about cycles and cyclists as anyone
else in Australia. The book, however, omits to give any
useful hintsabout mending the cyclist himself.
An Australian triplet did a mile in 1 min. 58J
sec. the other day. This is the time for Linton’s first
mile when he rode 31 miles in one hour. What a feat for
a triplet to cackle about!
* * * *
The second Sydney cycle corpse has arrived.
Deceased, one Frederick Hexter, collided with a cab and
smashed his skull. O that mine enemy would ride
a wheel 1
* * * *
Ugly cycle accident in M.L. One Thorpe hung
his bicycle on a clothes-line, and, spinning the wheel,
proceeded to clean the chain with a duster. The duster
got caught; Thorpe tried to extricate duster. Result :
Thorpe lost two fingers.
* * * *
Many Parisian she-cyclists have abandoned
stockings and now wear socks with their bloomers. The
bare calf is doubtless offered to the public eye for the
purpose of advertising the fact that the bloomer'ed portion
would not bleed sawdust.
* * * *
There are very few things which the ordinary
policeman won’t swear to when he gets started. At Mel-
bourne, the other day, a weedy youth with a bone-shaker
was charged with furious riding, and the “cop” climbed
into the box and swore, with a serious countenance, that
he was doing 30 miles an hour. Yet even plugger Bil-
martin, when plugging the immortal soul out of himself
on a made track, only achieved something like 27 miles
an hour, and the policeman who stopped Bilmartin while
going at that pace wouldn’t be able to swear to anything
for at least a week afterwards.

From a Sydney Ministerial paper : ‘ ‘ Owing
to the inability of the charitable institutions of the
city to meet the demands made upon their re-
sources on account of the ever-increasing applica-
tions for help,” &c. How we do keep on getting
“ jolly and fat ” !
A Sydney Domain worthy, fired with a
high ambition, obtained a free railway-ticket to
Forbes on the assurance that he was certain of a
job of work from an old friend when he got there.
Met a few nights later in his old haunt in the
Domain, he admitted that his return was quick,
but confidentially explained that all he wanted to
do at Forbes was to cut his name on the wooden
railing erected there round the grave of bushranger
Ben Hall.
At Westbury, a small N. Tas. township,
there is a fine, imposing, stone, slate-roofed school,
capable of seating some hundreds of children. It
is even a finer edifice than any township “hall.”
(If three houses in T. stand within a square-mile
they call themselves a “township,” and see about
having a “ hall ” —generally with a mortgage on
it.) visitors to W. ask how such a building came
to be erected. This is the answer : Some five or
six years back this building and a small one were
designed for State schools. This one was to be
situated at Launceston, the northern capital, the
other at Westbury. The designs got mixed—and
so the Launceston building got erected at West-
bury !
A recent Yic. garrotting case recalls an
incident of some years back. The victim, an old
man of nearly fourscore, was set upon by two
ruffians late at night, it being known to them that
he had £5 in his pocket. The old fellow had been
an athlete in his youth, and could still hit straight,
so that one of his assailants, coming incautiously,
received a jaw-shaker. It sealed the poor old
man’s fate, and after choking him the murderers
turned his pockets out without, however, finding
a single penny. The money had disappeared, and
the dismayed couple had (as it turned out) hanged
themselves for nothing. Next morning the body
was found, and at the post-mortem the doctor, on
opening with difficulty the old man’s clenched
hand, discovered in his fist a fiver ! The mur-
derer had been literally struck with the money
and never knew it.

Cheviot (M.L.), writes a Bulletin corre-
spondent, is a teetotal district, inasmuch as there
are no pubs, within 40 miles, and yet there is a
thirst that can only be quenched by barrels of
colonial beer. An enterprising ex-publican tried
to cope with the thirst aforesaid by retailing
chain-lightning whisky, per nip, in a dark passage
at the back of his temperance boarding-house, but
a grateful friend put him away, and a £5O fine
and 18 months for perjury was the result. But
still the thirst goes on. Various temperance
societies have tried to grapple with it, but m vain,
as, after a bout of tea-drinking, it resumes its
course with renewed vigor. Last Christmas was
a very dry one, as, owing to some delay in transit,
the holiday beer-supply was late in arriving. A
dismal gloom settled on the little township of
M‘Kenzie (named after Jock M‘K., Minister of
Lands), and instead of sounds of revelry on the
Christmas-eve, all that could be heard was a burly,
beer-eating waggoner wandering up and down the
main street enquiring, in pathetic tones, “ if any-
one had a drop in the cruet.” Next day the sup-
ply arrived, and all previous hardships were for-
gotten in a glorious burst, which lasted a week.
The thirst is still in full blast.

Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.

(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later: —“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint a's
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours verv gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

deliver it and all debts due to the Company must
be paid to Mr. James Boyne Hall of Brisbane the

desiring to vote at the meeting must produce to
