North Sydney, June 25,18%
Gentlemen, —I cannot speak too highly of your excellent
preparation of KOLA NUT. As a tonic, I found it far
superior to anything of the kind I ever used, and have
strongly recommended it to several friends, all of whom
(after taking it) are enthusiastic in their praise of it. Kola
Nut Tonic has a most beneficial effect after an attack oi
INFLUENZA ; on some of my acquaintances it has pro-
duced wonderful effects, particularly in strengthening the
muscles of chest and limbs. Many of my cycling friends
swear by it. I notice its use has been adopted by the
Alpine Club as a STIMULANT and NUTRIENT in moun-
tain-climbing expeditions, &c., as it generally increases
the power of the LUNGS and keeps off muscular fatigue,
and will prove invaluable to athletes. I feel confident
Kola Nut Tonic will have a large consumption, for those
who once try it will always keep it in store.—Yours faith-
fully,
CHARLES WISEIIEART,
Willoughby.
NO CYCLIST SHOULD BE WITHOUT THIS REMARK-
ABLE INVIGORATOR, ESPECIALLY LADIES.
EVERY SINGER, AUCTIONEER, and PUBLIC
SPEAKER should take KOLA NUT TONIC.
A recent issue of the Planters’ Monthly contains a
description of this remarkable nut and its uses :
“ Kola has the property of staying hunger, whilst sup-
porting the struggle for existence.
“ If a supply of nuts had been included in Mr. Stanley's
outfit his men would not have suffered so terribly for want
of animal food.
“ As might be expected , the value of Kola increases as
it makes its way into the interior of Africa, and some of
the tribes farthest removed from the sea pay for the dry
powder an equal weight of gold-dust.
“In a despatch from Mr. George Alexander Stevens,
Her Majesty’s Consul at Bahai, he calls attention to the
great powers of endurance in lifting heavy loads and
transporting them to long distances in the tropical
climate, attributed to the free use of Kola Bean. An old
African, after biting a piece of the magic Bean, lifted 17b
English pounds weight on to his head and carried the
same for a distance of four miles.
“Still another merit is named, however, which will
interest the temperance party. The negros who chew
Kola Nut have no desire to drink rum, and hence save
money and retain their strength.”
A woman—whose name under the circumstances cannot
be given—came into our shop the other day, with tears of
gratitude in her eyes, and said, “ My husband used to
come home on Saturdays DRUNK, but he has been taking
Kola Nut Tonic for four weeks, and now comes home
quite SOBER and places the whole of his wages in mv
hands. God bless you ! ”

The Papuans are addicted to the old heathen
practice of depositing a man’s weapons on his
grave. The cooking utensils and grass petticoat
are also laid on the grave of the woman. Strange
to say, the people who do this have no belief in a
future state.
“The Bushman’s Song,” “Last Week,”
and “Daylight is Dying,” the three songs from
“The Man from Snowy River,” recently set to
music, are now having an enormous sale. They
may be had from Angus and Robertson, Sydney,
and all musicsellers. Post free, 2/2 each.*
Blacks belonging to a camp near a little
N.S.W. coastal town were making good hauls of
fi-b jvhich were scarce elsewhere at the time, and
were advised to ship a lot to Sydney. A big
parcel was sent up by the steamer, and .Tacky, the
most truculent buck of the tribe, was deputed to
accompany them and look after the business.
“ Goo’-bye, you pfeller,” said Jacky, as he blithely
jumped aboard ; “ I bring you anythin’ back, eh ? ”
One old gin called out: “ Yes, you bring Billy one
pannikin and Mary one pannikin, and send tel’-
gram how much you getta fish.” Two days after-
wards the expectant tribe received a wire : “ Fish
sold, in 2,” and when the steamer arrived there
was a consignment of two pannikins but no Jacky,
who was having a large time in Sydney on the
proceeds of the fish. In about a week another
wire was received from the absent one: “Coming
back to-morrow.” Jacky’s arrival was greeted
with much joy, but he did not deign to acknow-
ledge the demonstration and walked straight camp-
wards, a jabbering procession at his heels. “ Wha’
for you nfeller follow me ? ” he asked at last.
“ Gibbit money for fish,” they shouted. “What
money you talk about ? ” he asked with a long-
suffering expression—“ don’t I send you two pan-
nikin ?. Don’t I send two telegram ? Those ting
cost plenty money—what you dam fool think ? ”

A' Chinaman turned up at a N.S.W.
country sale-yard, the other day, and was beguiled
by the auctioneer into buying for <£3 an old
broken-down scarecrow, rising 17, and totally
blind. Next day the horse was harnessed to a
vegetable-cart, and, having been started off by a
few gentle words from John, he proceeded to walk
through the gate before it was opened for him. In
his mild outburst of anger the Chow tapped the
horse none too lightly on the head, which sent him
back at a great rate until he was brought to a
standstill by the shanty, which got mixed with
the, back of"the cart. Nothing would induce the
poor horse to leave his anchorage until a friendly
yellow neighbor let off a bundle of crackers be-
hind his back legs ; and then the animal showed
he could pull by taking half the hut away with
him. After trying to work the horse for two days
the Chinaman brought him back to the sale-yards
and told the auctioneer to sell him for £3 10s.
Two hours afterwards the horse was returned: to
his former owner for lfis. fid. —the Chow getting
15s. 3d. He then departed telling the auctioneer
that he would be up next day to buy “oldliorsee—
no likee tarn young horsee —too muchee smashum
cart.” True to his word the Chinaman arrived
early next morning, and the second horse put up,
which was in a cart and had a neat set of harness
on, took John’s eye at once. He started bidding
against the auctioneer’s buttoner, and finally
secured the animal for £2 9s. The Chow received
a great ovation from the crowd when he led his
horse away after paying ; and he was smiling all
over. He didn’t smile next day when the horse
played precisely the same tricks as the one he
sold, and an intelligent countryman discovered it
was the same horse, clipped, and with three of its
legs and its face painted white. The Chinaman
now sells vegetables out of baskets. Chinese are a
dishonest lot.

Concerning crows’-nest and eggs, a Bul-
letin correspondent declares that he was once lost
on the Plains of Riverina, and lived on crows’
eggs for two whole days. Since then he slaughters
every crow within gunshot.
Dear Bulletin, —Has Mr. R. A. Fraser
never heard of the automatically-closing doors
applied to the railway-carriages of the district
railway (underground), London? They worked
admirably and did all that was demanded of them
—more. For it was found that the doors being
kept shut till the train arrived at a standstill—4os.
fine for alighting from a train in motion —com-
pelled a longer wait at each station ; the average
traveller drops off before the train stops, and
is never fined unless he falls down. Automatically-
closing doors were abolished and the trains were
run “ontime.” — Wubrym.
The banana is the most prolific of all fruits,
being 44 times more prolific than potatoes, and 131
times more than wheat.
An Illinois Judge has decided that, in
divorce cases, a husband is entitled to alimony
and lawyer’s costs. The order was that the hus-
band, being without means, was entitled to receive
from the wife’s income an allowance for his sup-
port of sdol. weekly pending suit, and also of
25d01. solicitor's fee to enable him to defend.
“Why is the sky blue?” That brainy
Vic. mathematician, William Sutherland, pro-
pounds a theory that it is due to the fact that, as
oxygen under low pressure becomes ozone (which
is blue in tint), all the higher strata of the atmo-
sphere must consist of ozone. The verdict of
physical scientists on this Australian contribution
will be waited with interest.

“Skew” Evans.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Three notes a week and a shanty in
England 1 Blow England, anyway ! ’Sowever,
it’ll be better than graftin’ a feller’s bloomin’ soul
out for nothin’ in this Gawd-forgot patch.”
Thus Silas Neame, shiftless “ cocky,” of Back-
gumbah, New South Wales, to the wild-haired,
freckled, flat-chested female he called wife, but
who neither honored nor obeyed him. He, native-
born, had just received legal tidings that his
grand-uncle in England, dying, had bequeathed
him a house and Al5O a year, on condition that he
(Silas) Tesided at Ashbourne, Kent, England. So
Silas sold his mean milkers, razor-backed hogs,
spavined horses, and decomposed implements,
slouched to Sydney, and there took steamer. Two
persons only, one a little, the other exceedingly,
regretted his departure —his daughter Cis, a bush
beauty and coquette of 16, who was “ryther
mashed ” on young Sam Levin, stockrider on
Backgumbah station, and Sam, desperately fond
of the girl, and heartbroken at parting.
“ But you’ll waitfor a feller, Cis ? I’ll go to thish-
yer new diggins at Teroarer, make my pile, and
come to England and splice you inside of two
years.”
Cis kissed and promised. Yet, bitten by the
tarantula of change, thought much of the possible
pleasures ensuing and little of Sam. He, a man
and earnestly in love, was, as goes without saying,
a fool. Within two years, having scooped .£BOO at
Teroarer, he was in Ashbourne, asking for Cis.
Silas, with strong beers oozing out of the corners
of his mouth, swore rings round him when his
daughter’s name was mentioned. The woman
threw her apron over her head and whimpered
like a locked-out dog. Kind neighbors were, how-
ever, ready to tell all about Cis—how she had
“ ’ooked it, a matter o’ nine month afore, with a
swell chap fro’ they blarsted cavalary barricks at
Canterbury,” and had been seen in Lunnon, “ a
raddled Jezebull! ”
To London Sam went seeking Cis, and there,
after months, and by what Occidentals call coinci-
dence and Orientals, wiser, kismet, found her, in
hospital, dying. She knew him, and gasped out
a name. The nurse supplied other particulars.
Sam sought nothing else. He did not even attend
the girl’s funeral. He was too drunk. He was
drunk and mad for many days, and—then !
* * * *
“For inspection—port arms!” General
foot-parade for the 24th Hussars, one sweltering
afternoon at Ferozebagh, Bengal. Captain Nether-
gat—with delicate irony the men called him
“Leathergut”—was about to inspect “ B ” troop
on their troop parade-ground. The troop-sergeant-
major had inspected them, and a sucking sub-
altern, whom the men swore at almost audibly,
but without vice—for they loathed foot-parades,
the sun was hot and their helmets tight—had,
without comprehension, nosed their buttons and
pouch-belts. Called to attention by their captain,
him they cursed silently, but atrociously. For
they hated him more than all bad things known in
earth or suspected in hell. He had exchanged
from the infantry some two years before, a fact in
itself condemnatory to horsemen. Added thereto,
he, “regimental as a button-stick” in barrack-room
or on foot-parade, flinched, when mounted, at
rough field-days, rode coward at a charge, and was
unmitigated bully and tyrant always. Many
times had “B” troop, with much pretended sweat-
ing at bit-reins and more surreptitious spurring,
tried to ride over him at brigade field-days. Had
he led the troop into action he would never have
ridden out alive. Two score of troopers would
have made that their business. And Nethergat
knew it and loved his men accordingly. He in-
spected them as if they were lepers. “ ’Tisu’t
exackly what the gory toe soldier says; it’s the
way the sanguinary swine says it and looks at a
man,” the men agreed. Now, he passed in silence
the first two files, halting, contemptuous, at the
third.
“ What’s this fellow’s name, Sergeant-Major
Dillon?”
“Evans, sir.”
Nethergat knew this well, but that form of in-
sult is ever effective.
“ Well, Evans is beastly dirty, as usual.”
The troop sergeant-major said nothing. He
knew his captain lied and that “Skew” Evans
was, as ever, spick and span.
The man himself stared straight to his front,
his face impassive and rigid as of a Hindoo graven
image. He was well used to this hazing from his
captain.
“ What service has he, sergeant-major ? ”
“Eighteen months, sir; joined the dep6t at
Canterbury just after you embarked for India.
Only been out here six months, sir. ”
_ “ Aw ! Thought he was a damn recruit. Make
him a prisoner when the dismiss sounds.” He
continued his inspection.
“Shoulder arms! —stand at ease!—stand easy !”
Said Dillon to his captain as they waited the
“March on,” “What crime shall I put against
private Evans?”
“Dirty on general parade, of course.”
“ Beg pardon, sir ; cleanest man on parade.
Colonel won’t like it, sir.”
The captain bored his teeth, prior to exercising
his large gift of verbal offensiveness, but checked
himself. He had reasons for placating Dillon.
“ Do as you damn please,” he snarled. “There’s
the trumpet ! troop, tenshun ! —fours right !
quick march ! ”
Two old moustaches, just off guard, “ foxed ”
the inspection from “B ” troop bungalow. Said
one : “ What’s that pig Leatherguts always down
on Skew Evans for ? Skew’s a good man, ain’t
he ? ”
“Yes, makin’s of a bloomin’ smart hussar —
good rider, saveys horses, straight comrade,” said
the other, Skew’s chum. “Some day,” he
observed reflectively, “one o’ these ’ere Board
School ’cruities with an uncut tail ’ll put a bullet
in old Leatherguts.”
“ Skew will, I expect, if the hinfernal swaddy
don’t let up on him.”
“ .No, I axed Skew oncet if he didn’t feel that
way. Skew looked black as a dust-devil, but he
says, ‘ let ’im rip; he’ll be bailed up worse than
shooting one of these days. I’m on his track.’
Adjutant told Skew he’d transfer him to ‘ C ’
troop, and give him the lance stripe.”
“ Why didn’t he go ? ”
“Loves his dear capting too much. Said he
wouldn’t leave ‘B ’ troop for no money. ‘More
confounded fool you,’ says the Adjutant. But
Dillon thinks a heap of Evans, and has saved him
time and again.”
“ Skew a good scholard ? ”
“Fust chop. Wasn’t bad when he joined the
depdt, and been attendin’ school ever since. He’s
a quiet bloke, too. Takes his beer, but don’t go
large. Got one of tlvun there foto fakements ;
best bloomin’ fotografier in the station.”
“ Leatherguts don’t shop him so often now ? ”
“ No, last time he clinked’im for ‘ dumb insol-
ence ’ —said he looked disrespeckful—old Daddy,
the colonel, says, ‘ Captain Nethergat,’ says he,
‘ you hardly seem to know what constitoots a good
soldier, not in the cavalry at least,’ he says;
‘ when we’ve a good man we keep him out of
trouble, not ram him into it. Dismissed ! ’ says
good old Daddy. The capting he gives Skew par-
ticular sheoke, same as before, but he don’t put
’im in the guard report. ”
The other summed up: “I’ve got a bloomin’
rupee ; let’s go down to the bazar and drink it.
They’re all marched on now. ”
Troop-sergeant-major Dillon,. “B” troop,
was admitted by his officers to be the best, and by
the men the smartest and straightest non-com. in
the corps. He was a good disciplinarian—a just
man and a merciful. Therefore “If ” troop would
—for that is a cavalry-man’s way—have ridden
after him to the very gates of Sheol, through them,
and out the other side. He had held his rank for
years; frugal and keen, he had saved and made
money—as his captain knew. But Dillon had the
ghastliest skeleton in his cupboard—as the whole
regiment knew. He had been six months married
to the 17-year-old daughter of orderly-room ser-
geant Jeff and Johanna his wife. The vocabulary
of the 24th was insufficient to paint the iniquities
of Mrs. Dillon’s mamma. She drank, she swore
in a manner the most seand t ;ooper might envy
but not emulate, she corrected Jeff with sauce-
pans, crockery and barr.».ck-roo.n furniture ; she
had poisoned him, ineffectual y, twice ; her amours
would have made an English society leader a
Begum, or a painted Hindoo goddess blush. .'Six
months back her daughter flitted through che
pqlcherj, fair-haired, shy, and, seemingly, virgin-
ally innocent. Married, and under her
tutelage, she developed most of that mother’s
vices. Dillon’s modest quarters became as the
abode of the damned. His young wife’s misde-
meanors were as miscellaneous as notorious ;
everyone but Dillon knew that the .person with
whom her name had recently been most frequently
coupled was—Captain Nethergat. And, at last,
he knew that also. When evil fortune thus smites
a man honorable, proud and punctilious, one who,
from the nature of things, might not obtain
divorce, two cour es only are open to him. Of
these Dillon promptly chose one.
* * * *
The night before “B” troop commenced
its annual musketry course, Sergeant Major
Dillon was very busy. Before watchsetting there
was not a pie in dispute in any man’s accounts,
not a horse shoe, not a spare nail out of place.
The last man he sent for was “ Skew ” Evans.
“Evans, you’re a man can keep your mouth
shut.”
“ I think so, Sergeant-Major.”
“I know it; I’ve watched you close. We’re
speaking as man to man, not as sergeant-major to
private soldier.”
“ Thank you,” said the other.
“Listen now, and serve me as I’ve tried to
serve you. Be at my bungalow, in the verandah
at north end, to-morrow morning at 7.25 by the


Contractors for Government stores tender
at ruinously low prices for items not wanted in
quantity, at the same time keeping up values on
items extensively needed. A Vic. contractor some
years back included in a railways tender leather
buckets at a shilling each. The price was so low
that Vic. Railways supplied every station with
leather buckets in place of the iron or zinc article.
That contractor lost =£soo through astutely listing
=£l buckets at 12d. each.
Mr. Ferderli, wine-expert at Dookie (Vic.)
Agricultural College, has produced some valuable
evidence of the improvement that can be made
upon ordinary wines by the use of a cultivated
levure in the fermenting process. A “ levured ”
sherry is found incomparably superior to the same
grape-juice fermented in the old ordinary way.
And this notwithstanding than an ordinary Vic.
sherry is more true to its European name than any
of the other yellow wines raised from the soil.
The Q. Kanaka trouble seems to be gradu-
ally settling itself. Sept. 1 the Helena returned
to Bundaberg, after a five months’ trip, with only
32 “recruits.” She had been chartered to bring
90, but the “ boys ” wouldn’t recruit. She tried
the Hebrides group of islands only, and “plenty
fellow yam, ” as a good harvest, was more potent
in the eyes of the islanders than a trip to Q. The
cost of the Helena’s voyage may be roughly set
down at =£looo, so the cheap labor must have cost
over =£3o a head landed at Bundaberg.
Prophet Varley’s pyrotechnics on the Papal
Conference of 1890 :
Inside the venerable pile of St. Peter’s sat the great
company of the Ecclesiastics. The heavens were black
to almost Egyptian darkness. A storm of unparalleled
continuance and violence held the whole city. Lightnings
flashed in vivid, rapid and terrifying succession. Thun-
ders like a thousand parks of artillery crashed, rolled and-
reverberated over the whole scene. No wonder that
nature herself, as at the time of the Spanish Armada,
lifted up her voice in tempestuous protest against the
confederated attempt to usurp the attributes ai.d func-
tions which alone pertain to the Living God.
Certainly. And Tarawera erupted because little
Rangatunipito sneaked into his uncle’s whare in
the night and went through his trousers for
eighteenpence which the beery warrior had found
under a poker-table down at the village pub.
Rats !

I have discarded all others.”
guard-room clock. Have you a watch ? ”
“ Yes.”
“ Then set it by the guard-room clock to-night.
Bring that kodak thing of yours with you to-
morrow. At 7.30 I will call you in. Then, photo-
graph everything you see in the room — everything,
mind. Take two or three photographs, if neces-
sary. You will touch nothing, speak to no one,
and never show the photographs, unless I—unless
you know you ought to. Do you understand ? ”
The man reflected. His face quivered a little.
“ I think so,” he said at last, sadly.
“ Say it all over.”
Evans repeated the instructions.
“That’s right—be on time, to the second ; it’s
important. ”
Dillon paused. “If you should ever want ad-
vice on this, and I cannot give it, speak to the
Quartermaster-sergeant; he’s my friend, and will
be yours. There’s nothing more.”
Skew went out, but presently returned.
“ Give me all that in writing, please, sergeant-
major.”
The other looked at him hard. “ You’re right,”
was all he said. He sat down and wrote; then
handed Evans the paper, rose, and stretched out
his hand. They looked in each other’s eyes. Each
recognised a man. “ Good-night, my lad.”
“Good— bye, sergeant-major.”
• ♦ * •
Next morning the troop went to musketry.
Sergeant-Major Dillon’s name appeared on the
sick-report. But he did not go to hospital. A
farrier of “ B ” troop, coming in early from shoot-
ing, was hailed by his sergeant-major.
“Have you cleaned your carbine yet, Farrier
Jones ?”
“ Not yet, sergeant-major ; I’m just in from the
butts.”
“Lend it to me, then. I want to shoot some-
thing, and if I use your carbine it’ll save dirtying
my own. ”
* * * *
At 7.25 a.m., by the guard-room clock,
Skew Evans stood in the north verandah of
Dillon’s bungalow—his kodak ready. At 7.30 a
voice, loud and clear, rang out:
“Now, Evans !”
There rang also the report of a carbine, with the
twang a bullet gives. Skew paused, paled, lifted
the curtain and looked. On the bed, the roof of
his head blown off, Dillon —already dead, a cord
stretched from his foot to the trigger of a carbine
lying across his breast. The impulse of Evans
was to rush to the dead man. But, recalling his
instructions, he fixed his kodak, touched the
button, and the sun limned the tragedy. On the
table by the bed lay two large canvas bags, seem-
ingly tilled with rupees, two large official enve-
lopes, and one smaller package. He photographed
the table. Then, hearing a step, concealed him-
self where he could command the room and the
button of his kodak. One entered, ejaculated,
viewed Dillon’s dead body, and lingered.
Once—twice—thrice, the button of the kodak
tapped !
* * * *
Dillon’s suicide caused universal sorrow,
but not surprise. Surprise, however, touched pro-
fundity when it was known that “B” troop
lacked their month’s pay, because Dillon had
made away with every anna of troop money in his
possession. So Captain Nethergat sorrowfully
reported to the colonel. He had, he said, “long
suspected poor Dillon of rash speculation.” The
accounts, he explained, were scrupulously correct,
only no farthing could be found of the large bal-
ance m Dillon’s hands. That afternoon, without
military honors, Dillon was buried. The regiment
cursed venomously when Nethergat, temporarily
acting adjutant, yelped across the parade-ground
to the non-com. commanding the funeral:
“ Trot, with that body ! Trot, confound you !
What the devil d’ye mean by walking with a
suicide ? ”
• * * *
A few nights afterwards the orderly-
corporal of “B” troop sang out—“ Evans! it’s
your turn to take the order-book to the captain’s
bungalow. Look out, he’ll bite your bloomin’
head off ! ”
“ Oh, it’s you, confound you ! ” said Nethergat,
with accustomed politeness. He opened the book
where marked by a piece of paper, “ You fool,
these are not the orders of the day !” His eye
caught the marker, ‘ ‘ Hello! what the deuce is
this ? ”
It was an unmounted photograph—Dillon lying
dead on his bed.
Evans saluted, “Beg pardon, sir—next page.”
The captain turned the leaf. There, another
photograph—Dillon’s table, the bags of money,
and the letters.
Nethergat stared, at first stupidly, then shook a
little, staggered, and sat down before his desk.
“ I —l don’t understand,” he muttered.
“ There are three more photographs there, Cap-
tain Nethergat; perhaps you’d better look at
them.” “Skew’s” tone was changed; it was
strident and commanding. The captain, dazed
and trembling, turned to the three photographs.
One —a man lifting from a table two canvas
bags.
Two —the same man covering with a military
cloak the hand holding the bags.
Three—the man departing through the south
door of Dillon’s bungalow. In his left hand two
official envelopes.
The man was—Captain Nethergat!
The captain looked, then, realising what it was
at which he looked, turned livid. At length
speech came and the bully started up, blustrous.
“You infernal scoundrel, how came you there?”
“ By Sergeant-Major Dillon’s written order.”
“ You lie, you ruffian ! Give it to me ! ”
Evans laughed unpleasantly. “ Let me make
things plain. I have Dillon’s written order, given '
the night before his death. I saw and photo-
graphed the bags of rupees on the table. I saw
you steal the rupees and two letters —Oh, don’t
threaten me ! I have you fixed, Captain Nether-
gat. I picked up, after you left, something which
escaped you —a letter containing a statement of
moneys in the bags, of Dillon’s frequent loans to
you, and your receipts. (Keep your hands down
or I’ll kill you). Those bags and the accounts
which were in the envelopes are in your big safe
there.”
The captain looked at the safe.
“ You needn’t look there,” said the other ; “ I
squared your khitmagar —who hates you and has
left you —to steal the keys of your safe. I have
them.”
Nethergat’s face i witched horribly ; his furtive,
evil eyes glanced from side to side. Then, like
lightning, he whipped open a drawer of his desk
and groped wildly therein.
The private sneered. “No use shooting. All
the proofs are in good hands. I’m not a new-
chum. Besides, your revolver isn’t there. Your
khitmagar hid that. There’s something in the
drawer will serve your turn better —prussic acid.
Thank your khitmagar again. ”
The two men glared at one another. Then, for
the first time, the private lost self-control and
burst out: “ Who are you, thief, seducer, coward,
and liar, that you should die as good soldiers die?
Poison yourself like the rat you are ! ”
At this the pitiful craven grovelled—” Good
God! Evans, you won t ruin me? Ive been
narsn ; it was all tor your good—a young soldier,
you know ! Swear you won’t split. Of course
you won t. 111 give you every rupee in the bags
—troop money and Dillons money, too, though
1 m internally pushed. I 1 buy you out-do any-
f n £ lt > man > t hlnk of my people ! Think
disgrace to my friends !
, friends ! Did you think of the disgrace
to Dillon? Did you—did you think of the dis-
grace to Cis Neame, whom you ruined and left,
you cursed pariah? Did you think of the dis-
grace to me, that worshipped every hair of her
head? Die, you dog ! die by poison, and thank
me for letting you ! I’ve followed you two years
on the off-chance of killing you myself.”
“ Cis—Neame ! My God ! Cis— ! Then you
ar ® ~— ”
The man that hates you most on earth and
would hunt you over the rampants of hell— her
sweetheart—Sam Levin ! ”
* * » *
The next day Captain Nethergat, 24th
Hussars, occupied a suicide’s grave, and no shadow
or stain remained on Dillon’s fame, But it was
not good that a man knowing what “Skew”
Evans knew should remain in the regiment. He
was permitted to purchase his discharge. Last
tune 1 met him he was a hatter ’on the western
ranges of Maoruand, and we talked over this true
story.
Sydney.
J. EVISON.
The Bulletin has received these truly
touching verses and publishes them as a text for
some parson who may feel inclined to hold forth
on the pessimism and irreverence of the Australian
backblocks youth :
ONE LAST APPEAL.
O, Lord of mercy, speak me fair, my last frail hope is thee.
What is the use, the earthly use, the blanky use of ME ?
Is there all round this w ide, wide world a hope for such
as I?
O, Lord of mercy, tell me straight, and let me live or die.
Was I just born to hang around, and mope and cadge and
bum ?
Or just for folks to frighten kids at what they might
become ?
Am I to blame for what I am, or is it simply luck ?
Or fell I from Thy pottery a hunk of useless muck ?
For I’m well-grown and strong and stout, my life throbs
loud within ;
I am not steeped, I’ll swear I’m not, in what the world
calls sin ;
I’ve never harmed a single soul, they can’t say that of me ;
Yet here I am—a stepping-stone for all humanity !
Shall ever I possess in life a pound to call my own ?
(I might as well express a wish to own the Torrid Zone).
You know, O Lord, I’ve tried my best to earn the world’s
bright gold,
Yet here I am, a failure—Ugh !—and twenty-three years
old.
Now I’ve a credit balance of, say, fifty years to run ;
I’ll write you out a cheque in full for every blessed one ;
And if you tell me that in life there is no use for me,
’Tis best to cash it right away, as far as I can see.
But if the world holds prospects fair—if I’m to share its
bliss ;
If fame awaits, or Glory bright, or Love’s delirious kiss;
If I’m to bask, a petted child, ’neath Fortune’s nod and
beck,
I pray that in your mercy, Lord, you’ll not present the
cheque 1
Our Secret.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We have a little secret, love,
A way to say “ good night ”
When night
Is anything but good or right.
I in my chamber, just above
Your chamber roof—one- two—
Throw down iriy boots, and so do you,
And thus we say good-night
When night
Is anything but good or right.
Venice.

“The trysting hour —I see her not!
Ah, well, I shan’t demur—

4.5 p.m.

“ My Dear Mr. BONNINGTON. Theatre Royal, Sydney, April 20, 1896.
Allow me, on behalf of Mrs. Holt, to thank you very sincerely for your
kind thought in sending her, on Saturday, a bottle of your famed Irish Moss. I may say that
it is an old and valued friend of ours, and I am sure our esteem of its virtues will be
strengthened by the acquaintance with it which we now renew, through your good agency
“ With all sincere wishes for your success and well-being,
“1 remain, yours cordially, BLAND HOLT.”
